

2016 WL 4120559 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO RONALDI JUAREZ-MUNOZ
File: AXXX XX2 138 - Buffalo, NY
July 13, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose E. Perez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy A. Merriman
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent timely appeals the Immigration Judge’s June 4, 2015, decision denying his motion to reopen and reconsider. The respondent has filed a motion to accept his late filed brief, which we grant. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief in opposition. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review issues of law, discretion, or judgment de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the motion to reopen and reconsider were untimely filed (IJ at 3). See sections 240(c)(6) and (7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6) and (7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1). Further, the respondent has not demonstrated that any exception to the time limitation on reopening applies. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4). On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in declining to exercise his discretionary sua sponte authority to reopen and/or reconsider his final order of removal. We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the motions.
 
We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of reopening for the reasons set forth in his decision denying reopening (IJ at 3-5). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent requested sua sponte reopening so that DHS may exercise its prosecutorial discretion (Motion to Reopen Sua Sponte filed 4/8/15). The DHS has not indicated that it will exercise its prosecutorial discretion in this case, and neither the Immigration Judge nor the Board have the authority to order DHS to do so. Further, the relief that the respondent seeks from DHS may not be available. See United States v. Texas, -- S.Ct.--, 2016 WL 3434401 (June 23, 2016), aff’g, 809 F.3d 134 (5th Cir. 2015). Since the respondent did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances for reopening proceedings, the Immigration Judge did not err in declining to exercise his discretionary sua sponte authority to reopen.
 
We also find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of reconsideration as moot (IJ Dec. dated 6/4/15 at 5). During proceedings before the Immigration Judge, the respondent, through counsel, admitted that he was not a citizen of the United States, that he was a citizen of Mexico, that he entered the United States illegally, and that he was removable as charged (Exh. 1; IJ Dec. dated 6/4/15 at 2, 4; IJ Order dated 10/20/14). His concession of removability was a condition precedent for the only relief he sought See section 240B(a)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a)(1); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i). Based on the respondent’s concessions, inter alia, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent the right to voluntarily depart within 120 days of the issuance of his order. Since the respondent did not voluntarily depart, the Immigration Judge’s order converted to an order of removal effective as of October 20, 2014.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent does not dispute the Immigration Judge’s finding as to alienage. Rather, the respondent argues, as he did in his motion to reconsider, that his Fourth Amendment rights were violated during a stop, which subsequently brought him to the attention of DHS and caused him to be placed in proceedings (Motion to Reconsider filed 3/19/15).1 The respondent contends, as he did before the Immigration Judge, that equitable tolling applies because he pursued his rights diligently and some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 8-11). The respondent argues that he was not aware of the constitutional violation of his rights until he spoke to his new lawyer.
 
Evidence obtained as a result of a Fourth Amendment violation is suppressible in the course of a civil deportation proceeding only if the violation is egregious or undermines the reliability of the evidence in dispute. See Almeida-Amoral v. Gonzales, 461 F.3d 231, 235 (2d Cir. 2006); see also INS v. Lopez-Mendoza, 468 U.S. 1032, 1050-51 (1984). An individual in removal proceedings who raises a question about the admissibility of evidence must come forward with proof establishing a prima facie case before the Government will be called on to assume the burden of justifying the manner in which it obtained the evidence. Cotzojay v. Holder, 725 F.3d 172, 178 (2d Cir. 2013).
 
Here, the respondent did not submit a signed, dated affidavit in support of his assertions (IJ Dec. dated 10/20/14 at 4 n. 1). See Motion to Reconsider Sua Sponte at pp. 23-24 (26-27). Moreover, it is well settled that arguments of counsel are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). As such, the respondent did not meet his burden of proving a prima facie case. Cotzojay v. Holder, supra.
 
Even assuming, purely for the sake of argument, that the information was obtained through an egregious constitutional violation, the respondent’s alienage was established through means independent of any evidence of the stop he wished to suppress. See Vanegas-Ramirez v. Holder, 768 F.3d 226, 236 (2d Cir. 2014) (an alien’s concessions of removability are not fruit of the illegality, but an intervening act of free will); Pretzantzin v. Holder, 736 F.3d 641, 651 (2d Cir. 2013) (evidence of alienage is admissible if obtained through means independent of or sufficiently attenuated from the primary illegality).
 
The Immigration Judge found, and the respondent does not dispute on appeal, that the respondent admitted during his removal proceeding that he was not a citizen or national of the United States and was a native and citizen of Mexico (IJ Dec. dated 6/4/15 at 2, 4). The respondent conceded alienage about two years after the date of the alleged violations, with the assistance of counsel.2 As a result, the concession of alienage was sufficiently independent of any violation that may have previously occurred. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge correctly denied the motion to reconsider sua sponte as moot and the appeal shall be dismissed.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s motion to suppress was attached as Exhibit B to the Motion to Reconsider Sua Sponte.

2

The respondent does not allege that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel. Nor has the respondent complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).

2016 WL 4120567 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YONG TEAK LEE A.K.A. YONG LEE
File: AXXX XX6 569 - Atlanta, Georgia
July 12, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hiba Ghalib, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of South Korea, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 24, 2016, denying his motion to reopen the removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, and the likelihood of future events, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that the respondent and his attorney appeared at a continued Master Calendar hearing on November 5, 2015. Based on the respondent’s admissions and concession of removability (Exh. 2) entered at the May 15, 2015, hearing, the Immigration Judge entered an order granting the respondent’s request for pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), with an alternate order of removal if he failed to depart on or before March 4, 2016. However, the respondent failed to voluntarily depart by the March 4, 2016, deadline. Instead, on March 4, 2016, he filed a motion alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, and seeking reopening in order to apply for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, 8 C.F.R. § 1255, based on his recent marriage to a United States citizen who had filed an immediate relative immigrant visa petition (Form I-130) on his behalf.
 
The record includes a document (Exh. 3) explaining the conditions attendant to the grant of the request for pre-conclusion voluntary departure, including the waiver of appeal, signed by both the respondent and his attorney on November 5, 2015, indicating their understanding and acceptance of the terms of the agreement. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i). The Immigration Judge’s November 5, 2015, decision became an administratively final order on that date when the respondent agreed to the terms of the grant of pre-conclusion voluntary departure, waived appeal and accepted that decision as final. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.14 and 1003.39.
 
*2 With certain limited exceptions, an alien is permitted to file one motion to reopen a final administrative order of removal, not later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent filed his motion to reopen on March 4, 2016, more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s administratively final order entered on November 5, 2015. Thus, the Immigration Judge properly denied the motion as time-barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
Moreover, to the extent that respondent is arguing that his legal representation before the Immigration Court was deficient, we agree with the Immigration Judge (3-4) that the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements for raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); accord Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222 (11th Cir. 2003). Notwithstanding, even if we were to find the respondent had complied with the Lozada requirements, in order to establish ineffective assistance of prior counsel, the respondent must establish that his former counsel’s performance was so deficient that it impinged the “fundamental fairness” of the hearing and he suffered prejudice thereby. See Ali v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 643 F.3d 1324, 1329 (11th Cir. 2011) (alien must establish prejudice in order to prevail on ineffective assistance claim), citing Mejia Rodriguez v. Reno, 178 F.3d 1139, 1146 (11th Cir.1999); see also Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273-74 (11th Cir.2005) (“Prejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different.”); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (establishing ineffective assistance of counsel requires an alien to demonstrate prejudice from counsel’s conduct, such that it compromised the fundamental fairness of the hearing). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, supra at 561. Here, no such prejudice has been shown.
 
Although the respondent now claims prejudice in that he would not have agreed to the grant of voluntary departure, if he had known he had other options, he does not identify in his motion what were those other options. Rather, the record reflects respondent’s former counsel elected to pursue the only apparent form of relief from removal then available to the respondent, pre-conclusion voluntary departure. An attorney’s tactical decisions in the preparation and presentation of an alien’s case, including accepting an extended period of voluntary departure, do not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel. See, e.g., Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 934 (9th Cir. 1986). We find no violation of due process or a denial of a fair hearing, as the respondent, represented by counsel, had ample opportunity to meet with him; and prepare for and present his case before the Immigration Court.
 
*3 Further, while an Immigration Judge retains limited discretionary authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte in “exceptional situations,” see Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997), we agree with the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 4-5) that the respondent’s motion presented no such situation.
 
Therefore, in light of the foregoing, we find that the Immigration Judge was correct in denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. Finally, in view of our disposition, the respondent’s request for a stay of removal will be denied. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion for a stay of removal is denied.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4120567 (BIA)

2016 WL 4120563 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEONARDO DAVID LUNA-VINAMAO
File: AXXX XX6 613 - El Paso, TX
July 12, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jason A. Nielson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William M. Hunt
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent’s motion is untimely filed and is denied on that basis. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The motion has not been shown to satisfy any exception to the time limitations imposed by law on motions to reopen removal proceedings. Further, untimely motions to reopen to apply for adjustment do not fall within any statutory or regulatory exception to the time limits on motions and generally will be denied. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009).
 
Moreover, we decline to equitably toll the applicable time limits based on the respondent’s asserted ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for advancing such a claim, or the requirement that he show prejudice resulting from former counsel’s alleged deficient performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n.2 (5th Cir. 2001). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process). Further, the respondent does not dispute that at the very least, he was aware of the Board’s February 7, 2012, decision approximately 3 months after the decision was issued. Other than hire current counsel, for which the respondent provides no dates or details, the respondent has not offered a persuasive explanation for waiting more than 4 years to file the present motion.
 
On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the untimeliness of the present motion. Nor has he demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen his proceedings under our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.
 
*2 Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 4120581 (BIA)

IN RE: JONATHAN POSSO CARMEN
File: AXXX XX9 746 - Charlotte, NC
July 8, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph A. Baker, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hilary Rainone
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia, appeals from the May 19, 2015, decision of the Immigration Judge denying a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including those on credibility, for clear error, and review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues on appeal on a de novo basis. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
The respondent was granted multiple continuances to await a decision on his application for deferred status pursuant to Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (DACA) which was ultimately denied. At his hearing before the Immigration Judge in May 2015, the respondent requested a further continuance to file a motion to vacate a criminal conviction. However, the pursuit of post-conviction relief does not constitute good cause to delay proceedings since the outcome of such an endeavor would be speculative, and a conviction is considered final for immigration purposes unless and until it is overturned. See Matter of Cuellar, 25 I&N Dec. 850, 855 (BIA 2012); Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987) (the decision to grant or deny a continuance for good cause is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal unless it is shown that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6. Therefore, we find no error in the decision of the Immigration Judge to deny the respondent’s motion for a further continuance. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in his removal proceeding, we find that he has failed to substantially comply with the procedural requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). By the respondent’s own admission, he has taken only initial steps to file a formal complaint with the state bar where his prior counsel is licensed (Brief at 4). Furthermore, former counsel’s alleged failure to file a motion in state court regarding the respondent’s criminal conviction is not relevant to these removal proceedings. We thus find that he has not established that prior counsel’s representation resulted in the prejudice necessary to prevail on such a claim. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Accordingly, the motion to remand will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4120581 (BIA)

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on January 15, 2016, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel and seeking reopening to reapply for cancellation of removal. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. Considering the totality of the circumstances presented, including the evidence submitted, we will grant the motion and remand the proceedings to provide the respondent a further opportunity to pursue relief. We do not express an opinion on the merits of the respondent’s entitlement to relief. On remand, the Immigration Judge may receive any additional evidence he deems appropriate to the full resolution of this matter. Accordingly, the motion will be granted.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings not inconsistent with this order.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark

2016 WL 4035749 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FALGUNIBEN PATEL
File: AXXX XX4 914 - Seattle, WA
June 30, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret W. Wong, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hana A. Sato
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India who was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on July 14, 2005, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge dated July 1, 2015, denying her motion to reopen. The respondent has filed an appeal brief, as well as a supplemental brief with additional evidence and argument, which we have construed as a motion to remand. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has expressed opposition to the appeal and the motion. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and her motion will be denied.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
An in absentia order of removal may be rescinded only where (1) the alien files a motion to reopen within 180 days of the date of the order of removal and demonstrates that the failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1), or (2) at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) and (2) of the Act, or was in Federal or State custody and the failure to appear was through no fault of the respondent. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i)-(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 722-23 (BIA 1999).
 
Proper notification under section 239(a)(1) of the Act includes informing an alien of the time and place for his or her scheduled appearance before an Immigration Judge. See section 239(a)(1)(G)(i) of the Act. Where a hearing notice is properly addressed and sent by regular mail according to normal office procedures, a presumption of delivery attaches, although it is weaker than the presumption that applies to documents sent by certified mail. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 671-73 (BIA 2008). Additionally, a hearing notice is properly served on an alien if served on the alien’s counsel of record. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; see also Garcia v. INS, 222 F.3d 1208, 1209 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
In her motion, which she filed on March 13, 2015, the respondent sought reopening of her proceedings on the basis of lack of notice. Specifically, the respondent alleged that she received no notice of her July 14, 2005, hearing, which demonstrated inadequate representation by her former counsel preventing her from having an opportunity to present her case. The respondent also alleged in her motion that the consequences for failing to appear at her hearing were not adequately represented to her at any time after her proceedings were initiated because the warnings in the Notice to Appear were given in a language that she barely spoke (Hindi) and she did not speak, write, or read English in 2005.1 She further alleged that her former attorney’s assertion (per the tape recordings of her in absentia hearing) that he informed her of her hearing date was not true as she never heard from her attorney after she last saw him at her bond hearing. According to the respondent, she first learned of her removal order in March or April 2013 after she hired an attorney to help her look into her immigration status and she never saw a copy of the in absentia removal order until her current attorney, with whom she started speaking in July 2014, showed it to her (Respondent’s Motion at 1-9 and Tab B, Affidavit).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge failed to consider whether she overcame the presumption of receipt under Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008), and instead simply concluded that she received proper notice because the Notice of Hearing (“NOH”) was sent to her attorney, without addressing her allegations regarding lack of notice (Respondent’s Brief at 4-7). The respondent also argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge completely ignored her contentions that her former counsel’s actions were shockingly inadequate and his performance so prejudicial as to warrant [a finding of] ineffective assistance. She asserts that she has a lack of understanding of the terms of her agreement with her former counsel and that she never filed a complaint against him but argues that, because she never signed an agreement with her former counsel, she should not be held to the same strict compliance [requirements] of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), as an individual who personally hired and signed an agreement with her attorney (Id. at 7-9).
 
The respondent also challenges on appeal the Immigration Judge’s finding that, by filing her motion almost 10 years after she was ordered removed in absentia, she did not demonstrate due diligence. According to the respondent, the delay was not unreasonable as she diligently pursued relief “since she discovered the circumstances of her claim” (Id. at 9-11). Finally, the respondent argues that sua sponte reopening of her proceedings is warranted because she intends to seek a provisional waiver, followed by a request for lawful permanent residence through consular processing, based on an approved visa petition; she is the primary caretaker of her United States citizen husband, who lost his right eye in 2000 and has had four eye surgeries in 14 years, as well as of their two United States citizen children; and, as a person of good moral character with no criminal record, is not a priority for removal under the DHS’s current guidelines (Id. at 11-13).
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s appellate arguments and we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision for the reasons stated in the decision (I.J. at 1-5). The respondent has not alleged that the NOH was not delivered as mailed and there are no indications on this record that that notice did not reach the respondent’s former attorney. Thus, as there is no question that the presumption of delivery attaches (section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act), the respondent’s argument that she did not receive notice of her hearing does not support a claim for reopening (Id. at 2-3).
 
Additionally, the respondent did not file her motion within 180 days of her removal order nor establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel so as to obtain equitable tolling of the filing deadline (Id. at 3-4). We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). Precedent from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this case arises, provides that while the requirements of Lozada are generally reasonable, they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means (I.J. at 4). See Correa-Rivera v. Holder, 706 F.3d 1128, 1131 (9th Cir. 2013) (quoting Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002)); see also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (“Lozada is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one”).
 
*3 We agree with the Immigration Judge that the general statements proffered by the respondent in her motion do not provide a clear indication of ineffective assistance of counsel and that the respondent has not complied with any of the Lozada requirements. First, the respondent’s affidavit does not detail the agreement that was entered into with her former counsel. Instead, it offers a vague reference of the nature of the respondent’s agreement with her attorney. Second, the respondent has conceded that she has not informed her former counsel of the allegations she has made against him, which has prevented that counsel from having an opportunity to respond to those allegations. Third, the respondent has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority against her former counsel or explained why such a complaint has not been filed (Id. at 3).
 
We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the evidence in this case shows a lack of due diligence by the respondent. The respondent could have discovered her former counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness when she hired her second attorney in March or April 2013 or, at the latest, in July 2014, when she spoke with her current attorney about the documents she has obtained pursuant to a FOIA request, yet she filed her motion in May 2015 (Id. at 4).
 
We find that, under the circumstances of this case, the respondent has not established a basis to rescind the Immigration Judge’s in absentia removal order and reopen her proceedings.
 
We also find that the respondent has not established that sua sponte reopening of these proceedings is warranted. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We do not find that the information provided by the respondent in this regard establishes that her case presents a truly exceptional situation warranting reopening.
 
In her motion, the respondent asks that we consider one of our unpublished decisions, dated June 25, 2015, in which we issued a remand after concluding that administrative closure to allow the alien to pursue an I-601 provisional waiver application may constitute relief for purposes of reopening. We will deny the motion for the reasons stated by the DHS in its brief opposing the respondent’s motion. See DHS’s brief titled “Department’s Opposition to the Respondent’s ‘Supplement to Respondent’s Brief in Support of Appeal.”DD’ First, the respondent’s submission, which was received after the September 17, 2015, deadline we set when we granted a briefing extension request on August 27, 2015, does not comply with the Board Practice Manual’s requirement that a brief filed outside the period granted in a briefing schedule be accompanied by a Motion to Accept Supplemental Brief that complies generally with the rules for motions, including service on the opposing party. See Board Practice Manual, section 4.7(g). Second, the respondent is asking the Board to rely on an unpublished decision, which has no precedential value. Third, even if we were to consider the non-precedential decision submitted with the motion, the circumstances in that case are very different from those in the instant case, particularly in that the motion in that case was timely filed. Thus, the respondent in the instant case is not similarly situated as the alien in our unpublished decision that he now seeks to have admitted into the record.
 
*4 Given the foregoing, we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen and will deny the respondent’s motion to remand. Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

According to the respondent, her best language is Gujarati and she speaks very little, and is not fluent, in Hindi (Respondent’s Motion, Tab B, Affidavit, item no. 6).

2016 WL 4035748 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AMITKUMAR BHIKHUBHAI PATEL
File: AXXX XX8 193 - Memphis, TN
June 30, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret W. Wong, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of June 19, 2015. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s June 10, 2015, motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on June 3, 2010. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Notice of Hearing (NOH) for the June 3, 2010, hearing was mailed to the respondent’s former counsel, Barry Frager, Esquire, on December 16, 2009 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 3). The respondent failed to appear at his hearing and an in absentia order was entered on June 3, 2010. The respondent filed a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings through present counsel on June 10, 2015, alleging that he did not receive notice of his hearing. The motion further alleged that prior counsel failed to inform the respondent of his hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding reopening not warranted based upon lack of notice because notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent (I.J. at 2-3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
While the respondent alleges his counsel did not notify the respondent of his hearing date, the Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent has not shown ineffective assistance of counsel insofar as he has not complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988)(I.J. at 2-3). See Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 857, 863 (6th Cir. 2006). We find without merit the respondent’s appellate contention that strict adherence to the Lozada requirements is not necessary (Respondent’s Brief, at 8), where the respondent has not explained his failure to fulfill any of the Lozada requirements. See Ibarra-Reina v. Lynch, __ F.3d __ No. 15-3395, 2016 WL 3212266, at 4 (6th Cir. June 7, 2016) (holding that although Lozada is not enforced rigidly, neither have requirements been as loosely applied as the alien suggests).1
 
*2 In Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998), the Board held that an alien may reopen for purposes of pursuing previously unavailable relief in circumstances where she was not given oral warnings in her native language of the consequences of failing to appear at a deportation hearing and where the motion complies with the general time limits governing motions to reopen. The motion is untimely, however, and thus reopening on this basis is not warranted.
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that no exceptional circumstances have been presented to warrant sua sponte reopening (I.J. at 11). Pursuant to our holding in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), the power to reopen sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). While the respondent has presented some positive factors and alleges hardship to his U.S. citizen spouse and children, these factors either individually or in the aggregate are not so exceptional as to trigger the limited authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte. Indeed, many aliens who have been present in the United States for a period of years have accumulated favorable equities subsequent to being ordered removed. See Matter of Bedford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000) (burden is on the movant to show exceptional circumstances).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We find meritless the respondent’s argument on appeal that the Immigration Judge failed to consider Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 675 (BIA 2008) (Respondent’s Brief, at 7). That decision is inapposite because the facts are distinguishable from the case here.

2016 WL 4035751 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HERBERT ORLANDO HERRERA-VENTURA
File: AXXX XX4 551 - Los Angeles, CA
June 30, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Berc Agopoglu, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated March 15, 2016,1 denying his motion to reopen. We will dismiss the appeal.2
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear on March 7, 2006 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). On January 25, 2007, the Court mailed written notice of a rescheduled hearing, both to the respondent, at his address of record, and to his former attorney (I.J. at 2; Exh. 2). As the respondent did not appear at the scheduled June 11, 2007, hearing, the Immigration Judge proceeded in absentia and ultimately ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States (I.J. at 2). The respondent filed his motion to reopen on February 29, 2016 (I.J. at 2).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion did not contain sufficient evidence to establish that reopening was warranted on account of lack of notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). In particular, the respondent has not submitted sufficient evidence to overcome the presumption that the United States Postal Service effectively delivered the Notices of Hearing (NOH) to himself and his attorney. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008) (holding that there is a slight presumption of delivery where notice is sent by regular mail and setting forth factors which should be considered when evaluating a claim that a NOH was not received); see also Sembiring v. Gonzales, 499 F.3d 981 (9th Cir. 2007); Salta v. INS, 314 F.3d 1076 (9th Cir. 2002). In support of reopening, the respondent did not present an affidavit from either himself or his former attorney disavowing receipt of the NOH. Even assuming for purposes of analysis that the assertions in the motion to reopen could substitute for an affidavit from the respondent, neither the motion nor the respondent’s appeal brief meaningfully contends that the respondent’s former attorney never received the NOH that the record indicates was sent to him. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (providing that when a person is represented, any required notice shall be given to his counsel of record); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that service on an alien’s counsel of record constitutes notice to the alien). Nor has the respondent contended in the alternative that his former attorney received the NOH but failed to inform the respondent of the hearing date, or that his former attorney in any other way provided deficient representation that potentially could give rise to a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Considering these circumstances, we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly declined to reopen these proceedings on the basis of lack of notice (I.J. at 2-3).
 
*2 The respondent’s motion was otherwise untimely. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i), (c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1), (4)(ii). The respondent has not meaningfully articulated changed conditions in El Salvador which would permit him to file an otherwise untimely motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i).
 
To the extent that the respondent seeks sua sponte reopening of these proceedings, such measures have not been shown to be warranted. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We note in particular that over 8½ years elapsed between the entry of the in absentia order in the (duly noticed) proceedings and the filing of the respondent’s motion to reopen, which evinces a lack of due diligence in the pursuit of his claims.3 Accordingly, we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
We acknowledge the respondent’s counsel’s appellate claim that the Immigration Judge erred in ruling on the respondent’s motion to reopen while (allegedly) not in possession of the record of proceedings (Respondent’s Brief at 4). However, upon our review, we conclude that the respondent has not established that the manner in which the Immigration Judge adjudicated his motion to reopen was insufficient or defective in any regard. The respondent’s counsel’s speculative belief that because the respondent’s file was not available for him to review at the Los Angeles Immigration Court on January 30, 2016, then the Immigration Judge must not have been in possession of the record of proceedings on March 15, 2016, when she denied the motion, is unsupported by objective evidence.4 Thus, we find no grounds upon which to conclude that the respondent was prejudiced or that he suffered any due process violation such that remanded proceedings would be warranted for further consideration of his motion to reopen.
 
For the reasons set forth above, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD

3

In an order dated August 20, 2007, we concluded that we were precluded from adjudicating the merits of the 2007 appeal in view of the requirement under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act that a motion to reopen be filed with the Immigration Judge.

It is unclear from the respondent’s counsel’s affidavit whether the immigration officer was referring to the Court’s record of proceedings, as counsel appears to suggest, or the Department of Homeland Security’s internal file or “A file” (Respondent’s Brief, Exh. A).

2016 WL 4035757 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KARIM NALI
File: AXXX XX6 772 - New York, NY
June 29, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murad Khan Ahmed, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Morocco, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s November 25, 2015, decision denying his October 22, 2015, motion to reopen removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility determinations, for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other questions de novo, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent entered the United States on September 19, 1998, as a nonimmigrant visitor, and remained longer than authorized (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). Thereafter, he was placed in removal proceedings, and on June 3, 2004, he was granted the privilege of voluntarily departing the United States pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), on or before September 30, 2004 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). The respondent did not depart (I.J. at 2). On October 22, 2015, he filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. It was denied.
 
On appeal, the respondent does not assert that his motion is timely (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent therefore must establish that he qualifies for an exception to the generally applicable motions deadline.
 
The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request to equitably toll the motions deadline based on his former attorney’s ineffective assistance. Namely, the respondent claims that his former attorney did not timely file the visa petition through which he now seeks adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i) (Respondent’s Brief at 2-3, 7). He also contends that sua sponte reopening is appropriate, as his motion is supported by exceptional circumstances (Respondent’s Brief at 5-8).1
 
In order to equitably toll the motions filing deadline based on ineffective assistance, an alien must, among other things, demonstrate that he exercised due diligence before and after he discovered or should have discovered the ineffective assistance. Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008).
 
The respondent, however, has not established that he exercised due diligence in pursuing reopening (I.J. at 3). The respondent should have discovered his former attorney’s ineffective assistance in August 2002, when the visa petition filed by his sister on his behalf was approved (I.J. at 3; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab H).2 Notwithstanding the foregoing, he did not file his motion to reopen until October 22, 2015 (I.J. at 3).
 
*2 To the extent the respondent suggests that he was unable to exercise due diligence because he experienced depression between 2004 and 2011 (I.J. at 3; Respondent’s Brief at 4, 8; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tabs M, N), the respondent’s explanation does not account for the periods between 2002 and 2004, or between 2012 and 2015 (I.J. at 3). As such, there is an insufficient basis to conclude that he was unable to exercise due diligence during the entire period he seeks to toll (I.J. at 3).
 
Even if the respondent had exercised due diligence, we have no authority to toll the April 30, 2001, deadline imposed by section 245(i) of the Act. The statute contains a fixed cut-off date for eligibility, and it is independent of any variable. We therefore cannot toll the statutory deadline for eligibility to pursue adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See generally Balam-Chuc v. Mukasey, 547 F.3d 1044, 1047-50 (9th Cir. 2008) (affirming the Board’s finding that the deadline imposed under section 245(i) of the Act operates as a statute of repose not subject to equitable tolling).3
 
Finally, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent’s case does not warrant sua sponte reopening (I.J. at 3-4). Sua sponte reopening is invoked sparingly and reserved for truly exceptional circumstances. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-35 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). The respondent has not established that his circumstances are so exceptional as to trigger the limited authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte (I.J. at 3-4). Further, he has not demonstrated that he is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status (I.J. at 3-4). See sections 245(c)(2), (7) of the Act; section 245(i) of the Act. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Insofar as the respondent requests a favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion, he must address his request directly to the Department of Homeland Security (Respondent’s Brief at 8).

2

Notably, the visa petition approval notice indicates that the petition was accorded a priority date of June 22, 2001 (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab H).

3

The respondent also claims that his former attorney’s conduct was ineffective because he advised the respondent not to depart the United States pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s grant of voluntary departure (Respondent’s Brief at 3). Nevertheless, the respondent’s failure to depart no longer acts as a bar to eligibility for discretionary relief because 10 years have passed since the entry of the voluntary departure order (Respondent’s Brief at 6). See section 240B(d)(1) of the Act. He thus has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by advice his former attorney allegedly provided relating to voluntary departure. See Rabiu v. INS, 41 F.3d 879, 882-83 (2d Cir. 1994).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ANGEL GONZALEZ
File: AXXX XX3 203 - Orlando, FL
June 27, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Stoller, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alexandra Rivas
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. On August 18, 2014, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On April 14, 2016, he filed an untimely motion to reopen. The respondent alleges that the time limitation should be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent contends, inter alia, that his former counsel should have disputed the charges of removability more vigorously and that he should not have been treated as an arriving alien. The respondent further argues that due to intervening precedent, his conviction is no longer an aggravated felony and he is eligible for cancellation of removal.
 
The motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel is denied as the respondent has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Although he attached a copy of a complaint to bar counsel, the motion does not include any evidence that it was actually filed (Motion, Exh. E). Further, the respondent has not demonstrated due diligence. See Avila-Santoyo v. Att’y Gen., 713 F.3d 1357, 1363 n. 5 (11th Cir. 2013) (en banc). The respondent has been aware of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, since our August 18, 2014, decision, but he still has not demonstrated compliance.
 
We will, however, grant the motion to reopen under our discretionary sua sponte power due to intervening precedent. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Donawa v. Att’y Gen., 735 F.3d 1275 (11th Cir. 2013)(conviction under section 893.13(1)(a) of Florida law does not qualify as an aggravated felony under the “drug trafficking crime” clause of section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act). Accordingly, we will remand this proceeding for further consideration of whether the respondent is eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark

26 I. & N. Dec. 765 (BIA), Interim Decision 3865, 2016 WL 3538417
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
MATTER OF ANTONIO GOMEZ-BELTRAN, RESPONDENT
Decided June 27, 2016
**1 *765 An alien cannot establish good moral character under section 101(f)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(f)(6) (2012), if, during the period for which it is required, he or she gives false testimony under oath in proceedings before an Immigration Judge with the subjective intent of obtaining immigration benefits.
 
FOR RESPONDENT:
Marina N. Alexandrovich, Esquire
Tempe, Arizona
FOR THE DEPARTMENT OF HOMELAND SECURITY:
Brent Landis
Senior Attorney
BEFORE: Board Panel: MALPHRUS and CREPPY, Board Members; GELLER, Temporary Board Member.
MALPHRUS, Board Member:
In a decision dated October 3, 2012, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable on his own admissions under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i) (2012), as an alien who is present in the United States without being admitted or paroled, and denied his applications for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1) (2012), and voluntary departure. The respondent has appealed from that decision and has filed a motion to reopen and remand. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes both the respondent’s appeal and his motion. The appeal will be dismissed, and the motion will be denied.1
  
I. FACTUAL AND PROCEDURAL HISTORY
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico who last entered the United States on January 1, 2007. On his application for cancellation of removal, the respondent claimed that he had only one conviction, which *766 was on January 9, 2009, for driving under the influence. However, when he testified before the Immigration Judge, the respondent at first stated that he had never been convicted of any criminal offense. He only acknowledged the conviction after being reminded by counsel that it was listed on his application. The respondent’s attorney then asked if he had “any other arrests or convictions anywhere in the world,” to which the respondent clearly answered, “No, sir.”
 
On cross-examination, the respondent initially reaffirmed his claim that he had no other arrests or convictions. After he repeatedly denied any further criminal activity, the DHS confronted him with a series of specific dates and offenses that the respondent had not disclosed on his application.2 Even after the DHS questioned the respondent about the first of these offenses, he did not correct his prior misrepresentation or voluntarily reveal the extent of his criminal history, waiting instead until the DHS asked him about each incident. The respondent eventually admitted five additional arrests or convictions that were not included in his application and were not disclosed during the hearing until the DHS confronted him with each one.
 
**2 The Immigration Judge found that the respondent gave false testimony regarding his criminal history at the hearing and determined that he was not credible. Based on the respondent’s false testimony, the Immigration Judge concluded that he lacked good moral character under section 101(f)(6) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(f)(6) (2012), and was statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal and voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge also denied his applications for relief in the exercise of discretion.3
  
II. ANALYSIS
  
A. Good Moral Character
 
The respondent has the burden of establishing that he meets all applicable eligibility requirements for cancellation of removal and that he merits a grant of relief in the exercise of discretion. See section 240(c)(4) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4) (2012); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d) (2016). To qualify for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, the respondent must establish, among other things, that he has been a person of *767 good moral character for at least 10 years immediately preceding the date of his application.4 Section 240A(b)(1)(B) of the Act.
 
Section 101(f)(6) of the Act provides that no person can establish good moral character if, during the period for which it is required, he or she “has given false testimony for the purpose of obtaining any benefits” under the Act. The term “testimony” in section 101(f)(6) is limited to oral statements made under oath. Matter of L-D-E-, 8 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1959) (holding that false statements in an application do not constitute testimony). The Supreme Court has determined that the statutory bar applies to any oral statements made under oath by a person who has a subjective intent to obtain immigration benefits, regardless of whether the misrepresentation is material. Kungys v. United States, 485 U.S. 759, 780 (1988) (stating that section 101(f)(6) “denominates a person to be of bad moral character on account of having given false testimony if he has told even the most immaterial of lies with the subjective intent of obtaining immigration or naturalization benefits”).
 
Immigration court proceedings are adversarial in nature and are governed by formal rules of procedure, which include requiring the Immigration Judge to take witness testimony under oath at transcribed *768 hearings. Section 240(b)(1) of the Act; Jacinto v. INS, 208 F.3d 725, 732-33 (9th Cir. 2000); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.7(b), 1240.9 (2016). False statements made under oath during proceedings before an Immigration Judge may constitute false testimony within the meaning of section 101(f)(6) of the Act. See Phinpathya v. INS, 673 F.2d 1013, 1018-19 (9th Cir. 1981) (“Testimony means a statement made by a witness under oath for the purpose of establishing proof of a fact to a court or tribunal.”), rev’d on other grounds, 464 U.S. 183 (1984); Matter of Barcenas, 19 I&N Dec. 609, 612 (BIA 1988) (finding that an alien’s false statements at a deportation hearing rendered him ineligible for voluntary departure); cf. Ramos v. INS, 246 F.3d 1264, 1266 (9th Cir. 2001) (holding that false statements made in an asylum examination are “false testimony”); Bernal v. INS, 154 F.3d 1020, 1023 (9th Cir. 1998) (holding that false statements made under oath in a naturalization interview are “false testimony”).
 
**3 Truthful testimony and disclosures are critical to the effective operation of the immigration court system. In the asylum context, courts have noted that the process depends on the alien’s fundamental obligation to tell the truth. Martinez v. Holder, 557 F.3d 1059, 1065 (9th Cir. 2009) (stating that the “asylum process is ultimately an honor system” and that the major check on the system is not the “[Immigration Judge’s] scrutiny or the government’s fact checkers,” but is, instead, “the asylum seeker’s belief that he or she will be held to that oath”); Diallo v. Gonzales, 447 F.3d 1274, 1280 (10th Cir. 2006) (“Asylum seekers must be held accountable for the veracity of statements that they swear to under oath.”). The same principle applies to applications for cancellation of removal.
 
In this case, the respondent testified that he had only been convicted of a single offense in 2009, denied any further incidents, and then admitted five additional arrests or convictions when confronted with each one by the DHS during cross-examination. He claims that his testimony was not untruthful because he honestly answered questions regarding specific offenses and dates and that it only appeared inconsistent when he was asked “vague, open-ended questions.”5
 
We do not agree. The questions regarding criminal history on the cancellation of removal application and at the hearing were not vague; they were simple and direct. The respondent was asked about his criminal history several times during the hearing and was given ample opportunity to disclose all his arrests and convictions before he was confronted by the *769 DHS. There is no indication that he did not understand the questions.6 Cf. United States v. Hovsepian, 422 F.3d 883, 887-88 (9th Cir. 2005) (en banc) (finding no subjective intent to deceive under section 101(f)(6) of the Act where inaccuracies resulted from poor memory, mistake, or vague questioning).
 
Nor was the interpretation of the proceedings in any way faulty. The respondent made no objection regarding the interpreter at the hearing, and he has made no showing on appeal that the interpreter did not perform competently. See Matter of D-R-, 25 I&N Dec. 445, 461-62 (BIA 2011); Matter of Exilus, 18 I&N Dec. 276, 280-81 (BIA 1982). The respondent claims that the interpreter mistakenly interpreted his date of birth as May 5, instead of May 14, but there is no indication that this discrepancy had any impact on his case. Furthermore, we find no merit to the respondent’s suggestion that the DHS may have questioned him about another person’s criminal record as a result of the incorrect birth date, because the respondent admitted each arrest or conviction referred to by the DHS. The remaining interpretation errors the respondent has identified were minor and unrelated to his false testimony. See Matter of D-R-, 25 I&N Dec. at 461-62.
 
**4 Although the respondent eventually answered questions truthfully when confronted with his criminal records, this does not undermine the fact that he made false statements when initially asked about his arrests and convictions. He did not fully and voluntarily disclose his criminal history and only admitted each arrest or conviction when specifically confronted with it by the DHS. See Ramos, 246 F.3d at 1266 (stating that the petitioner’s admission to lying did not preclude a finding that she lacked good moral character because ““her later honesty . . . does not remove her from the ambit of the statute”). Therefore, the respondent did not voluntarily make a timely recantation of his false testimony. Valadez-Munoz v. Holder, 623 F.3d 1304, 1310 (9th Cir. 2010) (stating that “when a person supposedly recants only when confronted with evidence of his prevarication, the amelioration [for recantation] is not available”); Matter of Namio, 14 I&N Dec. 412, 414 (BIA 1973).
 
When asked why he did not fully disclose his criminal activities, the respondent stated only that he “did not know.” The Immigration Judge did not err in rejecting this response as unpersuasive. The respondent’s *770 explanation raises a strong inference that he made false statements with the subjective intent to prevent the Immigration Judge from considering his criminal record in determining whether he is statutorily eligible for cancellation of removal and warrants such relief as a matter of discretion. See Akwasi Agyei v. Holder, 729 F.3d 6, 16 (1st Cir. 2013) (holding that the petitioner’s false testimony about his sham marriage warranted a finding that he lacked good moral character because his unexplained “fear” of telling the truth raised the “strong inference” that he was “afraid” he would be denied adjustment of status, which “is indistinguishable from a subjective intent to obtain immigration benefits via fraud”).
 
In determining whether the evidence is sufficient to establish that an applicant gave false testimony within the meaning of section 101(f)(6) of the Act, the trier of fact must conduct a case-by-case assessment. See Hovsepian, 422 F.3d at 887 (“Whether a person has the subjective intent to deceive in order to obtain immigration benefits is a question of fact.”); see also Siewe v. Gonzales, 480 F.3d 160, 167 (2d Cir. 2007) (“Drawing inferences from direct and circumstantial evidence is a routine and necessary task of any factfinder. . . . In the immigration context, the [Immigration Judge] is the factfinder to whom this indispensable work is delegated.”); accord Matter of D-R-, 25 I&N Dec. at 454. While not every misrepresentation or omission will be sufficient to constitute false testimony under section 101(f)(6) of the Act, this is not a close case. See generally Kungys, 485 U.S. at 781 (stating that the good moral character bar does not apply to mere ““concealments”).
 
**5 We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent actively sought to mislead the court regarding the extent of his criminal history when he only admitted his 2009 conviction and denied any others in responding to clear questions that called for full disclosure. Consequently, the Immigration Judge did not clearly err in finding that the respondent made false statements under oath with a “subjective intent to deceive” for the purpose of influencing the Immigration Judge’s decision regarding his applications for relief from removal. Id. at 780-81. We therefore conclude that the Immigration Judge properly held that the respondent is precluded from establishing good moral character under section 101(f)(6) of the Act and that he is statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1)(B) of the Act and for voluntary departure.7
 
*771 Moreover, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent does not merit a favorable exercise of discretion on his applications for relief from removal. See section 240(c)(4)(A)(ii) of the Act. Based on our de novo review of the record, we conclude that the respondent’s equities, which include his long residence and family ties in the United States, are outweighed by his significant criminal history and his untruthfulness during the hearing. See Aragon-Salazar v. Holder, 769 F.3d 699, 706-07 (9th Cir. 2014) (stating that the Immigration Judge can consider an applicant’s lack of truthfulness during the hearing as part of the discretionary determination on an application for relief). Accordingly, we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s applications for cancellation of removal and voluntary departure, and we will dismiss his appeal.
  
B. Motion To Remand
 
The respondent’s motion was filed during the pendency of his appeal, so it is considered to be a motion to remand. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4) (2016); see also Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976, 978-80 (BIA 1999) (discussing differences between a motion to reopen and a motion to remand in the context of an appeal). We conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated that a remand is warranted based on his newly submitted evidence and allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992); see also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127, 138 (BIA 2009) (stating that the requirements for a motion to remand are essentially the same as those for a motion to reopen).
 
First, the respondent has not substantially complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 597-99 (9th Cir. 2004). Nor has he presented a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel,” as provided under Ninth Circuit law. Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (“Lozada is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one.”). However, even if there had been substantial compliance, the respondent has not shown that his first attorney’s performance was deficient. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310-11 (BIA 1998).
 
**6 Contrary to the respondent’s assertions, prior counsel was aware that the respondent is the beneficiary of an approved visa petition. But since his priority date was not current and no visa was immediately available to him, the respondent was not eligible to adjust his status. Furthermore, administrative closure would not have been warranted because of the *772 remote availability of a visa and the speculative nature of the respondent’s ability to adjust his status, particularly in light of his false testimony and criminal history. See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688, 696 (BIA 2012).
 
In addition, we reject the respondent’s assertion that his prior counsel was ineffective because he did not discover or disclose the respondent’s complete criminal history. The respondent admitted that he misrepresented his criminal history to his first attorney, who stated that the respondent had only informed him of the 2009 offense. The responsibility for the respondent’s false testimony, and its consequences, therefore rests on his own shoulders.
 
We find no merit to the other arguments the respondent raised in his motion regarding his ineffective assistance claim. Therefore, because the respondent has not established that prior counsel’s performance was deficient or that it adversely affected the outcome of the proceedings, we find no basis for remanding. See Lianhua Jiang v. Holder, 754 F.3d 733, 741 (9th Cir. 2014) (finding no denial of due process where the alleged violation did not affect the outcome of the proceedings); Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 857-58 (9th Cir. 2004) (per curiam) (stating that prejudice must be shown to prevail on a due process claim based on ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
Finally, although the respondent claims that he is now eligible for additional relief from removal, he has neither submitted an application nor established prima facie eligibility for any such relief. INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 323 (1992) (stating that a motion to reopen is properly denied when a prima facie case for the relief sought has not been established); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Footnotes

1

Subsequent to filing a brief on appeal, the respondent’s attorney moved to withdraw as counsel of record. Because no adequate basis has been shown to permit withdrawal of counsel at this late stage of the appeal, the motion is denied.

2

We note that the question on the cancellation application regarding the applicant’s criminal history is broadly worded to require full disclosure of all arrests and convictions.

3

We review the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including those relating to credibility, to determine if they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2016). We review de novo all questions of law, discretion, and judgment. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).

4

The respondent’s false testimony occurred during the period in which he was required to establish good moral character. In Matter of Ortega-Cabrera, 23 I&N Dec. 793 (BIA 2005), we held that an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act is a continuing application for purposes of evaluating whether an applicant has established the required 10-year period of good moral character. In this case, the respondent provided false testimony before the Immigration Judge in June 2012, 2 years after his application was filed in May 2010, and 4 years prior to our current resolution of the matter.
In Castillo-Cruz v. Holder, 581 F.3d 1154, 1162 (9th Cir. 2009), the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the jurisdiction in which this case arises, cited Matter of Ortega-Cabrera and applied our approach to determining the period of good moral character for section 240A(b)(1) cancellation of removal. Moreover, other circuit courts have expressly deferred to our interpretation under Chevron, U.S.A., Inc. v. Natural Resources Defense Council, Inc., 467 U.S. 837 (1984). Rodriguez-Avalos v. Holder, 788 F.3d 444, 452-55 (5th Cir. 2015); Duron-Ortiz v. Holder, 698 F.3d 523, 526-28 (7th Cir. 2012).
In Aragon-Salazar v. Holder, 769 F.3d 699, 706 n.4 (9th Cir. 2014), the Ninth Circuit held that an application for special rule cancellation of removal under the Nicaraguan Adjustment and Central American Relief Act (“NACARA”), Pub. L. No. 105-100, 111 Stat. 2160, 2193 (1997), is not a continuing one for purposes of evaluating good moral character and that the required period of good moral character refers only to the 7 years immediately preceding the filing of the application. The court declined to follow Castillo-Cruz, finding that it arose “in a different context.” Id. We conclude that Castillo-Cruz continues to be controlling in the context of applications for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act.

5

The respondent may have been confused by the use of the term ““departure” during a line of questioning concerning his entries and departures. However, in finding that the respondent gave false testimony, the Immigration Judge did not appear to rely on this possible inconsistency, and we have not considered it in reviewing his decision.

6

The respondent was represented by counsel throughout the proceedings, and there were no indicia of incompetency. See Matter of M-A-M-, 25 I&N Dec. 474, 477 (BIA 2011) (stating that “an alien is presumed to be competent” and that “absent indicia of mental incompetency, an Immigration Judge is under no obligation to analyze an alien’s competency”).

7

Since the respondent has not established good moral character, we need not address the Immigration Judge’s determination that he did not meet his burden to prove the requisite hardship under section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act. See INS v. Bagamasbad, 429 U.S. 24, 25 (1976) (stating that “courts and agencies are not required to make findings on issues the decision of which is unnecessary to the results they reach”).

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew Park, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final order of removal in these proceedings was entered by the Board on September 18, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent filed an untimely and number-barred motion to reopen his proceedings on May 19, 2016. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent does not dispute the untimeliness of his motion, but alleges ineffective assistance of counsel and requests that the Board exercise its sua sponte authority to reopen his proceedings in the interests of justice. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent has submitted evidence that the Superior Court of the State of California, County of Orange, has allowed the respondent to withdraw his guilty plea and vacated his 2014 conviction for possession for sale of marijuana in violation of California Health and Safety Code Section 11359 and allowed him to plead guilty to accessory to a crime, a felony in violation of Penal Code section 32 (Supplement to Motion to Reopen filed on June 7, 2016, at Tabs 3-5). Now the respondent has presented evidence indicating that his prior conviction was vacated because the plea procedures did not comply with CPL§ 440 (see Motion at attachments, Supplement to Motion to Reopen at Tabs 3-4). See Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003) (holding that a conviction that has been vacated by the criminal court based upon a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes); see also Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010) (holding that a criminal lawyer representing a non-citizen in connection with a guilty plea has a constitutional duty to advise the defendant that conviction may pose a risk of deportation).
 
Under the circumstances, we will reopen the proceedings on our own motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We express no opinion on the outcome of the case. On remand, the Immigration Judge may receive any additional evidence she deems appropriate to the full resolution of this matter. Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s removability and if necessary, any eligibility for relief from removal.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2016 WL 4035841 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HORACIO ALEJANDRO CORDERO-ALVIZO
File: AXXX XX7 417 - Harlingen, TX
June 10, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mary H. Berlanga, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s June 19, 2015, decision denying his motion to reopen removal proceedings held in absentia on May 14, 2015. The Department of Homeland Security has not opposed the appeal, which will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. On appeal, the respondent reiterates that he did not receive notice of his hearing. He states that he did not receive a notice of hearing at the address he provided to the court, and therefore was unaware of the May 14, 2015, hearing. The record reflects that the Notice to Appear was served personally on the respondent on December 6, 2013, and that he received oral notice in the Spanish language and written notice of the the consequences of failing to appear at his removal hearings (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). On January 6, 2014, attorney Ronald Higgins entered an appearance on behalf of the respondent (I.J. at 1). On January 7, 2014, attorney Avia Castillo-Garza entered an appearance on behalf of the respondent (I.J. at 1). The Immigration Court mailed both Mr. Higgins and Ms. Castillo-Garza a hearing notice for the respondent’s May 14, 2015, removal hearing (I.J. at 1). There is no evidence in the record indicating that the notice of hearing was returned to the immigration court as undeliverable.
 
Based on the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent received proper notice inasmuch as written notice of his hearing was provided to his attorneys of record. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (finding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (providing that whenever a person is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney or representative of record, or to the person himself if unrepresented). To the extent the respondent asserts that his former attorneys provided ineffective assistance of counsel, he did not establish such a claim. The respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements for filing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as enunciated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 Moreover, to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel for purposes of reopening, the respondent must demonstrate that he was substantially prejudiced by his former counsels’ performance. See Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n.2 (5th Cir. 2001) (citation omitted). Proving prejudice requires that the respondent make a prima facie showing that, absent counsels’ deficient performance, he would have been entitled to the relief he sought. See Miranda-Lores v. INS, 12 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) (citation omitted). However, the respondent has not provided any applications for relief or established that he is prima facie eligible for any form of relief from removal. The fact of Mr. Higgins’s resignation of his law license does not, in and of itself, constitute ineffective assistance of counsel in this case such that the respondent suffered prejudice as a result of his representation.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4035841 (BIA)
