U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LORENA LOPEZ ESPINOZA
File: AXXX XX8 489 - Imperial, CA
June 10, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murray D. Hilts, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John D. Holliday
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 5, 2014, decision denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, under a clearly erroneous standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The following facts are not in dispute. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, entered the United States on or about May 1985 (I.J. at 1; Application for Cancellation of removal (Form EOIR-42A) at 2). The respondent was granted temporary resident status on February 1, 1989, and adjusted her status to that of lawful permanent resident (LPR) on March 5, 1993 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 2; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 24). On June 25, 1997, the respondent was issued a Notice to Appear charging her with removability under section 212(a)(2)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(C), as an alien the Attorney General knows or has reason to believe is an illicit trafficker in a controlled substance (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). On November 14, 1997, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and denied her application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a) (I.J. at 2; Order of the Immigration Judge, dated Nov. 14, 1997). The respondent did not appeal that decision (I.J. at 1). The respondent was removed to Mexico, and she unlawfully re-entered to the United States soon thereafter (Resp. Brief at 4).
 
On February 10, 2014, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, arguing that ineffective assistance of prior counsel prevented her from pursuing an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act (I.J. at 2; Resp.’s Motion to Reopen). Specifically, she asserts that under Ortega de Robles v. INS, 58 F.3d 1355 (9th Cir. 1995)--which held that an LPR could include time spent as a lawful temporary resident to establish the requisite 7 years of lawful unrelinquished domicile for former section 212(c) relief--she had established 7 years of continuous residence for section 240A(a) relief at the time of her removal hearing (Resp. Brief at 7-10). Her prior counsel’s “failure to keep abreast” of Ortega de Robles v. INS, supra, amounted to ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp. Brief at 7-10). The Immigration Judge denied reopening, concluding that the respondent had not shown that she was prejudiced by prior counsel’s assistance (I.J. at 3-4). See Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 587 (9th Cir. 2011); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, with the following notations. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). At the time of the respondent’s removal hearing in 1997, neither the Board nor the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit had held that an LPR could include time spent as a lawful temporary resident to establish 7 years of continuous residence for cancellation of removal (I.J. at 4). The respondent’s reliance on Ortega de Robles v. INS, supra, is misplaced as that case addressed eligibility for former section 212(c) relief and was issued 2 years before cancellation of removal existed as a benefit. See Matter of Abdelghany, 26 I&N Dec. 254, 257 n.6 (BIA 2014) (explaining that the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996 (effective April 1, 1997) eliminated former section 212(c) relief and created cancellation of removal). Thus, contrary to the respondent’s appellate assertion, there is no “““clear[] conflict” between Board precedent at the time of the respondent’s removal hearing and prior counsel’s concession that the respondent did not have the requisite 7 years of continuous residence (Resp. Brief at 10). As noted by the Immigration Judge, the Board did not hold that admission as a temporary resident could be counted toward the residency requirement for cancellation of removal until 2 years after the respondent’s removal hearing (I.J. at 4). See Matter of Perez, 22 I&N Dec. 689, 692 (BIA 1999).
 
The respondent argues that her prior counsel should have argued that the holding in Ortega de Robles v. INS, supra, regarding lawful domicile for former section 212(c) purposes applied to continuous residence for section 240A(a) purposes (Resp. Brief at 6-8). Given the unsettled nature of the law at that time of the respondent’s removal hearing, we agree that prior counsel did not commit ineffective assistance of counsel by conceding the respondent’s ineligibility for cancellation of removal (I.J. at 4-5). Moreover, we agree with the Immigration Judge that respondent did not establish prejudice, as the law was unsettled at that time. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899-90 (9th Cir. 2003) (explaining that prejudice results when “the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings”) (internal citation or quotation marks omitted).
 
Finally, considering the totality of the circumstances presented and alleged, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances sufficient to justify exercising his discretionary authority to reopen the respondent’s removal proceedings sua sponte (I.J. at 4-5; Resp. Brief at 10). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: RICHARD AUSTIN PALMER

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard Joseph Link, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michele Henriques
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)] - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s December 1, 2015, decision denying his motion to terminate these removal proceedings. See Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998) (discussing Immigration Judge’s obligation to weigh the merits of a motion to terminate filed by the Department of Homeland Security after a charging document is filed and jurisdiction has vested with the Immigration Court). The Department of Homeland Security has offered a statement of non-opposition to this appeal. The appeal will be sustained and the proceedings will be terminated.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was charged with removability pursuant to the above-listed charge. Prior to the commencement of these proceedings, the criminal court granted the respondent’s motion to withdraw his guilty plea to the underlying offense. See Motion Tabs A-C. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s unopposed motion to terminate the removal proceedings, finding that “it appears that the vacatur was entered solely [sic] for the basis of avoiding immigration consequences and not vacated on the merits of the (underlying) charge.” See Immigration Judge’s Decision at 3.
 
In Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003), the Board held that if a criminal court vacates an alien’s conviction for reasons solely related to rehabilitation or immigration hardships, rather than on the basis of a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying criminal proceedings, the conviction is not eliminated for immigration purposes. Both parties to these proceedings argued before the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s attorney in his criminal case rendered ineffective assistance of counsel when advising him of the immigration consequences of a guilty plea. See Transcript of the Proceedings at 6-8, 16, 19-20. On appeal, the respondent asserts that the vacatur was based on ineffective assistance of counsel and both parties argue that termination of the removal proceedings would not contravene the holding in Matter of Pickering, supra.
 
*2 While the December 18, 2013, decision of the criminal court resulted in the vacatur of the respondent’s conviction to enable him to replead and avoid the immigration consequences of his initial plea agreement, upon review of the evidence before the Immigration Judge, it is clear that the vacatur was based, at least in part, on the finding that the respondent’s defense attorney rendered ineffective assistance by providing the respondent with incorrect advice regarding the risk of removal arising from his December 7, 2012, plea agreement. See Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 369 (2010). The criminal court’s October 18, 2013, decision vacating the conviction was not entered “““solely” to enable the respondent to avoid the immigration consequences of his conviction. The criminal court’s decision was also entered to correct a procedural defect in the criminal proceedings whereby the respondent was provided improper legal advice regarding the immigration consequences of his guilty plea prior to entering the plea. Accordingly the respondent’s appeal will be sustained, and the motion to terminate will be granted.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to terminate the removal proceedings is granted and the proceedings are terminated.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 3924027 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ERVIS FERNANDO MATOS A.K.A. WILFRED M. ROSARIO
File: AXXX XX2 312 - Boston, MA
June 9, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lawrence Gatei, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; termination
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic. On June 26, 2012, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his request for a continuance to pursue post-conviction relief and ordering his removal. Subsequent to our decision, the respondent was removed to the Dominican Republic (Respondent’s Motion at 2, 4). On March 1, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and terminate proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a response to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent claims that the conviction which formed the basis for the Immigration Judge’s removal order was vacated for immigration purposes (Respondent’s Motion at 1-5). In this regard, the respondent submits state court records which indicate that, on April 7, 2014, a Massachusetts criminal court vacated his 2009 conviction for knowingly or intentionally manufacturing, distributing, or dispensing cocaine and for knowingly or intentionally possessing cocaine pursuant to Chapter 94C, Sections 32A(a) and 34 of the Massachusetts General Laws (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab B). The respondent asserts that these documents constitute previously unavailable material evidence and requests that proceedings be reopened pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) and terminated (Respondent’s Motion at 3-4, 6).
 
The decision in these proceedings became final on June 26, 2012, when the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(7). With certain exceptions not pertinent here, a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before September 24, 2012. The motion to reopen was not filed until March 1, 2016, and the respondent has not articulated any basis for equitable tolling of the statutory time limitation (Respondent’s Motion). See Xin Qiang Liu v. Lynch, 802 F.3d 69, 75 (1st Cir. 2015); Neves v. Holder, 613 F.3d 30, 36-37 (1st Cir. 2010). Thus, the respondent’s motion to reopen based on previously unavailable material evidence is denied as untimely.
 
*2 The respondent also requests that we exercise our discretionary authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte in light of his vacated conviction (Respondent’s Motion at 1, 4-6). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent bears the burden of proof to establish the reason the conviction was vacated. Rumierz v. Gonzales, 456 F.3d 31 (1st Cir. 2006); Matter of Chavez, 24 I&N Dec. 272 (BIA 2007). A conviction vacated under a rehabilitative statute or to avoid the immigration consequences of the conviction, remains a conviction for immigration purposes. See Herrera-Inirio v. I.N.S., 208 F.3d 299, 306 (1st Cir. 2000); Matter of Marroquin-Garcia, 23 I&N Dec. 705, 713-17 (A.G. 2005); Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). A conviction vacated by a criminal court based upon a procedural or substantive defect is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes. See Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006); Matter of Pickering, supra.
 
The documents attached to the respondent’s motion include a criminal docket and handwritten docket entries related to the respondent’s 2009 conviction (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab B). The docket entries indicate that the respondent filed two motions to vacate his conviction with the criminal court, both of which were denied (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab B). The docket entries also indicate that two motions to reconsider were filed and that hearings were held with respect to the respondent’s requests for post-conviction relief. The last entry in the docket states, “Case Nolle Pressed all fees and fines remitted” (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab B). Copies of the transcript and the motions referenced in the docket entries were not provided.
 
The respondent, through counsel, asserts that his motion to vacate his conviction was based on a constitutional claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and evidence tampering (Respondent’s Motion at 2). However, uncorroborated declarations of counsel are not evidence and we are unable to discern the basis for the vacatur of the respondent’s conviction based on the record before us. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505-06 (BIA 1980). As the respondent has not presented evidence sufficient to establish the basis for the criminal court’s order, he has not met his burden of proof. See Rumierz v. Gonzales, supra; Matter of Chavez, supra. Moreover, the respondent has not established that he acted with diligence by filing his motion to reopen nearly two years after his conviction was vacated. Lastly, the respondent has not established that exceptional circumstances which warrant sua sponte reopening otherwise exist in his case (Respondent’s Motion at 2-3).1
 
*3 In light of the foregoing, we decline to exercise our discretionary authority to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, supra, at 976 (holding that the Board’s sua sponte authority is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to circumvent the time limits where enforcing them might otherwise cause hardship); see also Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 112 (BIA 2009). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and terminate is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We acknowledge the family ties referenced by the respondent in his motion (Respondent’s Motion at 2-3). However, the hardship caused by separation from family members is not an exceptional situation which warrants sua sponte reopening. See Matter of J-J-, supra; see also Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009).

2016 WL 3924042 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN BELTRAN ORTIZ
File: AXXX XX1 425 - Houston, TX
June 6, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a motion requesting the Board reconsider our decision of March 8, 2016, in which we affirmed the denial of his first untimely motion to reopen. The respondent also again seeks reopening with the submission of additional evidence. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). As a motion to reconsider, this motion is timely filed. Insofar as the respondent seeks reopening, this motion is untimely and number-barred. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The record does not contain a response from the Department of Homeland Security. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider must identify a material error of fact or law in the decision for which reconsideration is being requested. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The respondent argues that his prior motion should have been construed as a “statutorily authorized” motion to reopen, notwithstanding that it was untimely filed. In support of this argument, the respondent relies on an interpretation of the term “sua sponte” which is limited to circumstances in which the Board chooses to reopen proceedings in the absence of a motion from the parties (Motion to Reconsider and Reopen at 6-11). He contends that if his motion was “statutorily authorized,” and not subject to reopening under “sua sponte” discretionary authority provided for in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), then the limitation on our discretionary authority in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d) does not apply.
 
We are not persuaded of any material error in the Board’s decision of March 8, 2016. First, the respondent relies solely on a narrow reading of the phrase “““on its own motion” in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), which ignores the proviso that the Board may reopen “at any time.” The phrase “at any time” is inclusive of times when the Board is ruling on a motion from the parties. The respondent does not cite any authority for his interpretation of the Board’s authority under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Moreover, as the respondent acknowledges, the respondent’s interpretation directly contradicts not only Board precedent but also relevant circuit court authority (Motion at 9-11). See Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288 (5th Cir. 2009); Navarro-Miranda v. Ashcroft, 330 F.3d 672 (5th Cir. 2003); see also Garcia-Carias v. Holder, 697 F.3d 257, 265 (5th Cir. 2012).
 
*2 Further, the respondent ignores that on appeal the Board was not interpreting our own authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), but rather the Immigration Judge’s authority to do so under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). While these regulatory provisions are generally interpreted in an identical fashion with respect to sua sponte reopening, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) is explicit that an Immigration Judge’s authority to reopen sua sponte may be utilized “upon his or her own motion at any time, or upon motion of the Service or the alien” (emphasis added). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Finally, if the respondent’s argument were accepted it would require the mandatory denial of his motion to reopen on the basis of untimeliness. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act contains an explicit timeliness limitation on motions to reopen. For those motions such as the respondent’s which do not fall within a statutorily authorized exception from the time limits, the regulatory authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte represents the sole mechanism by which the motion could be granted. For these reasons, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.
 
The respondent also seeks to present additional evidence to cure defects in the prior motion, and to that end this motion constitutes an untimely, second motion to reopen. Specifically, the Board previously noted that the respondent’s motion to remand on appeal did not comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent’s current motion seeks to remedy that deficiency by submitting evidence of compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
As alternatively noted by the Board in our decision of March 8, 2016, the respondent has not set forth a viable argument for ineffective assistance. The respondent has alleged equitable tolling was warranted because prior counsel did seek equitable tolling in the respondent’s first untimely motion. However, he has not explained why competent counsel would have sought equitable tolling in his first motion to reopen.1 Equitable tolling is ordinarily reserved for circumstances in which a party is actively misled or in which ineffective assistance of counsel prejudiced an alien and prevented a timely filing of an application or motion. See, e.g., Ott v. Johnson, 192 F.3d 510, 513 (5th Cir. 1999). The respondent, by contrast, was seeking reopening based on an intervening change in law, which does not constitute a basis for equitable tolling. See Matter of X-G-W-, 22 I&N Dec. 71 (BIA 1998); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999). To the extent that the respondent seeks sua sponte reopening based on an intervening change in law, we construe the departure bar in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d) as a limitation on our discretionary authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte.
 
*3 Finally, we note that the respondent has not shown he would no longer be found removable even if we reopened proceedings. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). The respondent references United States v. Chapa-Garza, 243 F.3d 921 (5th Cir. 2001), which did hold that convictions for driving under the influence do not establish removability for an aggravated felony crime of violence (Motion at 2-3). However, the respondent had also conceded removability under section 237(a)(2)(E)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(E)(i), and the respondent does not allege that he is no longer removable on that basis.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Respondent has submitted a letter from prior counsel which acknowledges that he did not seek equitable tolling in the first motion. However, the letter does not suggest prior counsel admitted to rendering ineffective assistance, as respondent claims, as the letter does not suggest that prior counsel was deficient for having not done so (Motion at 3-4; Motion, Tab A, at 7).

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Vacated by IN RE: OLYMPIA CONCEPCION TORREZ-PALACIOS, BIA, June 1, 2017
2016 WL 3924057 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLYMPIA CONCEPCION TORREZ-PALACIOS
File: AXXX XX8 483 - Los Angeles, CA
June 2, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert Gordon Berke, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on March 29, 2002, after not appearing at a hearing. She filed a motion to reopen on October 17, 2014, and she appeals from the Immigration Judge’s December 12, 2014, decision denying the motion. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that exceptional circumstances, to wit, ineffective assistance of counsel, prevented her from appearing at her hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) (providing that a motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal); Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672 (9th Cir. 2011) (requiring for equitable tolling that an alien acted with “due” or “reasonable” diligence in discovering any “deception, fraud, or error” that prevented an alien from timely filing a motion). In her declaration, the respondent states that she appeared at her hearing with her former counsel, and she was informed by the Immigration Court that she would receive her Notice of Hearing by mail. However, the Notice of Hearing reflects that the respondent was personally served on February 15, 2002. In addition, the respondent states that a year passed, and she did not hear either from her former attorney or the court. She states that she attempted to contact her former attorney via telephone, was unable to reach him, and discovered in 2013 that she had been ordered removed from the United States in absentia. Notwithstanding her personal service of the Notice of Hearing and whether she satisfied the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has not explained why she did not immediately inquire about her immigration status with the Immigration Court or another attorney after she was unable to make contact with her former attorney. In light of the respondent’s lack of due diligence, we decline to equitably toll the 180-day for filing her motion to reopen, and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny it as untimely filed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 3924057 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 3924073 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILLIAM ALFONSO MORA VELASQUEZ A.K.A. WILLIAM M. MORA
File: AXXX XX4 712 - Lumpkin, GA
May 31, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amy L. Velasquez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Nicaragua, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated February 9, 2016, denying his request for a continuance and ordering his removal from the United States. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent does not dispute that he is subject to removal from the United States because he is an alien who is present in this country without being admitted or paroled by an immigration officer or who arrived at any time or place other than as designated by the Attorney General (I.J. at 1-2; Exh. 1). See section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i). Moreover, he does not contend that, at the present time, he is eligible for any form of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.1(a)(1) (enumerating many of the forms of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge). For example, on appeal, the respondent concedes that his conviction for sexual battery precludes him from establishing eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1) (Respondent’s Br. at 4). The respondent has declined to apply for voluntary departure (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 8).
 
Despite the absence of an express opposition from the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), the respondent did not establish that there was “good cause” for a continuance of these removal proceedings (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 1240.6. While the respondent, through counsel, claimed that he had contacted an attorney to file for post-conviction relief, he did not present any evidence that he had actually filed for post-conviction relief with the appropriate state court (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 8-9). See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (recognizing that the statements of counsel are not evidence). Overall, considering the speculative nature of the outcome of the respondent’s yet-to-be filed post-conviction relief claim and the absence of any evidence that such a claim would be filed in the near future, it was appropriate for the Immigration Judge to conclude these removal proceedings by denying the respondent’s request for a continuance and ordering his removal from the United States. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that a continuance should not be granted where it is being sought “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”); cf. Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009) (holding that a continuance may be warranted where an alien has demonstrated that he is the beneficiary of a pending immigrant visa petition and established a likelihood of success on an application for adjustment of status).
 
*2 The respondent has also not established that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his request for a continuance and enter an order of removal. See Lapaix v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 605 F.3d 1138, 1143 (11th Cir. 2010); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-57 (BIA 1983). Notwithstanding the respondent’s aforementioned conviction for sexual battery, he is otherwise subject to removal from the United States as charged in the Notice to Appear. Moreover, the respondent has not shown that, if his conviction was vacated, the outcome of these proceedings would likely be different, i.e., he would have been granted relief from removal.1 See Ibrahim v. INS, 821 F.2d 1547, 1550 (11th Cir. 1987). In particular, the respondent has not established that, if his conviction was vacated, he would likely succeed on the merits of a claim to cancellation of removal. While, through counsel, the respondent contends that “life has become extremely difficult” for his family, he has not presented any evidence which establishes that his removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to a qualifying relative. See section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act; Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001) (holding that to establish “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship,” an applicant for cancellation of removal must demonstrate that his or his qualifying relative would suffer hardship that is substantially beyond that which would ordinarily be expected to result from the alien’s deportation).
 
Finally, the respondent has not established that the record should be remanded to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the evidence that he has presented on appeal. The respondent, who contends that his conviction for battery was the result of a consensual encounter with a man, has presented evidence that, subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s entry of the order of removal, he filed for post-conviction relief based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and other constitutional defects in his criminal proceedings. See generally Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010); Boykin v. Alabama, 395 U.S. 238 (1969). However, despite the filing of a claim to post-conviction relief, his conviction remains final for immigration purposes. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323 (BIA 1996); Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992). Overall, considering the lack of evidence which establishes that the respondent is otherwise prima facie eligible for relief from removal, we are not persuaded that, if the record was remanded, the respondent’s speculative eligibility for post-conviction relief and, in turn, eligibility for cancellation of removal would be shown to amount to “good cause” for a continuance. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (holding that a party seeking a remand must establish that, if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were remanded, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case).
 
*3 For the reasons set forth above, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance and order his removal from the United States to Nicaragua. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

While, on appeal, the respondent refers to being a “category one enforcement priority,” the classification of an alien by the DHS is not subject to review by this Board. See Matter of Fede, 20 I&N Dec. 35, 36-37 (BIA 1989). As such, we do not deem the potential loss of an opportunity to be deemed a lower enforcement priority by the DHS’s internal classification procedures to amount to prejudice warranting remanded proceedings. If the respondent believes that he should not be removed from the United States, he is not precluded from requesting a stay of removal from the DHS. See 8 C.F.R. § 241.6.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MAURICIO CORTEZ A.K.A. JOSE M CORTEZ
File: AXXX XX0 869 - Arlington, VA
May 25, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey A. Devore, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Collin Schueler
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was before the Board on December 7, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his motion to remand on grounds of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent filed this motion to reopen proceedings on March 7, 2016, again alleging ineffective assistance. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion.
 
As we stated in our prior order, the appeal brief and motion to remand did not document that the respondent is eligible for Temporary Protected Status (TPS). Accordingly, prejudice resulting from ineffective assistance of counsel was not shown. The present motion includes documents that were neither offered at the hearing nor included with the respondent’s previous motion to remand. The motion states that “counsel was in possession of evidence which the [r]espondent had provided” (Mot. at 3), but does not specify the evidence or explain why it was not provided with the previous motion.
 
The documents submitted with the present motion do not establish the respondent’s continuous physical presence in the United States so as to be eligible for TPS. Only one document dates from before March 9, 2001, and that document merely evidences the respondent’s presence on a particular date and does not establish continuous presence. See Mot., Tab C. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (“A motion to reopen proceedings shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing [.]”). As the respondent’s evidence does not demonstrate eligibility for relief, prejudice has not been established. Further, as noted, the respondent has not explained why the evidence now offered for the first time was not previously presented, including with the motion to remand. For these reasons, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 3226593 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALI AMINZADEM
File: AXXX XX8 004 - Oklahoma City, OK
May 20, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arthur Campbell Cooke, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Iran, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s August 4, 2015, decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a brief in opposition to the appeal. Proceedings will be reopened and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel such that his proceedings should be reopened. The Immigration Judge acknowledged that the respondent’s previous counsel did not file the application for relief from removal - specifically, cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a) - within the time frame the Immigration Judge had set (I.J. at 1). However, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent’s own acts contributed to his counsel not filing the application, and therefore, he had not demonstrated that ineffective assistance had prejudiced the outcome of proceedings (I.J. at 2).
 
Upon our de novo review, we determine that the respondent’s proceedings merit reopening. We first note that the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed jointly with the DHS (I.J. at 1). Further, as noted above, the DHS has not filed an opposition to the respondent’s current appeal. Second, the respondent has met the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (I.J. at 2). The respondent’s previous counsel was aware of the filing deadline and did not file the cancellation application within the time frame set by the Immigration Judge or withdraw her representation of the respondent (I.J. at 1).
 
Thus, while we acknowledge the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent was afforded an opportunity to apply for relief, we do not agree that the application was waived, when the respondent affirmatively hired counsel to file the application, and counsel neglected to do so. Finally, the respondent is prima facie eligible for relief from removal and has submitted the relevant application. See Joint Motion to Reopen Proceedings filed June 15, 2015. Under the totality of the circumstances, we agree that the respondent’s proceedings merit reopening and the record should be remanded to the Immigration Judge for a hearing on the respondent’s eligibility for relief from removal under section 240A(a) of the Act. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 3226593 (BIA)

2016 WL 3226601 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YAN QING CAO
File: AXXX XX4 953 - New York, NY
May 19, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Chunyu Jean Wang, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 On January 14, 2016, the respondent submitted her third motion to reopen proceedings in which the Board dismissed her appeal on August 16, 2004. The motion will be denied.
 
To the extent that the respondent suggests that the Board committed error in not addressing issues relating to the Immigration Judge’s conduct of the hearing, this aspect of her motion is in the nature of one seeking reconsideration. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Whether treated as a motion to reopen or reconsider, the respondent’s motion is untimely, and as a motion to reopen it is also number-barred as it is her third such motion. Sections 240(c)(6), (7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6), (7); 8 C.F.R. § § 1003.2(b), (c)(2).
 
The respondent contends that reopening and/or reconsideration is nonetheless appropriate in light of misconduct on the part of the Immigration Judge, and as her first attorney gave her erroneous legal advice, preventing her from pursuing derivative asylee status. The respondent also asserts that other attorneys failed to raise or address the issues she now cites in her motion. As she avers that she experienced a gross miscarriage of justice and her due process rights were violated, she asks that proceedings be sua sponte reopened. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
However, the substantive basis for the respondent’s motion is that she was provided ineffective assistance by the numerous attorneys1 who have previously represented her in these proceedings. Despite this assertion, the respondent has not complied, even substantially, with any of the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against any of her various former counsels. Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133, 143 (2d Cir. 2007); Twum v. INS, 411 F.3d 54 (2d Cir. 2005); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). She correctly notes that her first attorney, who prepared her asylum application, was later disbarred (and was expelled from practicing before this Board on May 6, 2002). However, this fact alone does not exempt the respondent from any compliance with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against him or any of her other former attorneys.
 
*2 In this regard we note that her first attorney never appeared in person before the Immigration Judge on the respondent’s behalf. While an associate at his firm did appear on one occasion, that hearing was continued, and the merits of the respondent’s asylum claim were not reached (Tr. at 6-11). Thereafter the respondent appeared with other counsel, not associated with her first attorney’s law firm, with the Immigration Judge noting at the February 15, 2001, hearing that her first attorney had been arrested. Neither he nor anyone from his law firm was representing the respondent at the time of her merits hearing on April 28, 2003. Rather, the respondent had three other attorneys appear on her behalf during the remainder of her hearings (Tr. at 14, 30, 48, 58, 61). The respondent was also represented by three different attorneys during the course of her appeal and two prior motions to reopen. As such, the fact that her first attorney and/or his office was involved at the outset of the respondent’s case does not establish he was ineffective per se and does not excuse her failure to comply with any of the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against any of her subsequent former representatives. Therefore, reopening on this basis is not warranted.
 
As noted, the aspect of the respondent’s motion arguing a gross miscarriage of justice relative to the former-Immigration Judge’s handling of her case is in the nature of one seeking reconsideration. It is untimely by more than a decade. Section 240(c)(6) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). As indicated above, to the extent that the respondent avers that former counsel erred by not raising this issue, she has not complied with the requirements for reopening on such a basis. Twum v. INS, supra. The respondent has not identified any exceptional circumstance preventing her from such compliance, or which otherwise supports sua sponte reopening or reconsideration of her proceedings. Matter of G-D-, supra at 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (“As a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations.”); Matter of J-J-, supra.
 
We further do not find that this case presents a gross miscarriage of justice as alleged by the respondent. We do not condone either the tone or non-judicious comments of the Immigration Judge. However, as the Board specifically declined to join in the Immigration Judge’s frivolous finding, the respondent was not precluded from pursuing adjustment of status, contrary to her arguments otherwise.
 
This case is also distinguishable from the situation presented in Matter of Farinas, 12 I&N Dec. 467, 471-72 (BIA 1967), wherein we found a gross miscarriage of justice. In that case the respondent’s deportation order, which was valid when entered, became invalid by virtue of controlling circuit precedent issued prior to the execution of the order. We further note that another case, Matter of Malone, 11 I&N Dec. 730, 731-32 (BIA 1966), also finding gross miscarriage of justice, is distinct from the respondent’s case as that respondent’s deportation order was clearly not in accord with the law as interpreted at the time of issuance. Neither of these situations is present here. Also, unlike those cases, the respondent’s arguments are largely premised on various claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. Both the circuit where this case arises and the Board have clear precedential authority regarding the requirements for such claims, and the respondent has made no effort to comply with any of the same, nor explained why no action has been taken in this regard.
 
*3 As such, we find that the respondent’s motion is not exempt from the noted statutory bars, and the exercise of our sua sponte authority is not warranted. Therefore, we find that neither reopening nor reconsideration is warranted. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Hope Malia Holiona
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record indicates that the respondent has had some eight different attorneys representing her during the proceedings below, on appeal, and in the course of her prior two motions (Tr. at 6, 14, 30, 61; Exh. 2 (Form I-589); Notice of Appeal; 10/5/04 Motion to Reopen; 4/2/07 Motion to Reopen).

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN COX-SANTIAGO
File: AXXX XX0 056 - Chicago, IL
May 18, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
AL Kola, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alexandra Kostich
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 20, 2015, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his October 14, 2014, removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
In his motion to reopen, which was not accompanied by an affidavit, the respondent alleged that his failure to appear resulted because he mistook the hearing date, believing it was on December 15, 2014. The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that the respondent’s confusion regarding his hearing date did not constitute exceptional circumstances beyond his control that would excuse his failure to appear. See Uriostegui v. Gonzales, 415 F.3d 660 (7th Cir. 2005) (concluding that mishearing the date uttered by the interpreter was nowhere near the illustrative examples of exceptional circumstances given in the statute).
 
On appeal, the respondent now alleges lack of notice and also avers that his prior attorney never informed the respondent of his court date. The respondent did not assert lack of notice in his motion to reopen, and he has not proffered an affidavit or explained why lack of notice was not previously alleged. Counsel’s arguments are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).
 
*2 Ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an exceptional circumstance that excuses an alien’s failure to appear and allows reopening of the removal proceedings and rescission of a removal order. Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472, 473-74 (BIA 1996). However, the alien must satisfy the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) for claiming ineffectiveness of counsel. Specifically, the alien must: “(1) submit an affidavit establishing that she had an agreement with counsel to represent her and detailing its terms; (2) present evidence that she has given notice to her counsel of the ineffectiveness claim and an opportunity to respond to the allegations, and include any response she has received; and (3) if the attorney violated his ethical or legal obligations, show that she has filed a complaint with the governing disciplinary authorities or explain why she has not done so.” Lin Xing Jiang v. Holder, 639 F.3d 751, 755 (7th Cir. 2011) (citing Matter of Lozada). Satisfying the Lozada requirements “““is a necessary condition to obtaining reopening on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.” Id.
 
Here, the respondent has not identified who his prior attorney was. Nor has he fulfilled any of the requirements necessary for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, supra. Consequently, reopening on this basis is not warranted. See, e.g., Ghaffar v. Mukasey, 551 F.3d 651, 656 (7th Cir. 2008) (“Ghaffar did not comply with any of the Lozada requirements. Consequently, the Board acted well within its rights to deny his motion for remand”); Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498, 504 (7th Cir. 2001) (“[W]e have difficulty understanding how an alien who fails to comply with the Board’s criteria can succeed in challenging its decision”).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: FRANCISCO RAMIREZ RAMOS
File: AXXX XX4 180 - Lumpkin, Georgia
May 12, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fred (Rocky) Rawcliffe, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Blake Doughty
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent filed a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s December 2, 2015, decision. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, and the likelihood of future events, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015; Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other issues in an appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The sole issue presented on appeal is a due process challenge to the Immigration Judge’s conduct of the proceedings by failing to continue the proceedings to allow the respondent to appear with the counsel of his choice.
 
The record reflects that when the respondent first appeared before the Immigration Judge at a master calendar hearing on November 12, 2015, the Immigration Judge continued the proceedings for 6 days, until November 18, 2015, to allow the respondent to appear with counsel. On that date, the respondent appeared with counsel, at which time the pleadings were taken (Tr. at 31). Respondent’s counsel indicated that respondent would likely seek cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The Immigration Judge (Tr. at 31) continued the proceedings for one week, until November 25, 2015, for the filing of all applications for relief from removal, admonishing the respondent and his attorney that if not filed by that time they would be deemed abandoned. At the reconvened proceedings, no applications were submitted. The respondent’s counsel claimed (Tr. at 33) that he had not prepared any applications for relief because respondent’s wife had told him his “services would no longer be required.” This was contrary to the respondent’s explanation to the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 33) that his wife had told him that his attorney no longer wanted to represent him. Respondent’s counsel (Tr. at 34) sought clarify the communication he had with respondent’s wife claiming he had advised them that “““there was very little likelihood that [the respondent] can prevail on any kind of application,” notwithstanding the substantial equities supporting the respondent’s request, because “a cancellation of removal is very difficult to win.” The Immigration Judge continued the case until December 2, 2015, for the filing of all applications for relief, noting that as respondent’s counsel had yet to submit a motion to withdraw, he continued to represent the respondent (and not his wife) in these proceedings. A week later at the reconvened proceedings, when no applications were filed, respondent’s counsel told the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 40) that he thought respondent’s newly-retained counsel would appear and file the application for cancellation of removal. The Immigration Judge (Tr. at 40) rejected that explanation, noting that as no new Notice of Appearance (Form EOIR-28) had been filed with the Immigration Court by any other attorney on behalf of the respondent, he still continued to represent him as the attorney of record. The Immigration Judge proceeded to find all applications for relief abandoned for failure to timely file and ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States.
 
*2 We note that all applications and related documents that are to be considered in a proceeding before an Immigration Judge must be filed with the Immigration Court having administrative control over the record of proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(a). The regulations provide that if an application or supportive documentation is not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application shall be deemed waived. See 8 CF.R. § 1003.31(c); Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992) (“The Board has long held that applications for benefits under the Act are properly denied as abandoned when the alien fails to timely file them.”); Matter of Jean, 17 I&N Dec. 100 (BIA 1979). Failure to file necessary documentation or to meet security and biometric requirements also “constitutes abandonment of the application” unless the applicant demonstrates “good cause” for such failure. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.47(c).
 
We acknowledge the regulations allow an Immigration Judge to find the respondent’s failure to submit his application for relief from removal within the time set to constitute an abandonment of the application. However, that decision must be balanced against the due process concerns of ensuring the respondent has a full and fair hearing. The respondent’s entire immigration proceedings before the Immigration Judge commenced and were concluded in less than 3 weeks. The record clearly reflects that the continued representation of the respondent by said attorney was not viewed favorably by either party, and the record indicates the respondent was in the process of obtaining new legal representation. The outcome of the respondent’s case was clearly prejudiced by said attorney’s actions, i.e., his failure to timely submit the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal.1
 
Consequently, we find that under the particular circumstances of this case, in order to ensure the respondent’s right to a full and fair hearing, a remand is appropriate to allow the respondent to appear represented by his newly-retained counsel, who could then properly file the necessary application for relief from removal.
 
Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the respondent was clearly prejudiced by the failure to timely file the application for relief from removal, we do not find it necessary at this juncture to determine whether the actions (or inaction) of said attorney constituted ineffective assistance of counsel.

IN RE: YEFRI BERTIN SORTO-HERNANDEZ

Jonathan R. Maldonado, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa P. Durant
Assistant Chief Counsel

*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 2, 2016, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On January 16, 2015, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed and the respondent waived appeal. On February 1, 2016, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to reopen, requesting sua sponte reopening of proceedings to allow the respondent to apply for asylum, withholding of removal and protection under the Convention Against Torture (“CAT”) (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2-3). The respondent also stated that he may raise a claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel, after obtaining additional information (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 4, fn 3). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposed reopening, arguing that the respondent did not assert exceptional circumstances to justify sua sponte reopening (DHS Opposition to Motion at 4). The DHS further asserted that, to the extent that the respondent was asserting ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent’s motion should be denied for failure to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (DHS Opposition to Motion at 5-6).
 
On February 2, 2016, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion, finding that the respondent did not comply with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent appeals this determination.
 
In his motion to reopen and on appeal, the respondent claims that previous counsel rendered ineffective assistance. Along with his brief on appeal, the respondent has filed new evidence that he claims supports his assertion that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel and that his actions prejudiced the respondent (see Respondent’s Brief at 7-9, see also attachments A-D). Among these documents is a letter written by the respondent’s prior counsel stating that although the respondent articulated a fear of return to El Salvador, no asylum application was pursued before the Immigration Court or the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) (see attachment D).
 
*2 The respondent has shown ineffective assistance of counsel, which justifies a remand. Based on the totality of the circumstances, we determine it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to allow the respondent to pursue an application for asylum and related relief.1 The Immigration Judge should also consider whether, in accordance with section 235(d)(7)(B) of the William Wilberforce Trafficking Victims Protection Reauthorization Act of 2008 (“TVPRA”), Pub. L. No. 110-457, 122 Stat. 5044  (codified at 8 U.S.C. § 1158(b)(3)(C)), the USCIS has initial jurisdiction over any application for asylum and withholding of removal.
 
On remand, the parties should be given the opportunity to present additional evidence and arguments. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The removal proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and entry of a new decision.
 
Margaret M O’Herron
FOR THE BOARD

We emphasize that the Immigration Judge did not err in denying the respondent’s motion to reopen given the evidence before him at the time of his decision.

IN RE: HOXQUELIN GOMEZ-HEREDIA A.K.A. OQUELIN GOMEZHEREDIA A.K.A. SOKELIN HEREDIA
File: AXXX XX9 342 - New York, NY
May 5, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raymond G. Lahoud, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kamephis Perez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 12, 2016. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and denied a requested continuance.1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. At the hearing, the respondent requested a continuance of proceedings to enable him to pursue post-conviction relief with regard to the convictions that serve as the basis for removal and render him ineligible for relief from removal. The respondent was granted a continuance from July 28, 2015, to September 1, 2015, to submit an application for relief. He submitted no application on that date (I.J. at 2-3). Another continuance was granted to enable the respondent to submit the plea minutes from the conviction (I.J. at 3; Exh. 4). The case was then continued to January 12, 2016, for a decision (I.J. at 3). The respondent requested another continuance because he was still pursuing the motions to vacate the convictions that render him removable (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge denied the request in part because the plea minutes relating to the latter conviction show that the state court judge asked the respondent whether he understood that a guilty plea may result in his deportation (I.J. at 3-6; Exh. 4).2
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §.§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). We conclude that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors in determining that good cause was not shown for a continuance.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motions, such motions do not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). Nor do they render the convictions non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992). We also note that the respondent’s motions were scheduled to be heard on April 27, 2016, but the respondent does not indicate that the convictions have been vacated.3
 
*2 Also on appeal, the respondent challenges his classification as an arriving alien. He asserts that his re-entry into the United States was not an admission. He asserts that his June 2015 trip to the Dominican Republic was brief, casual, and innocent, and therefore should not be considered an admission under the Fleuti doctrine that arose out of Rosenberg v. Fleuti, 374 U.S. 449 (1963).
 
However, this Board has concluded that the amendment to section 101(a)(13) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(13), by the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Division C of Pub. L. No. 104-208, 110 Stat. 3009-546 (Sept. 30, 1996) (IIRIRA), eliminated the Fleuti doctrine. See Matter of Collado-Munoz, 21 I&N Dec. 1061 (BIA 1998). In Matter of Collado-Munoz, this Board held that, under the amended section 101(a)(13), a lawful permanent resident who was convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude, on return from travel abroad, would be seeking admission. The Supreme Court assumed this to be the case in Vartelas v. Holder, 132 S. Ct. 1479, 1484-85 & n.2 (2012). In that case, the Supreme Court held that the Fleuti doctrine still applies to lawful permanent residents with pre-IIRIRA convictions who travel abroad. See Vartelas v. Holder, supra, at 1489-92. However, in the present case, both of the respondent’s convictions are post-IIRIRA convictions, having occurred on January 27, 1999, and October 6, 2010. Consequently, the amended section 101(a)(13) applies to the respondent, and he was properly classified as a lawful permanent resident seeking admission.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Blair O’Connor
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Relief from removal is not an issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S. Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

3

If the respondent were to ultimately prevail in his post-conviction motions, he may file a motion to reopen with the Board asking it to consider such evidence.

IN RE: ARISTEO SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX6 457 - Houston, TX
April 29, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rory H. Potter
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on July 9, 2015, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our April 28, 2015 affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s November 5, 2013 decision. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his 2004 removal order based on a change in the law. The respondent now filed a motion to reopen with the Board. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has opposed the respondent’s current motion. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
As the DHS correctly points out, the respondent’s motion, submitted on July 10, 2015, is both untimely and number-barred. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent acknowledges that his current motion is number-barred, but argues that sua sponte reopening is nonetheless warranted on the basis of the ineffective assistance of his attorney. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 219-20 (5th Cir. 2008) (“This court has held that a request for equitable tolling of a time- or number-barred motion to reopen on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is ‘in essence an argument that the BIA should have exercised its discretion to reopen the proceeding sua sponte based upon the doctrine of equitable tolling.”DD’ (internal citation omitted)).
 
The respondent’s attorney’s failure to meet the Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit’s filing deadline for appealing the Board’s decision does not demonstrate a sufficient basis to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings. The respondent did not meet the procedural requirements to claim ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496-98 (5th Cir. 2000) (upholding the Lozada requirements). In fact, the respondent has retained the same attorney for this motion to reopen. The respondent also does not demonstrate that he merits a favorable exercise of our discretion, after previously filing an untimely motion and failing to raise issues before the appropriate adjudicative body, and after we have twice previously declined to exercise our sua sponte authority. See generally Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 2848927 (BIA)

2016 WL 2848945 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZENADO RUIZ-NIEVES A.K.A. ZENAIDO RUIZ-NIEVES
File: AXXX XX3 260 - Adelanto, CA
April 27, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven R. Espinoza, Esquire
*1 A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was mailed on December 21, 2015. The appeal was accordingly due on or before January 20, 2016.1 The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was mailed after the due date and filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on March 16, 2016. We conclude that the appeal is untimely.
 
In conjunction with the appeal the respondent files a motion to accept late-filed appeal.2 Counsel states in his February 29, 2016, declaration that his staff did not calendar the deadline to file the Notice of Appeal with the Board and did not bring the Immigration Judge’s decision to his attention until on or about February 9, 2016 (Motion Exh. B). We conclude that the respondent does not show substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to properly allege ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent does not present his affidavit regarding the ineffective assistance of counsel claim. His February 16, 2016, statement that he wants counsel to file an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen leaves many questions unanswered (Motion Exh. C).3 The second requirement is excused because counsel obviously knows about the allegations of ineffective assistance.
 
The respondent did not file a complaint against counsel with California state bar authorities or explain why not. Counsel did not self-report his ineffective assistance to California state bar authorities. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 825-26 (9th Cir. 2003) (failure to file bar complaint not fatal where former counsel submitted letter of self-report to bar); section 6068(o) of the Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code (duties of attorney). When an alien retains prior counsel to pursue an argument of inadequacy of that same attorney, the reporting requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, remain meaningful. It is not for the attorney to decide issues regarding his or her prior conduct. The self-reporting requirement lets a state bar decide and puts the state bar on notice if there are further problems, particularly problems of a continuing nature.
 
*2 We conclude that the respondent does not show substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada and we thus do not consider this appeal by certification. The Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
Because we are dismissing the appeal as untimely, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See sections 240(c)(6)(A) & (B) and 240(c)(7)(A) & (C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6)(A) & (B) and (c)(7)(A) & (C); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); see Matter of J- J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
In light of the foregoing, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to accept late-filed appeal is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed as untimely.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 6, 2015, which ordered him removed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but we review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed upon noting that no application for relief was filed and that no relief was available. We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. Although the respondent indicated on the Notice of Appeal that he intended to file a separate written brief or statement, no such submission has been received, despite counsel being granted the requested extension. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(E). In the Notice of Appeal, the respondent states only that there was ineffective assistance of counsel and that counsel failed to file for any relief on behalf of the respondent. However, the respondent does not in any way comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, he has not identified any relief for which he may be eligible. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
