2016 WL 1084515 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMON ALEXIS RAMOS-ZEPEDA
File: AXXX XX4 273 - New York, NY
February 24, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Victoria Campos, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; administrative closure
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on March 4, 2003, after not appearing at his hearing. He appeals from the Immigration Judge’s April 16, 2015, decision denying his March 6, 2015, motion to reopen. The respondent also has requested administrative closure of these proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed, and the motion will be denied.
 
The Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
In his appellate brief, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge incorrectly denied sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s and the Immigration Judge’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). He states that his former attorney advised him not to appear at his hearing, and he has been a productive member of society. He further states that he is the beneficiary of an approved visa petition (Form I-130) filed by his United States citizen spouse and eligible for a provisional unlawful presence waiver. In addition, the respondent argues that his spouse would suffer depression and hardship if he were removed from the United States. However, the respondent has not informed his former counsel of his ineffective assistance of counsel allegation and thus deprived counsel of the opportunity to respond. Moreover, the respondent has not exercised due diligence regarding his obligations before the Immigration Court. In light of the foregoing, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying sua sponte reopening of these proceedings.
 
Moreover, the respondent’s motion for administrative closure will be denied. Administrative closure is an administrative convenience that removes cases from the calendar and does not result in a final order. See Matter of Lopez-Barrios, 20 I&N Dec. 203 (BIA 1990); Matter of Amico, 19 I&N Dec. 652 (BIA 1988). A final order has already been entered. In addition, we decline to reopen these proceedings to grant administrative closure to await processing of a provisional unlawful presence waiver.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed, and the motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 1084515 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 946690 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIO CAMPOS-NAVARRO
File: AXXX XX2 771 - Denver, CO
February 18, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jonathan M. Hyman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kalin Ivany
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 4, 2014, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge failed to take into consideration the impact of the Deferred Action for Parental Accountability (DAPA). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) filed a motion for summary affirmance. We will dismiss the appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The record shows that the respondent was placed in removal proceedings with the issuance of the Notice to Appear dated May 5, 2010. In a decision dated September 18, 2014, the Immigration Court granted respondent relief in the form of pre-conclusion voluntary departure for 120 days and also issued an alternate order of removal to Mexico if the respondent failed to depart on or before January 16, 2015. On November 26, 2014, the respondent filed a motion to reopen claiming that he may be eligible for a recently announced deferred action program. On December 2, 2014, DHS filed an Opposition to Respondent’s Motion to Reopen. The Immigration Judge denied the motion and the respondent timely appealed.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by failing to address his allegation that his previous counsel inadequately advised him of the consequences of accepting voluntary departure. Although the respondent indicates on appeal that he filed a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that, although the respondent raised this issue, he specifically disclaimed any ineffective assistance of counsel claim (I.J. at 1; Motion at 5). Accordingly, the Immigration Judge did not fail to address any claim for ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent noted in his motion to reopen that he was basing his request to reopen his case for consideration of the announcement by the President of the United States of America of an imminent Executive Order calling for additional deferred action called DAPA. The Board finds that it need not reach the question of whether such executive action constitutes collateral relief outside the jurisdiction of the Immigration Judge because, in this case, that executive action is not currently available and it is speculative whether it will become available in the future.
 
The Immigration Judge properly noted that, at the hearing on September 18, 2014, the respondent received oral notice of the consequences of failing to voluntarily depart, including the consequences of filing a motion to reopen. The respondent’s voluntary departure order automatically terminates and converts to the alternate order of removal to Mexico. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(b)(E)(iii).
 
*2 Under the circumstances in this case, we find that the Immigration Judge did not err in denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 946690 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 946721 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PHILIP CHI YAN MAN
File: AXXX XX8 479 - San Francisco, CA
February 17, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Zachary Nightingale, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael D. Steinberg
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on July 11, 2013, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. On October 1, 2015, the respondent filed a second, untimely motion to reopen seeking remand to pursue a waiver of inadmissibility. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondent’s motion. The respondent, in turn, has filed a reply to the DHS’ opposition. The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
Section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), states that an alien may file one motion to reopen. The respondent’s current motion, however, is his second motion to reopen. The motion therefore is numerically barred.
 
Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, in turn, states that motions to reopen “““shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal.” We entered the final administrative order of removal in the respondent’s case on February 6, 2013, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision pretermitting his application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). The respondent, however, did not file his current motion to reopen until more than 2 ½ years later. The respondent’s motion to reopen therefore is untimely.
 
The respondent does not claim that his motion fits within any of the statutory or regulatory exceptions to the time and numeric limits on motions. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) - (iv) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). The respondent instead argues that his motion is timely because it is based on a U visa waiver denial which constitutes new and previously unavailable evidence. In the alternative, the respondent claims that the ineffective assistance of his former attorney prevented him from pursuing a U visa at an earlier date and that he has acted diligently since discovering his eligibility for a U visa. Accordingly, he maintains that the time and numeric limits on his motion should be equitably tolled.
 
The respondent further contends that he has met the procedural requirements for reopening on the basis of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and he states that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of his former attorney because his former attorney’s errors prevented him from pursuing a U visa at an earlier date.
 
The respondent asks that his proceedings be reopened and remanded to the Immigration Judge so that he may renew, before the Immigration Judge, the waiver application he made in connection with his application for a U visa. The respondent claims that the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over the waiver application and cites L.D.G. v. Holder, 744 F.3d 1022 (7th Cir. 2014), in support of this claim. The respondent also maintains that the ability to renew his waiver before the Immigration Judge is particularly important in his case due to the factual and legal errors the DHS made in adjudicating the waiver. Finally, the respondent requests sua sponte reopening in light of the exceptional circumstances and the humanitarian factors that exist in his case.
 
*2 We disagree with the respondent’s claims that his motion is not time or numerically barred. The discovery of new and previously unavailable evidence does not eliminate the time and numeric limits on motions to reopen. Further, the respondent has not established that reopening due to ineffective assistance of counsel and diligence is appropriate in his case. Any errors his former attorney made related to relief that is not available in removal proceedings, namely, a U visa and the accompanying waiver of inadmissibility under either section 212(d)(3)(A)(ii) or 212(d)(14) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(d)(3)(A)(ii) or (d)(14). Accordingly, the alleged errors did not affect the outcome of the respondent’s removal proceeding or result in prejudice. See, e.g., Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793-94 (9th Cir. 2005) (stating that prejudice results when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings). The respondent, in fact, has been able to pursue a U visa and waiver of inadmissibility before the DHS and is still pursuing a motion to reconsider before that agency.
 
The respondent also argues that, while the DHS has exclusive jurisdiction over U visas and 212(d)(14) waivers, an Immigration Judge may consider his application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(d)(3)(A)(ii) filled in conjunction with his U visa application. The case the respondent has cited in support of this argument, however, L.D.G. v. Holder, supra, is not controlling in the respondent’s case because his case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. Further, as the DHS argues in its opposition to the respondent’s motion, we do not find L.D.G. v. Holder persuasive authority on this issue.
 
Finally, the respondent’s motion does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen his proceedings under our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997)(stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely, number-barred motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 946721 (BIA)

CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has filed an untimely and number-barred motion to reopen, through which he seeks reopening and rescission of the in absentia order of removal that was entered in his case on March 17, 2004, and raises a number of arguments regarding ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion. These proceedings will be reopened and the record remanded.
 
Considering the totality of circumstances presented in the respondent’s motion, we find it appropriate to exercise our discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Upon review of the record, it is not clear that the respondent was removable as charged at the time of the entry of the removal order, based on his 1985 conviction. See Ledezma-Galicia v. Holder, 636 F.3d 1059 (9th Cir. 2010). Moreover, even if removable, he was a long-time permanent resident of the United States who would have been eligible to pursue an application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). We are persuaded that the proceedings should be reopened and the in absentia order of removal rescinded. Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, these proceedings are reopened, and the in absentia order of removal is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
David B. Holmes

IN RE: CECILIA D. GUERRERO A.K.A. CECILIA DE LAS MERCEDES SANCHEZ DE GUERRERO A.K.A. CECILIA SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX6 565 - New York, NY
February 10, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sin Yen Ling, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael McFarland
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B)
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(U)
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
AMENDED ORDER
 
This matter was previously before the Board on August 17, 2015, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s April 21, 2015, order of removal. The respondent now moves to reopen proceedings alleging ineffective assistance by her prior counsel. The Department of Homeland Security opposes reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
The record reflects that the respondent was convicted on January 30, 2014, of conspiracy in the fifth degree, in violation of New York Penal Law § 105.05, for which she received a sentence of one year (Exh. 2, Tab B). The record further reflects that on January 30, 2014, the respondent was convicted of attempted criminal possession of a controlled substance in the third degree, in violation of New York Penal Law § 110-220.16(1), for which she received a sentence of 18 months (Exh. 2, Tab B). On April 21, 2015, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States based on her concession, through counsel, to the charges listed in the Notice to Appear, and her failure to apply for any relief from removal. The Board summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision on August 17, 2015, after the respondent did not raise any challenges to the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
The respondent now moves to reopen proceedings alleging that her former attorney rendered ineffective assistance in failing to challenge the removal charges or apply for any relief from removal. As an initial matter, we note that the respondent has complied with the evidentiary requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).1 However, the respondent has not established that she was prejudiced by her former immigration counsel’s actions. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556-57 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 Although the respondent states that she would be eligible for relief from removal if her conviction was determined to not be not an aggravated felony, she provides no specific authority or argument that her conviction under New York Penal Law § 110-220.16(1), which involved possession with intent to sell narcotics, is not an aggravated felony. We also find that the respondent has not presented a prima facie case for relief to support a reopening of proceedings. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (the Board may deny a motion to reopen based upon a failure to establish a prima facie case for the relief sought).
 
As an aggravated felon, the respondent is ineligible for asylum or cancellation of removal. See sections 208(b)(2)(A)(ii), 208(b)(2)(B)(i), 240A(a)(3) of the Act. An aggravated felony involving unlawful trafficking in controlled substances is presumptively a particularly serious crime rendering an alien ineligible for withholding of removal. See section 241(b)(3)(B)(ii) of the Act, Matter of Y-L-, A-G-, R-S-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 270, 273-77 (A.G. 2002) (aggravated felonies involving unlawful trafficking in controlled substances are presumptively particularly serious crimes). The respondent has not put forth any argument to rebut application of the presumption in this case.
 
As for protection under the Convention Against Torture (CAT), we do not find that the respondent has established a prima facie case for protection to support her motion to reopen. The respondent indicates that she fears two men who were involved in the trafficking of drugs with her and her husband. However, she makes no claim that these men have ties to the government in Ecuador, nor has she provided any evidence or argument to establish that the Ecuadorian government would acquiesce with or be willfully blind to any torture perpetrated against the respondent. 8 C.F.R. § 1208.18(a)(1) (torture under the CAT must be “inflicted by or at the instigation of or with the consent or acquiescence of a public official or other person acting in an official capacity”).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TENISINI TAUFALELE A.K.A. HALATOA FAKATOUMAFI A.K.A. HALATOA MOANA FAKUTOUMAFI
File: AXXX XX3 398 - West Valley City, UT
February 5, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kimberly J. Trupiano, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law (conceded)
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; voluntary departure; remand
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s December 16, 2014, decision, denying his application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), for failure to establish prima facie eligibility, and denying his alternative application for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), based on insufficient evidence of good moral character. During the pendency of this appeal, the respondent filed a motion, styled as a motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel and requesting remand of the record. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded. The record will be remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issued de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The following facts are not in dispute. The respondent, a native and citizen of Tonga, entered the United States as a nonimmigrant visitor and remained longer than authorized (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 4; Exh. 1). He is the beneficiary of an approved Alien Relative Petition (Form I-130) filed on his behalf by his United States citizen wife (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 4). At a hearing held in 2013, the respondent indicated his intent to apply for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(a) of the Act based on the approved visa petition (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 11). The Immigration Judge scheduled a final hearing on the respondent’s adjustment application for September 15, 2014 (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 14-15, 17).
 
At the respondent’s September 15, 2014, hearing, his then-counsel requested a continuance because the adjustment application was incomplete: the fingerprints and medical examination had expired, and the affidavit of support for the sponsor and joint sponsor were deficient (I.J. at 3: Tr. at 17-20). See sections 212(a)(1), (2), and (4) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1152(a)(1), (2), (4) (outlining health-related, criminal, and public charge grounds of inadmissibility). Over the DHS’s objection, the Immigration Judge continued the respondent’s case until December 16, 2014 (I.J. at 4; Tr. 19, 21). However, at the December 2014 hearing, problems persisted with the sufficiency of the affidavits of support (I.J. at 4; Tr. at 23-27). The Immigration Judge declined to further continue proceedings and instead concluded that the respondent had not established his statutory eligibility for adjustment of status (I.J. at 4; Tr. at 27). The Immigration Judge also denied the respondent’s alternate request for voluntary departure on good moral character grounds (I.J. at 8). See section 240B(b)(l)(B) of the Act; see also section 101(f) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(f) (defining good moral character).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent alleges that his inability to demonstrate prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status and voluntary departure was due to the ineffective assistance of his prior attorney (Resp. Brief at 2; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 2-5). This is essentially a request for remand, with requirements similar to those for reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-73 (BIA 1992). Generally, such a motion will not be granted unless it states new and material facts, is supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material, and establishes a prima facie case of statutory and discretionary eligibility for the underlying relief sought. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3); see also INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); Maatougui v. Holder, 738 F.3d 1230, 1240-41 (10th Cir. 2013) (confirming that a motion to reopen should be supported by new evidence establishing a prima facie claim to relief); Osei v. INS, 305 F.3d 1205, 1208-09 (10th Cir. 2002); Matter of Coelho, supra.
 
In support of remand, the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements articulated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Osei v. INS, supra, at 1209 n.2. The respondent filed a state bar complaint against his former attorney, advised the former attorney of the allegations against him, and submitted a motion supported by an affidavit detailing how the attorney’s performance was deficient and impacted the respondent’s case.
 
Specifically, the respondent alleges that his former counsel did not prepare him for the hearing, did not request that his wife file her taxes, improperly recorded information on the affidavit of support, and did not ensure that the joint sponsor’s affidavit was duly executed or supported by proof of his United States citizenship, resulting in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent could not overcome the public charge ground in qualifying for adjustment of status (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 7-8, Tabs 41-46; Tr. at 17-18, 20, 24-27). See section 212(a)(4) of the Act; section 213A of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1183a (outlining the statutory requirements for the affidavit of support); 8 C.F.R. §§ 213a.2(a)(l)(i)(A), (ii), (v), (b)(1), (c)(l)(i)(C), (c)(2), (ii) (for the various requirements related to proper execution of the affidavit of support, who may file the affidavit of support, and the evidence required to demonstrate sufficient income in support of the affidavit). With his motion, the respondent also included a letter from an accountant stating that the accountant is working with the respondent’s wife to file her back taxes (Resp. Motion to Reopen at Tab 33). The respondent also alleges he was prejudiced when his attorney did not prepare him for questioning about his 2010 arrest, and that numerous family members would have testified on his behalf as to his good moral character if they had been asked to do so-- factors relevant to the Immigration Judge’s denial of voluntary departure for lack of good moral character (Resp. Motion to Reopen at Tabs 5, 6, 7, 10, 12, 14, 16, 18, 20, 22; I.J. at 6-8; Tr. at 27-51). See section 240B(b)(l)(B) of the Act.
 
*3 We find that remand is warranted in the interests of justice. The respondent has demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s lack of preparation and communication with the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.102(o), (r) (requiring an attorney before the Immigration Court to adequately prepare for all proceedings and maintain communication with the respondent); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640; see also Ochieng v. Mukasey, 520 F.3d 1110, 1115 (10th Cir. 2008) (requiring the respondent to demonstrate prejudice in order to establish ineffective assistance of counsel warranting remand). Here, for instance, the respondent’s prior counsel: (1) did not adequately inform him of the documents required to present a sufficient affidavit of support even after certain deficiencies were identified by the Immigration Judge; (2) did not prepare him for questions he might be asked about his criminal history in qualifying for voluntary departure; and (3) openly acknowledged the affidavit of support was deficient (Tr. at 17-21, 25-27; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 31-46). Moreover, prior counsel proffered no persuasive evidence to rebut the ineffective assistance claim, even though the attorney was notified of the respondent’s allegations. Many of the identified errors in prior counsel’s performance have been rectified by current counsel. Notwithstanding the foregoing, we express no opinion as to the ultimate merits of the respondent’s applications for relief. In light of our disposition of this matter, we decline to address the respondent’s assertions on appeal that the record, as it was constituted before the Immigration Judge, supported a grant of adjustment of status or voluntary departure.
 
Upon remand, the parties will have an opportunity to supplement the record with any documentation or testimony they may choose to present relevant to the respondent’s applications for relief. However, we remind the respondent and his counsel that he is responsible for presenting a complete application for relief supported by the requisite documentary evidence. Failure to follow this instruction and any directives issued by the Immigration Judge may result in a finding that he is statutorily ineligible for any requested forms of relief. See, e.g., Matter of Interiano-Rosa, 25 I&N Dec. 264, 266 (BIA 2010).
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER; The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*4 Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 807204 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LIVINGSTON TONEY
File: AXXX XX5 212 - Los Angeles, CA
February 4, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaime Jasso, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s June 27, 2014, decision ordering him removed from the United States. The respondent requests that the matter be remanded to the Immigration Judge to allow the record to be supplemented with missing transcripts. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge found that the respondent, a native and citizen of Trinidad and Tobago, who became a lawful permanent resident of the United States in 1981, was properly charged as an arriving alien because he departed the United States in 2005, and did not return until 2008, thus abandoning his residency (I.J. at 2-3; Exh. 7). Accordingly, the Immigration Judge sustained the charged of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), and ordered the respondent removed to Trinidad and Tobago. The Immigration Judge did not make a determination as the respondent’s inadmissibility under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act (I.J. at 3).
 
The respondent does not challenge the finding of removability on appeal, but rather argues that he has been deprived of due process in his immigration proceedings because the transcripts of record are missing hearings from the first 2 years of his proceedings (Resp. Br. at 1-4). He argues that, as a result, he has been prevented from fully challenging his order of removal, and establishing his ineffective assistance of counsel claim (Resp. Br. at 1-4, 6-8, 11-12).
 
As the respondent correctly points out on appeal, missing transcripts are not a per se violation of due process (Resp. Br. at 9-10). United States v. Medina, 236 F.3d 1028, 1031-32 (9th Cir. 2001) (explaining, in an illegal reentry case, that missing immigration transcripts did not violate due process in immigration proceedings). In order to establish that a violation of due process has occurred, the respondent must still establish that the missing transcripts rendered his proceedings fundamentally unfair and that he was prejudiced as a result of that unfairness. Id.; see also Dent v. Holder, 627 F.3d 365, 373 (9th Cir. 2010); Colmenar v. INS, 210 F.3d 967, 971 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 We are not persuaded that the respondent has been prevented from reasonably presenting his case on appeal in such a way that rendered his proceedings fundamentally unfair. While the absence of a transcript of proceedings surely affects the nature of appellate review, it does not completely deprive the alien of all means of challenging the errors below. United States v. Medina, supra, at 1032. The respondent could have explained the presence of errors below through his memory, other evidence in the record, or witness testimony. See id. The respondent has not attempted to do so here, and thus he has not shown that he was prevented from fully presenting his case. See id.
 
Furthermore, even if he had established a fundamental unfairness in the proceedings, he must still establish that he suffered prejudice as a result. In the context of missing transcripts, an alien must do more than offer a “vague assertion that, if a tape recording was available, he might be able to locate some defect in the proceeding.” Id. Here, rather than asserting that ineffective assistance of counsel had occurred, the respondent asserts that he believes he may have suffered ineffective assistance of counsel, but that he needs the missing transcripts to determine the nature of his claim (Notice of Appeal, Resp. Br. at 11-12). Without more of an assertion from the respondent about how he believes he was deficiently represented, we conclude that his claims on appeal amount to no more than speculation as to his plausible grounds for relief, which is insufficient to show prejudice. Id.; see also United States v. Corrales-Beltran, 192 F.3d 1311, 1318-19 (9th Cir. 1999); United States v. Alvarado-Delgado, 98 F.3d 492, 493 (9th Cir. 1996).
 
This is particularly true where, contrary to the respondent’s assertions on appeal, the respondent need not strictly comply with the filing and reporting requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), if ineffective assistance of counsel is plain on the face of the record (Resp. Br. at 11-12). See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 597-98 (9th Cir. 2004). The respondent does not assert in any meaningful way that ineffective assistance was plain on the face of the record; nor does he allege what he believes the missing transcripts would prove, if they were produced. Accordingly, the respondent has not established that the missing transcripts prevented him from establishing his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
We also disagree with the respondent that he was prejudiced because the record contains no evidence of the charges against him or his plea to those charges. The Notice to Appear is contained in the record at Exh. 1. Further, while it appears that the respondent did enter earlier pleadings through his attorney, that were contained in the missing transcripts, he also submitted subsequent written pleadings (Exh. 16). The Immigration Judge acknowledged and relied on those written pleadings in rendering his decision (Exh. 16; Tr. at 17; IJ at 2). The respondent does not allege any error of his prior counsel in submitting these pleadings or explain why these are insufficient to establish the charges and his pleadings. Accordingly, the respondent has not established a due process violation that requires remand of this matter. The following orders will be entered.
  
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
*3 FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 807204 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 807232 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUILLERMO LOPEZ A.K.A. GUILLERMO LOPEZ SANCHEZ A.K.A. MEMO LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX6 343 - Dallas, TX
January 29, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jered Dobbs, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 In a decision rendered March 9, 2015, the Immigration Judge denied a joint motion to reopen and terminate proceedings without prejudice filed by the respondent and the Department of Homeland Security (the DHS) on August 8, 2014. The respondent timely appealed that decision. The appeal will be sustained and the parties’ joint motion to terminate will be granted.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “““clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgement, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, entered the United States as a nonimmigrant visitor in 2001 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). The respondent married a United States citizen and adjusted his status to that of a conditional permanent resident in 2005 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). The respondent did not timely apply to remove the conditions on his residency; his joint petition to remove the conditions was filed approximately two years after the relevant deadline (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1; Joint Motion, Tab C). United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) denied the petition because it determined that there was not good cause for the late filing, and the DHS commenced these removal proceedings through issuance of a Notice to Appear, charging the respondent as removable because his conditional permanent resident status had been terminated (Exh. 1). Before an Immigration Judge, the respondent conceded his removability and sought to renew his joint petition to remove the conditions on his residency (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge set a date for the respondent to file the petition with the Immigration Court, accompanied by proof that the requisite filing fee had been paid (I.J. at 2). He did not file the petition; on February 15, 2012, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed (I.J. at 2; Joint Motion, Tab F).
 
In August 2014, the respondent and the DHS filed a joint motion to reopen and terminate proceedings before the Immigration Court, so the respondent could pursue adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255, before USCIS (I.J. at 2). Along with the motion, the respondent submitted: (1) evidence that his wife, through whom he originally obtained his conditional resident status, had filed a new visa petition on his behalf; (2) voluminous documentation of their eight year marriage; (3) a note from his former lawyer suggesting the prior attorney was responsible for the failure to file the petition to remove conditions before the Immigration Judge; and (4) his statement (Joint Motion, Tabs A, G, I, J).
 
*2 The Immigration Judge, observing the motion was not time or number barred because it was jointly filed, denied the motion because the respondent had not submitted proof that the visa petition had been approved, and thus the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent could not establish prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act (I.J. at 3-4). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iv). To the extent that the respondent’s former lawyer was at fault, the Immigration Judge found the respondent had not complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), in making a formal claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 4).
 
We disagree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that reopening and termination without prejudice is not warranted in this case (I.J. at 3-4). Notably, an agreement between the respondent and the DHS on an issue regarding the proper course of action “should, in most instances, be determinative.” See Matter of Yewondwosen, 21 I&N Dec. 1025, 1026 (BIA 1997). Here, the Immigration Judge concluded that the joint motion of the parties could not overcome the respondent’s failure to demonstrate prima facie adjustment eligibility because he did not have an approved visa petition (I.J. at 4 n.1). However, the standard for reopening is met when “the evidence reveals a reasonable likelihood that the statutory requirements for relief have been satisfied. . . . We have not required a conclusive showing that eligibility for relief has been established.” Matter of S-V-, 22 I&N Dec. 1306 (BIA 2000) (citing Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413, 419 (BIA 1996) (emphasis added). In addition, contrary to the Immigration Judge’s assertion that an approved visa petition is a necessary prerequisite for reopening, we have specifically found reopening to be appropriate where a pending visa petition is supported by evidence establishing a sufficient likelihood that the visa petition will be approved. See, e.g., Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253, 256 (BIA 2002). In this case, there is a reasonable likelihood of the visa petition being approved, given the respondent remains married to the same spouse through whom he obtained his conditional permanent residence nearly 10 years ago. Matter of S-V-, supra.
 
Therefore, given the joint motion and the respondent’s evidence that the visa petition is likely to be granted, we will reopen these proceedings and terminate them without prejudice pursuant to the parties’ request. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
*3 FURTHER ORDER: The parties’ joint motion to reopen is granted, and the instant proceedings are reopened and terminated without prejudice at the joint request of the parties.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 807232 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: JAFET E. GARCIA-DIAZ
File: AXXX XX5 519 - Miami, FL
January 25, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ernest Bradley Westerhold, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael A. Mansfield
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, has appealed the August 15, 2017, decision of the Immigration Judge finding him removable as charged and ineligible for relief from removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has also filed a timely motion to remand the record so that he may present a motion to terminate before the Immigration Judge. The DHS has not responded to this motion.
 
The respondent’s motion is supported by evidence that the 11th Circuit Judicial Court in and for Miami-Dade County, Florida, vacated the respondent’s nolo contendere plea underlying his conviction pursuant to Florida Statute 827.03(2), based on due process violations regarding ineffective assistance of counsel. Given the evidence presented, we find that the respondent’s vacated conviction may not be considered a conviction for immigration purposes. Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010); Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006); Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003).
 
Given that the respondent’s conviction served as the sole basis for his removability, we find no need to remand the record to the Immigration Court, and we hereby terminate proceedings. The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s proceedings are terminated.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 807250 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EVERLINE GESARE NYABWARI
File: AXXX XX3 208 - Dallas, TX
January 22, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Kenya. The Board dismissed his appeal on September 26, 2013, and thereafter denied several motions. On November 13, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel of former counsel. The motion is denied.
 
The pending motion is untimely filed, as well as number-barred, and no exception to these restrictions placed by law on motions to reopen removal proceedings has been shown to be applicable here. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Further, we find no basis to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
We decline to equitably toll the time and number bar because the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He argues that former counsel failed to continue to object to the admissibility of a Form I-9, and that this was ineffective assistance. We disagree. It is settled that a Form I-9 is admissible in immigration proceedings to support charges of removability against an alien and to determine his or her eligibility for relief from removal. Matter of Bett, 26 I. & N. Dec. 437 (BIA 2014).
 
Furthermore, it has been over 2 years since the appeal was dismissed, and the respondent has not adequately explained why he delayed in raising his current claims. See Rodriquez-Manzano, 666 F.3d 948, 955 n.7 (5th Cir. 2012) (regarding ineffective assistance, “current regulations impose what is effectively a due diligence requirement on post-1992 cases...”). There is, therefore, no basis for abrogating the motions deadline. Accordingly, the motion is denied. The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 807250 (BIA)

2016 WL 807268 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEREMEA ISY NOWELL A.K.A. SEREMEA NOWELL
File: AXXX XX4 062 - Honolulu, HI1
January 19, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pamela Brown Blackburn, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Chandu Latey
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Federated States of Micronesia, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s January 13, 2015, decision, denying his request for voluntary departure. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. At the outset, we find that the respondent does not meaningfully contest the Immigration Judge’s legal conclusion that he is statutorily ineligible to apply for asylum and withholding of removal under sections 208 and 241(b)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1158, 1231(b)(3), and protection under the Convention Against Torture (CAT) (Notice of Appeal at 2; Respondent’s Brief).2 The respondent likewise does not meaningfully contest the Immigration Judge’s denial of voluntary departure or his request for a continuance. Therefore, we deem those issues waived. See, e.g., Matter of Cervantes, 22 I&N Dec. 560, 561 n.1 (BIA 1999) (expressly declining to address an issue not raised by party on appeal); Matter of Gutierrez, 19 I&N Dec. 562, 565 n.3 (BIA 1988) (same).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion in finding him removable. The respondent claims that the guilty plea which he entered in connection with his 2002 conviction for attempted rape in the Commonwealth of the Northern Mariana Islands (CNMI) Superior Court, in violation of 6 CMC sections 1301 and 301, was not knowingly given because his criminal attorney failed to advise him of the immigration consequences of his conviction (Respondent’s Br. at 3). The respondent represents on appeal that after his hearing, he filed a Petition for a Writ of Coram Nobis in CNMI on May 20, 2015, which is still pending.
 
We dismiss this contention. The respondent did not challenge the use of his 2002 criminal conviction in these proceedings. The respondent, in fact, admitted to the allegation in the Notice to Appear (NTA) concerning this conviction and conceded his removability on both grounds lodged against him, including for having been convicted (or having committed) a crime involving moral turpitude (Tr. at 20, 25, 35-36), See Notice to Appear at 3. See also section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I). Accordingly, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination of removability based on the record. We have held, moreover, that an alien waives his opportunity to pursue an issue on appeal when he fails to raise it before the Immigration Judge at his hearing. See Matter of R-S-H, 23 I&N Dec. 629, 638 (BIA 2003). Since the respondent raised no objection to the use of his 2002 conviction in these proceedings, he waived this issue for review on appeal.
 
*2 Moreover, the respondent’s claim that his conviction may not be used to determine his removability is without merit because while an alien is free to file a motion to vacate his criminal conviction before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010),3 the filing of a post-conviction motion does not render the conviction non-final for immigration purposes. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323 (BIA 1996); Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
Finally, we understand the respondent’s fear of being removed and thus, being unable to obtain the necessary medical care he needs for his serious health issues (Respondent’s Brief). However, the Board and the Immigration Judges have limited jurisdiction and can grant only those forms of relief from removal that are expressly authorized by Congress. See Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 1988); Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348 (BIA 1982). We have no power to grant equitable remedies or to confer general humanitarian relief on aliens. If the respondent wishes to pursue relief on humanitarian grounds, he is not precluded from seeking additional consideration from the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). For the foregoing reasons, the decision below is affirmed, and we find that the respondent has not established that these proceedings were fundamentally unfair to remand the record for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Proceedings before the Immigration Judge in this matter were completed in Saipan, Commonwealth of the Northern Mariana Islands (CNMI), where the case was docketed for hearing (see OPPM No. 04-06) and where the hearing notice directed the respondent to appear via video teleconference pursuant to section 240(b)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act. The Immigration Judge conducted the hearing remotely from Honolulu, Hawaii.

2

Even if we to address these issues, the respondent did not express any fear of persecution or torture to qualify for relief or protection (I.J. at 2: Tr. at 37). The only fear expressed by the respondent related to his fear that if removed, he would be unable to obtain the medical treatment which he needs.

3

In Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010), the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel.

2016 WL 807274 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CESAR LEYVA-HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX1 514 - Tucson, AZ
January 15, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ginger E. Jacobs, Esquire
*1 The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal on March 26, 2014. We found that the Immigration Judge properly denied his request for a continuance to await adjudication of a U visa application, which is for certain victims of physical or mental abuse. Section 101(a)(15)(U) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(15)(U). We found no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent had not demonstrated that he was the victim of a qualifying crime, that the respondent had not demonstrated that he had experienced substantial physical or mental abuse as the result of being a victim of a crime, and that the respondent had not shown prima facie eligibility for a U visa as he had not obtained a law enforcement certification, and the U visa application had not been filed with the DHS. The respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen on October 9, 2015, and later made a supplemental filing. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion to reopen, to which the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded, will be denied.
 
According to the motion, the respondent was removed to Mexico on April 24, 2015, and currently resides there (Respondent’s Mot. at 6, 9). Even if the motion were to be deemed timely filed, and within the Board’s jurisdiction notwithstanding the respondent’s removal, see Toor v. Lynch, 789 F.3d 1055 (9th Cir. 2015), the motion would be denied.
 
The respondent claims that former counsel Patricia G. Mejia was ineffective because she did not file a timely petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, concerning our March 26, 2014, decision (Respondent’s Mot. at 9-11; Exh. D).1 In Dearinger ex rel. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042 (9th Cir. 2000), the Ninth Circuit found that where an alien is prevented from filing an appeal in immigration proceedings due to counsel’s error, and is thereby deprived of appellate proceedings entirely, such error mandates a presumption of prejudice inasmuch as the adversary process itself has been rendered presumptively unreliable. See Salazar-Gonzalez v. Lynch, 798 F.3d 917, 921-22 (9th Cir. 2015). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009)(the Board has discretion, pending rulemaking, “““to consider claims of ineffective assistance of counsel based on conduct of counsel that occurred after a final order of removal had been entered”). While the failure to file a timely petition for review with the Ninth Circuit raises a presumption of prejudice, in this case that presumption has been rebutted. The respondent does not show how the Board’s March 26, 2014, decision (which, as noted, affirmed an Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of a continuance pending adjudication of a U visa) would have been subject to challenge before the Ninth Circuit.
 
*2 The respondent conversely argues that counsel did not inform him that filing a Ninth Circuit petition for review would revoke the grant of voluntary departure, under section 240B(b) of the Act (Respondent’s Mot. at 12; Respondent’s Affidavit). However, the respondent understands English, and this was explained in the Board’s March 26, 2014, decision (Tr. at 1, 5, 13).2
 
The motion also argues that former counsel provided ineffective assistance, by not requesting a favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion from the DHS (Respondent’s Mot. at 12-13). However, an application for prosecutorial discretion is a collateral matter involving the DHS’ independent exercise of discretion, and is outside the jurisdiction of the Immigration Court or Board, see, e.g., Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998); Matter of Singh, 21 I&N Dec. 427, 435 (BIA 1996). Therefore, this claimed deficiency in representation is insufficient to demonstrate ineffective assistance in the proceedings before the Immigration Court or Board.
 
The motion additionally argues that former counsel was ineffective, because she did not pursue a stay of removal with the DHS, after the Ninth Circuit court dismissed his appeal as untimely (Respondent’s Mot. at 14). However, this alleged ineffective assistance of counsel is beyond the scope and jurisdiction of the removal proceedings, as it allegedly occurred after the completion of the removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge and Board.
 
As the respondent has not shown that reopening is warranted in this matter, the pending motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

Former counsel, while stating that the respondent would proceed before the Ninth Circuit pro se, admitted that the petition for review was not timely filed. Mejia Resp. at 7, 13.

2

With the supplemental filing, the respondent presents a letter from former counsel. She states that the respondent speaks English, and she discussed the Board’s decision, including its warning concerning voluntary departure, with the respondent. Mejia Resp. at 5.

2015 WL 10090670 (BIA)

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEVY SAUL SAMAYOA-MONTUFAR
File: AXXX XX2 588 - E1 Paso, TX
December 23, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Peter E. Torres, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Claudia Ochoa
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative decision on August 28, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision denying a previous motion to reopen these proceedings in which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia on May 16, 2000. The respondent again seeks reopening. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied as untimely and number-barred, as the respondent filed it beyond the 180-day exceptional circumstances deadline, and has not demonstrated that an exception to the time and number limits applies. See Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (c)(7)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) and (c)(7)(C).
 
The respondent urges equitably tolling of the time and number limits for motions to reopen, because he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. In particular, the respondent avers that he did not attend his hearing, because prior counsel did not mail the notice of hearing to the respondent’s address. See generally Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 219-20 (5th Cir. 2008) (in the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim is construed as a challenge to the Board’s discretionary sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings); United States v. English, 400 F.3d 273, 275 (5th Cir. 2005) (recognizing that equitable tolling should only apply in “rare and exceptional circumstances”).
 
We are not persuaded of any material error with counsel’s purported typographical error in the respondent’s mailing address. Nor has the respondent demonstrated the requisite due diligence in raising ineffective assistance, where he waited 14 years to present the claim. See De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 883 (5th Cir.2004) (observing that prevailing on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim requires substantial prejudice). Notably, prior counsel filed a motion to withdraw from representing the respondent, because the respondent was uncooperative, did not maintain contact, and did not fulfill his contractual agreement. See Motion to Withdraw as Counsel, dated May 16, 2000. Under the circumstances, the respondent has not shown deficient representation of prior counsel or diligent pursuit of the claim.
 
Nor has the respondent demonstrated exceptional circumstances warranting sua sponte reopening. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 10090670 (BIA)

2015 WL 10090672 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ERBIBERTO JIMENEZ-ANGUIANO
File: AXXX XX5 051 - Kenosha, WI1
December 22, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Isabel Martinez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anastasie M. Sénat
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated September 21, 2015, denying a requested continuance and ordering his removal from the United States. The respondent’s appeal, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent does not contest that he is, at the present time, subject to removal from the United States as charged on the Notice to Appear under the provisions of sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) and (a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(A)(2)(a)(I)(i) and (a)(6)(A)(i). I.J. at 3; Exh. 1. He also does not contend that, at the present time, he has requested any form of relief from removal which can be granted to him in these proceedings. The issue raised on appeal is whether the Immigration Judge should have granted a requested further continuance.
 
The respondent’s desire to pursue an appeal of his conviction or post-conviction relief with the Indiana courts, with the desired result of vacating his conviction, rendering him potentially eligible for discretionary relief, does not amount to “good cause” warranting a continuance of these removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. We note that the respondent was given several continuances, for attorney preparation time and for time to file for post-conviction relief in the Indiana court in which he was convicted. Tr. at 6, 13, 19, 30. While the respondent has described the basis on which he is pursuing post-conviction relief, to include a detailed assessment of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel in criminal proceedings in state court, his pursuit of post-conviction relief remains speculative. I.J. at 4-5. As such, we conclude that the respondent’s post-conviction relief claims do not warrant a continuance of these proceedings. See Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that an alien should not be granted a continuance “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”); see also Pawlowski v. Holder, 482 F.App’x. 162, 165 (7th Cir. 2012) (holding that it is permissible to decline to grant a continuance of removal proceedings based upon an alien’s unsubstantiated speculation that he might receive post-conviction relief).
 
*2 If the respondent believes that he should be permitted to remain in this country while he exhausts his post-conviction remedies, he is not precluded from requesting a stay of removal from the DHS. See 8 C.F.R. § 241.6. However, as the respondent’s arguments on appeal do not persuade this Board to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to enter an order of removal in this case, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge in this matter were completed at a detention facility in Kenosha, Wisconsin (see OPPM No. 04-06). The Immigration Judge conducted the hearings there remotely from the Immigration Court in Chicago, Illinois, via video teleconference pursuant to section 240(b)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act.

2015 WL 10090681 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ERNESTO TIRADO FLORES
File: AXXX XX8 399 - Houston, TX
December 21, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Gamez, Esquire
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. He has appealed a decision, mailed on August 6, 2015, in which an Immigration Judge denied his motion to reopen. The respondent has filed a brief concerning the decision of the Immigration Judge, while the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
The respondent was found subject to removal as an aggravated felon, based on a 2004 Texas conviction for burglary of a habitation. He was ordered removed on September 15, 2005, and was removed the following day. He was again removed from the United States on January 3, 2014.
 
The respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on June 8, 2015. Section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b); I.J. at 2. He argued that his burglary conviction had been vacated on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.1
 
The respondent sought to have the Immigration Judge reopen his case under the judge’s sua sponte authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). However, the Immigration Judge correctly found that he lacked jurisdiction to reopen the proceedings sua sponte, due to the respondent’s removal. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); I.J. at 2. The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has held that 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d), relating to motions before the Board, is invalid because it contradicts statutory language permitting one motion to reopen removal proceedings with no restriction on the location from which it is filed. Garcia-Carias v. Holder, 697 F.3d 257 (5th Cir. 2012). The respondent’s motion, filed many years late, was not filed pursuant to the statute, but rather sought to have the Immigration Judge reopen these removal proceedings sua sponte. Navarro-Miranda v. Ashcroft, 330 F.3d 672, 675 (5th Cir. 2003) (Board acted reasonably in determining that the departure regulation deprived it of its regulatory power to reopen or reconsider decisions sua sponte); Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288, 292-93 (5th Cir. 2009).
 
*2 The appeal will, therefore, be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge correctly found that the respondent did not present evidence which would show why the criminal court dismissed the respondent’s conviction on February 5, 2015. I.J. at 1-2. The respondent attached to his brief the criminal court judge’s memorandum, which states that criminal counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel.

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AMADO ALFONSO SANDOVAL-LINARES

December 15, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alary E. Piibe, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(C), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(C)] - Controlled substance trafficker
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated May 8, 2014, denying his motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against him on May 12, 1999. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including any determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent received proper notice of his removal hearing because he was personally served with the Notice to Appear along with notice of the time, date, and location of the hearing. Section 239(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a). With respect to his claim of exceptional circumstances, the respondent’s motion is untimely because he did not file his motion within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s decision. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge properly declined to equitably toll the applicable filing requirements based on the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The doctrine of equitable tolling applies when “despite all due diligence, [the party invoking equitable tolling] is unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of the claim.” Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001). Here, the respondent had been put on notice that he was in removal proceedings and was scheduled to appear for a hearing on May 12, 1999, when he was personally served with the Notice to Appear, along with notice of the time and date of the hearing, on April 25, 1999. The respondent’s claim that he spoke with an attorney on the telephone after he returned to the United States in 2000 (which would have been more than 180 days after the Immigration Judge’s May 12, 1999, decision) does not establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent bears the burden of establishing a basis for rescinding and reopening, and he has not provided sufficient details to make his claim persuasive. Nor did the respondent show substantial compliance with the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Finally, the respondent did not show that he acted with the due diligence required for equitable tolling where his motion was filed 14 years after the filing deadline had passed. It was unreasonable for the respondent to take no steps to reopen his case for well over a decade based on the statement of an attorney who apparently declined to represent him. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir 2003) (person seeking to toll period for filing motion to reopen must show he has acted with due diligence).
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has not established an “exceptional” situation sufficient to warrant sua sponte reopening of the proceedings. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent acknowledges in his motion that a Form I-213 (Exh. 2), detailing the basis for the removal charge, had been admitted into evidence at the 1999 hearing (Respondent’s motion at 4-5; I.J. at 2). Moreover, the respondent’s arguments regarding the sufficiency of the Immigration Judge’s decision could have been made years ago. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed
 
Joan B Geller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 9809849 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER ORTEGA-SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX3 501 - Oakdale, LA
December 14, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Drayton D. Berkley, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorraine L. Griffin
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent, who is a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s August 31, 2015, decision denying his motion to reopen these removal proceedings. The respondent filed a brief concerning the decision of the Immigration Judge, and the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) filed a “Motion for Summary Affirmance”.1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
On March 3, 2009, the respondent was charged with being subject to removal under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i), in that he arrived in the United States at some point in 2003 or 2004, and was not then admitted or paroled after inspection by an immigration officer. An Immigration Judge issued an order of removal on April 22, 2009, which stated that the respondent had made no application for relief from removal. The respondent waived the right to appeal to the Board.
 
Six years later, on August 5, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. The respondent brought a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Timothy Flowers, an attorney who has been suspended from practice by this Board. The respondent in an affidavit claimed that Flowers did not return his phone calls, never came to see him in detention, and did not appear in Immigration Court.
 
At the April 22, 2009, removal hearing, the respondent stated in his affidavit, he appeared in Immigration Court without Flowers and “consented to removal”. Absent Flowers’ inaction, the respondent claimed, he might have been able to have the removal proceedings “cancelled”. According to his motion, Flowers erred by not filing an application to cancel removal, apparently pursuant to section 240A(b) of the Act, due to extreme hardship that would occur to the respondent’s family upon his removal (Respondent’s Mot. at paras. 13, 26-27).
 
The Immigration Judge in denying reopening correctly found that the motion was untimely filed. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1)(motion must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order). The Immigration Judge also properly concluded that the respondent did not establish exceptional circumstances which would warrant sua sponte reopening under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b) (I.J. at 2). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). That is, at the April 22, 2009, removal hearing, the respondent was given an opportunity to continue the hearing until counsel was available, but elected to proceed without representation (I.J. at 3). Thus, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was without merit. Id.
 
*2 The Immigration Judge also properly concluded, in denying reopening, that the respondent’s motion to reopen did not establish eligibility for relief from removal. Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247, 251 (BIA 2007). That is, the motion to reopen filed with the Immigration Judge indicated that the respondent wished to seek cancellation of removal for nonpermanent residents under section 240A(b) of the Act, but the respondent did not establish the required 10 years of continuous physical presence in the United States. Sections 240A(b)(1)(A) and 240A(d)(1) of the Act.2 The respondent does not argue otherwise in his brief.
 
As for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, the respondent did not argue in his motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge that this relief should have been granted by the Immigration Judge in 2009. While the respondent argues in his brief that he should have been advised about the possibility of seeking voluntary departure, he does not set out how he would have established eligibility for such relief before the Immigration Judge at his April 22, 2009, removal hearing.
 
The respondent claims that the Immigration Judge did not consider how the respondent might be granted “a waiver of [in]admissibility for humanitarian or family purposes.” With regard to humanitarian relief, however, the Immigration Judge and Board have limited jurisdiction and can grant only those forms of relief from removal that are expressly authorized by Congress. Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 1988). If the respondent seeks a favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion, he must direct his request to the DHS.
 
The respondent’s brief contends that he filed a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge on September 29, 2015, and that the Immigration Judge has failed to consider it. Such motion is attached to the respondent’s brief, although it is barely legible. As the respondent filed an appeal with this Board, jurisdiction vested with us, and the Immigration Judge committed no error in not addressing the motion to reconsider. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). We moreover find no basis for disturbing the decision of the Immigration Judge, after reviewing the motion to reconsider.
 
The appeal will, therefore, be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
David B. Holmes

MOTION

Stacy Tolchin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Elly Laff
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was before the Board on September 29, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent filed the present motion to reconsider our decision and to reopen proceedings on October 22, 2015, and filed supplemental materials that the Board received on November 4, 2015. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
In this matter, the Immigration Judge entered the removal order in 2006 and the respondent moved to reopen in the Immigration Court in 2015, approximately 9 years later. The Board affirmed the denial of reopening on grounds that the motion was untimely and that the respondent had not demonstrated an exceptional situation to warrant sua sponte reopening. In seeking reconsideration, the respondent argues that since there is no regulatory time limit for reopening sua sponte, his motion was denied for failure to meet a non-existent regulatory deadline. This is incorrect; sua sponte reopening was denied not for timeliness but for lack of an exceptional situation. The present motion does not identify any error of law or fact in our decision or argument that was overlooked. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); see also Matter of 0-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 58 (BIA 2006). The motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
The respondent also seeks reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board held in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), that due process rights may be violated, and the time and numerical limits for filing a motion to reopen may be tolled, when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from reasonably presenting his case. An alien seeking reopening for ineffective assistance must show that he acted with due diligence. See Iturribarria v. I.N.S., 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003); Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090, 1096-97 (9th Cir. 2007) (holding that alien failed to act with due diligence in waiting 6 months after adverse Board decision before consulting with another attorney). Equitable tolling of a filing deadline is a form of relief that is extended “only sparingly” even in situations where a claimant has “actively pursued” his remedies. Irwin v. Dep’t of Veterans Affairs, 498 U.S. 89, 96 (1990). Even if counsel’s performance was deficient (a question we do not decide), the respondent has not shown that he actively pursued his remedies; in particular, there is no satisfactory explanation for the delay of more than 1 year in moving to reopen after the respondent obtained post-conviction relief in a state criminal matter. The motion to reopen will therefore be denied as barred by the applicable statutory and regulatory time and number limits.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JOSE MAURICIO CORTEZ A.K.A. JOSE M CORTEZ
