IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey A. Devore, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Temporary Protected Status
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the May 22, 2013, decision of the Immigration Judge denying and deeming abandoned his application for Temporary Protected Status (TPS) and ordering him removed from the United States. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge for a new hearing. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
The record before the Board indicates that U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) denied the respondent’s application for TPS in 2004 for failure to document his eligibility. See Tr. at 20; Motion to Remand, Tabs A, B. The respondent through counsel filed a new TPS application with USCIS in 2012, even though he was then in removal proceedings. See Motion, Tabs O - Q. In the Immigration Court, the respondent submitted only the application form, without any supporting evidence or documentation. The Immigration Judge issued an order of removal, but also advised the respondent that he would grant a timely-filed motion to reopen with an application for TPS or cancellation of removal. See Tr. at 31-33. The respondent did not file a motion to reopen.
 
The respondent’s appeal brief and present motion to remand claim ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board held in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), that due process rights may be violated when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from reasonably presenting his case and prejudice results. In Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), we held that an alien must show prejudice resulting from the ineffective assistance and will not establish prejudice if he does not document that he is eligible for the relief sought. See also Figeroa v. I.N.S., 886 F.2d 76 (4th Cir. 1989) (holding that even in a clear case of ineffective assistance, the alien must establish prejudice by providing objective evidence that he is entitled to relief).
 
The brief and motion do not document that the respondent is eligible for TPS. The appeal brief makes a conclusory claim of eligibility and the motion addresses counsel’s performance,1 but nowhere does the respondent point to documents establishing his eligibility. Prejudice from ineffective assistance is not shown.
 
The respondent cites Matter of Interiano-Rosa, 25 I&N Dec. 264 (BIA 2010), in arguing that the TPS application should not have been denied or deemed abandoned for failure to file supporting evidence. In that case, the alien timely filed his application for relief and timely filed numerous supporting documents, although he failed to file some documents as directed by the Immigration Judge. The Board held on these facts that the application should be adjudicated on the basis of the testimony and the timely-filed documents. In the present matter, the respondent filed no supporting documents at all (I.J. at 1). The Immigration Judge was correct in holding that the respondent’s testimony alone could not have established his eligibility for TPS (id.), as TPS requires documentation of eligibility in all cases. Therefore, the Immigration Judge properly pretermitted the respondent’s unsupported application.
 
*2 For the above reasons, the Board will affirm the removal order and deny the motion to remand.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

Counsel is faulted, inter alia, for failing to file the respondent’s tax returns as ordered by the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 16), but the returns are not submitted with the present motion.

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 11, 2014, denying her motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously granted the respondent voluntary departure at a hearing held on January 18, 2006. We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
In her motion to reopen, the respondent asserts that she was misadvised by the accredited representative who requested only voluntary departure on her behalf at the January 18, 2006, hearing. The respondent further asserts that after the hearing she immediately retained new counsel to file a motion to reopen, but that attorney failed to file the motion and misled her regarding its status. In denying the respondent’s motion to reopen filed on July 3, 2014, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent was questioned regarding voluntary departure at her final removal hearing and was provided an opportunity to ask questions of her own. However, the Immigration Judge did not sufficiently address the ineffective assistance of counsel claims raised in the respondent’s motion to reopen. Under the particular circumstances of this case, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of these claims and the entry of a new decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (limiting the Board’s fact-finding ability on appeal); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen.

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Vacated by IN RE: GUSTAVO ADRIAN-CABALLERO, BIA, April 11, 2016

November 27, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 On January 26, 2015, the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s October 29, 2014, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal entered on August 27, 2002. On April 22, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and rescind with the Board. On July 21, 2015, the Board denied the motion, and on September 14, 2015 the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen and reconsider with the Board. The motion will be denied.
 
In his prior motion before the Board, the respondent offered an affidavit in which he stated that he never received any notices from any government office for his removal hearing. The Board found the respondent’s affidavit to be inconsistent with his affidavit submitted in support of his motion before the Immigration Judge in which he stated that he did not have any problems with the mail at his address of record.
 
In his prior motion before the Board, the respondent also set forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), against the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge and on appeal before the Board, Martha E. Garza, Esquire. The respondent asserted that he did not tell Attorney Garza that he did not have problems with the mail at his address of record; Attorney Garza prepared the affidavit in English; and Attorney Garza had him sign the affidavit without having read it to him in Spanish. In response to the allegations against her, Attorney Garza stated that in accordance with her office’s routine practice, the affidavit was read to the respondent in Spanish for corrections and signature. Attorney Garza also reminded the respondent that he met and consulted with a former associate of the firm, Raed Gonzalez.
 
Raed Gonzalez is the respondent’s current attorney. With the instant motion, Attorney Gonzalez has offered a notarized affidavit in which he states that he was not personally involved with the respondent’s case when he was employed in the office of Attorney Garza, and he attests that “it was never (Attorney Garza’s) routine policy nor routine practice at the office to consistently read affidavits back to non-English speaking clients in their native language.” Attorney Gonzalez further states that “[f]requently attorneys and legal assistants in (Attorney Garza’s) office simply would type the declaration and take the signature of the client without reading it back to them.”
 
Attorney Gonzalez implies that Attorney Garza did not respond truthfully regarding her handling of the respondent’s case. Although Attorney Gonzalez’s affidavit impugns Attorney Garza’s integrity, the respondent has not offered evidence that Attorney Garza has been notified of, and given a chance to respond to, the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in the instant motion, or that a proper complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 648 (5th Cir. 2014) (counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed to respond before allegations of ineffective assistance are presented to the Board); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639 (setting forth the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board). We conclude, therefore, that equitable tolling of the filing deadline based on the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim is not warranted in this case. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). Further, it has not been shown that an exceptional situation is present in this case to warrant reopening sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OMAR OSORIO-BATALLA
File: AXXX XX3 104 - Seattle, WA
November 27, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sandy Restrepo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ryan A. Kahler
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 19, 2014. The respondent has also moved for a remand. The motion will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under a clearly erroneous standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon our de novo review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion for a continuance to pursue a “U” visa as a crime victim. See section 101(a)(15)(U)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(15)(U)(i). While the Immigration Judge did not specifically reference our precedent decision in Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012), concerning the adjudication of such motions, we find that he nonetheless gave adequate consideration to relevant factors and properly ruled on the respondent’s motion.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.6; see also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). Relevant factors for consideration where a continuance is based upon a U visa outlined in Matter of Sanchez Sosa include whether there is government opposition to the motion, evidence of an underlying crime that may serve as a basis for a U visa, and evidence that the alien is prima facie eligible for a U visa. In this instance, the motion was opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and the respondent presented no evidence to show that the relevant visa petition (Form I-918) had been filed and was being processed, or that the respondent had obtained the necessary law enforcement certification to show that he qualified for the visa (Tr. at 9). See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14. Thus, the respondent failed to demonstrate prima facie eligibility for a U visa. Under the circumstances, we find that the respondent did not establish good cause for a continuance and we conclude that the Immigration Judge did not abuse his discretion in denying the continuance.
 
*2 The respondent’s motion to remand appears to claim that he received ineffective assistance from prior counsel. The motion is accompanied by a copy of a U visa petition, an unsigned law enforcement certification in support of the visa petition, and an affidavit in support the visa petition detailing an immigration fraud allegedly committed against the respondent. The motion is defective as a claim of ineffective assistance because the respondent has failed to substantially comply with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective representation outlined in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (I.J. at 4). Given the vague nature of the affidavit underlying the Form I-918 concerning the alleged fraud and the equally vague descriptions of both prior and current counsel regarding the actions taken in connection with the filing of the Form I-918, we do not find that deficient representation is sufficiently plain on the record to excuse compliance with Matter of Lozada. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir.2000). Inasmuch as the respondent has not shown that he prima facie eligible for a U visa, we also find that he has failed to demonstrate the prejudice required to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance because he did not establish that he is prima facie eligible for the relief sought. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (an alien asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must demonstrate prejudice); see also Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793-94 (9th Cir. 2005) (stating that an alien must demonstrate that prior counsel’s performance may have affected the outcome of the proceedings).
 
Effective January 20, 2009, an Immigration Judge who grants an alien voluntary departure must advise the alien that proof of posting of a bond with the DHS must be submitted to the Board within 30 days of filing an appeal, and that the Board will not reinstate a period of voluntary departure in its final order unless the alien has timely submitted sufficient proof that the required bond has been posted. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3). See Matter of Gamero, 25 I&N Dec. 164 (BIA 2010). The Immigration Judge provided the respondent with the required advisals and granted the respondent a 60-day voluntary departure period, conditioned upon the posting of a $500 bond. The record before the Board, however, does not reflect that the respondent submitted timely proof of having paid the bond. Therefore, the voluntary departure period will not be reinstated, and the respondent will be ordered removed from the United States to Mexico pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s alternate order.
 
*3 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 8561133 (BIA)

2015 WL 8562302 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO CRUZ MONTER A.K.A. SERGIO CRUZ MONTES
File: AXXX XX2 222 - Buffalo, NY
November 24, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew C. Hagen, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michele Henriques
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated March 13, 2014, finding him removable from the United States, as charged, and denying his motion to suppress evidence.1 See Motion to Suppress (December 2, 2013). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge for the reasons stated therein. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The sole issue on appeal is the respondent’s request that we reopen these proceedings based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
A motion to reopen may be denied if the respondent: (1) fails to establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought; (2) fails to introduce previously unavailable, material evidence; or (3) is found not to be entitled to a discretionary grant of the relief sought even if the first two requirements are met. INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992). With respect to the second ground, the regulations state that a “motion to reopen proceedings shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
A motion to remand must conform to the same standards as a motion to reopen where the respondent seeks to present new evidence, but will only be granted if the new evidence “would likely change the result in the case.” See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992). Here, the respondent seeks a remand based on a claim that prior counsel was ineffective for various reasons. See Respondent’s Brief at 8-11.
 
The respondent has not fulfilled the prerequisites for allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Debeatham v. Holder, 602 F.3d 481 484-85 (2d Cir. 2010). On appeal, the respondent makes general allegations concerning what actions former counsel should have taken, but in lieu of setting forth the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel represented to the respondent, the respondent provides a statement in which he identifies several issues he claims that prior counsel did not appropriately address (Respondent’s Brief at Exhibit B). Debeatham v. Holder, supra, at 484-85. The record does not contain proof that former counsel has been notified of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond. Respondent’s current counsel indicates he sent prior counsel a letter and a copy of the respondent’s brief on appeal. The record does not contain proof that a complaint has been filed with the disciplinary authority, and we are not persuaded by the respondent’s statement claiming that he did not file a grievance with the New York State Bar Association because he was unaware of that option.
 
*2 However, even if the respondent had complied with the Lozada requirements, he has not shown prejudice resulting from counsel’s alleged deficiencies. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638. The respondent has not identified any relief for which he may be eligible, or submitted the appropriate application for relief and supporting documents, pursuant to 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed on December 19, 2013, but reopened proceedings, sua sponte, on December 20, 2013.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUCIANO DURAN-ARREOLA A.K.A. LUCIANO ARREOLA
File: AXXX XX4 701 - Eloy, AZ
November 23, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eric G. Bjotvedt, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s July 9, 2015, denial of voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for pre-conclusion voluntary departure based on a finding that the respondent did not warrant relief in the exercise of discretion (I.J. at 4-5). Specifically, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s Arizona criminal conviction for Solicitation to Commit Child Abuse with Sexual Motivation was not outweighed by his positive equities (I.J. at 4; Exh. 2). The respondent argues on appeal that a remand is warranted in this matter, because: (1) the Immigration Judge erred in considering the incorrect voluntary departure standard; and (2) he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel in that he was prevented from testifying at his hearing (Resp. Br. at 4). We disagree that a remand is warranted in this case as the respondent has not established that either of the alleged errors have caused him prejudice. See Vargas-Hernandez, 497 F.3d at 919, 926 (9th Cir. 2007); Lopez-Umanzor v. Gonzales, 405 F.3d 1049, 1059 (9th Cir. 2005); Colmenar v. INS, 210 F.3d 967, 971-72 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
First, we acknowledge that the Immigration Judge laid out the legal standard for post-conclusion voluntary departure at the outset of her order, when, in fact, the respondent seeks pre-conclusion voluntary departure (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 15-16). However, later in the decision the Immigration Judge properly acknowledged the appropriate form of relief sought (I.J. at 4). Further, the Immigration Judge based her denial on a finding that the respondent did not warrant relief as a matter of discretion, a determination that is dispositive of both forms of relief Compare sections 240B(a) and 240B(b) of the Act; see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(b), (c). We find no merit in the respondent’s appellate argument that the discretionary standard is higher for post-conclusion voluntary departure, and find no indication that the Immigration Judge employed an inappropriate discretionary standard against the respondent (Resp. Br. at 5; I.J. at 4-5). Though our case law recognizes that post-conclusion voluntary departure contains a more stringent eligibility requirement in that there are certain bars to relief, there is no differentiation in our case law between the discretionary standards employed in pre and post-conclusion voluntary departure. See e.g. See Matter of Arguelles-Campos, 22 I&N Dec. 811, 817 (BIA 1999). Based on our de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the severity and recentness of the respondent’s criminal conviction in this matter outweighs his positive equities, including his remorse over his actions, his family ties in the United States, and the isolated nature of the conviction (I.J. at 4-5). See Matter of Gamboa, 14 I&N Dec. 244 (BIA 1972); see also Matter of Arguelles, 22 I&N Dec. 811, 817 (BIA 1999).
 
*2 Additionally, we disagree with the respondent that a remand is warranted based on ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp. Br. at 7-9). Regardless of whether the respondent’s counsel acted ineffectively, the respondent has not established the necessary prejudice to warrant remand. See e.g. Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793 (9th Cir. 2005) (“To prevail [on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim], the petitioner must demonstrate first that counsel failed to perform with sufficient competence, and, second, that she was prejudiced by counsel’s performance.”). We disagree with the respondent that the addition of his testimony regarding the circumstances surrounding his arrest and conviction could alter the outcome in these proceedings (Resp. Br. at 8). Nothing the respondent claims he would testify to could alter the fact that the respondent plead guilty to, and was convicted of this offense. We also disagree that any of the additional evidence of hardship to the respondent’s family, submitted for the first time on appeal, would have altered the outcome here (Resp. Br. at 8; Resp. Exh. B). As discussed above, based on our de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge the severity and recentness of this conviction are not outweighed by the respondent’s positive equities. See Matter of Gamboa, 14 I&N Dec. at 244; see also Matter of Arguelles, 22 I&N Dec. at 817.
 
Accordingly, there is no need to remand this matter, as neither the iteration of the pre-conclusion voluntary departure standard by the Immigration Judge, nor the respondent’s testimony regarding the circumstances surrounding his conviction, or the evidence submitted with this appeal, would alter the outcome of these proceedings. See Vargas-Hernandez, supra, at 926; Colmenar v. INS, supra, at 971-72. The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 8561140 (BIA)

APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent is a female native and citizen of Guatemala. On December 16, 2005, an in absentia order of removal was entered against her pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent filed a motion to reopen on August 5, 2014. On August 22, 2014, an Immigration Judge denied the motion. The respondent now appeals from that decision. The respondent is not challenging that she received proper notice of her scheduled hearing on December 16, 2005. Rather, she contends that an unnamed notario gave her bad advice not to attend her hearing and that her motion is thus subject to equitable tolling. We disagree. The Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent has not shown that she received ineffective assistance of counsel, Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), to excuse her failure to appear at her hearing. Contrary to her claims, the record also reflects that when the Notice to Appear (NTA) was personally served on her, the respondent was advised in her native language, Spanish, of the consequences for failing to appear. See also Exhibit 2, Form I-213, at 3 (respondent indicated that she understood her obligation to report for her hearing). Lastly, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not shown that she is prima facie eligible for relief, such as withholding of removal or voluntary departure, to warrant reopening (I.J. at 2; Motion at 4).1 The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDWARD PSZCZOLA
File: AXXX XX5 199 - Chicago, IL
November 18, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Justin R. Burton, Esquire
*1 This case was last before us on May 8, 2015, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s September 17, 2013, decision to deny his request for a continuance and his application for cancellation of removal. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen proceedings on August 7, 2015. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from his prior counsel, who, he argues, failed to properly prepare him and failed to file sufficient evidence in support of his requests for a continuance and for relief from removal. This evidence includes various affidavits from family, as well as tax documents and his wife’s medical records.1 He argues that if his attorney had introduced such documents at the hearing, the Immigration Judge would not have found that he did not warrant relief as a matter of discretion (I.J. at 3-5). In support of his ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent has submitted, inter alia, various affidavits, a photocopied complaint to the Supreme Court of Illinois, a letter to his prior counsel informing her of his ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and her response to the respondent’s allegations.
 
Notwithstanding this evidence, the Seventh Circuit Court of Appeals has held that an alien cannot prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim if the ultimate relief he is seeking, such as a continuance or cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act, is discretionary in nature. See Dave v. Ashcroft, 3363 F.3d 649 (7th Cir. 2004). Moreover, since we do not find any prejudice as a result of his prior attorney’s actions or inactions, we would not otherwise remand the record on our own accord (Bd. Dec. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
While the respondent now proffers various affidavits and tax returns, we do not find that such evidence materially affects the Immigration Judge’s decision. As an initial matter, the respondent argues that his prior attorney misunderstood the filing instructions given by the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 9). While the respondent’s observations may be valid, he does not dispute that he was granted a further opportunity to support his applications for relief (Tr. at 18-23). As such, the respondent suffered no prejudice as a result of this misunderstanding. During the continued proceedings, the Immigration Judge considered testimony by the respondent’s son (Tr. at 53-57) and further accepted and read a letter from the respondent’s daughter (Tr. at 46, 55).2 While the respondent now submits affidavits from his family, we do not find that these affidavits materially alter the Immigration Judge’s findings regarding the respondent’s equities.3 The Immigration Judge weighed the family hardship which would result upon the respondent’s removal, but found that his criminal record outweighed this hardship (I.J. at 4-6; Bd. Dec. at 2). Notwithstanding the respondent’s proffered evidence, we continue to find that our overriding obligation is to safeguard the interests of the United States, and not just those of the respondent and those close to him (Bd. Dec. at 2).
 
*2 As such, we do not find that the respondent has presented a viable ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and we find no basis upon which to disturb our prior decision. The following order will, thus, be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2015 WL 8561171 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUSTAVO HERNANDEZ-ANDRADE A.K.A. GUSTAVO HERNANDEZ A.K.A. ANDRE HERNANDEZ A.K.A. RAFAEL MENDEZ HERNANDEZ A.K.A. RAFAEL HERNANDEZ MENDEZ A.K.A. JUAN ELADIOLUIS VASQUES
File: AXXX XX1 863 - Adelanto, CA
November 16, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ruben Salazar, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 27, 2015. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable based on his criminal convictions and ordered him removed. The respondent challenges removability on appeal, asserting there was insufficient evidence of alienage and his conviction. The respondent also challenges denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Notice to Appear alleges that the respondent is not a citizen or national of the United States. It also alleges that the respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. The respondent admitted these factual allegations (Tr. at 12). Based on this evidence, the Immigration Judge found the allegations proved (I.J. at 2). We do not find this factual finding to be clearly erroneous.
 
The Notice to Appear also alleges that, on March 28, 2014, the respondent was convicted of Possession for Sale of a Controlled Substance (Methamphetamine), in violation of California Health and Safety Code section 11378 (Exh. 1). A record of this conviction was entered into the record of proceedings (Exh. 2). See section 240(c)(3)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.41(a). Cf. United States v. Snellenberger, 548 F.3d 699, 701-02 (9th Cir. 2008) (en banc) (minute order sufficient). In addition, the respondent admitted the conviction (Tr. at 13). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.41(d), 1240.10(c). Cf. Pagayon v. Holder, 675 F.3d 1182, 1189-1190 (9th Cir. 2011); Barragan-Lopez v. Mukasey, 508 F.3d 899, 905 (9th Cir. 2007). Based on this evidence, the Immigration Judge found the conviction proved (I.J. at 2-3). We do not find this factual finding to be clearly erroneous.
 
*2 The Immigration Judge found the statute to be divisible, applied the modified categorical approach, and, after reviewing the conviction record, found the respondent removable for having been convicted of an aggravated felony involving illicit trafficking in a controlled substance.1 The respondent argues that his conviction for Possession for Sale of a Controlled Substance (Methamphetamine), in violation of California Health and Safety Code section 11378, does not constitute an aggravated felony involving illicit trafficking in a controlled substance because the California law criminalizes drugs not criminalized under federal law and one of the submitted documents does not identify the drug.
 
We agree that the statute of conviction is divisible and the substance involved in his conviction must be established before the charge of removability can be sustained. See Padilla-Martinez v. Holder, 770 F.3d 825, 832 n.3 (9th Cir. 2014). See also U.S. v. Torre-Jimenez, 771 F.3d 1163, 1165-67 (9th Cir. 2014); Coronado v. Holder, 747 F.3d 662, 668-69 (9th Cir. 2014); U.S. v. Valdavinos-Torres, 704 F.3d 679, 689 (9th Cir. 2012) (holding that the modified categorical approach could be used as part of elements-based inquiry to ascertain portion of California prohibited substances statute to which alien had actually pleaded guilty to violating). Under the modified categorical approach, courts conduct a limited examination of documents in the record of conviction to determine if there is sufficient evidence to conclude that a defendant was convicted of the elements of the generically defined crime even though the statute of conviction is facially over inclusive. See Shepard v. U.S., 544 U.S. 13 (2005).2
 
We find that the judicially-noticeable documents submitted in this case sufficiently establish that the controlled substance involved was methamphetamine, and support the charge of removability. Count 1 of the complaint for case number RIF1314876 states that the respondent “did willfully and unlawfully possess for sale a controlled substance, to wit, methamphetamine” (Exh. 2 at 2). The plea form indicates that the respondent pled guilty to a violation of California Health and Safety Code section 11378, as reflected in count 1 of the complaint. It says: “I will enter a guilty plea to the following charges and enhancements: Ct 1: HS 11378...” (Exh. 2 at 4). In addition, the court’s sentencing memorandum refers to “charges admitted: I 11378 HS” (Exh. 2 at 5). There is no dispute that methamphetamine is a controlled substance under the federal law. See 21 U.S.C. § 812(c), sch. II(c), III(a)(3).
 
*3 Within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, a state drug crime qualifies as an aggravated felony if it contains a “trafficking element.” See e.g., Daas v. Holder, 620 F.3d 1050, 1054 (9th Cir. 2010). A state offense has a “trafficking element” if it involves the possession of a federally-controlled substance with intent to sell it for a price. See Rendon v. Mukasey, 520 F.3d 967, 976 (9th Cir. 2008). In order to convict a defendant of possessing a controlled substance for sale, a California prosecutor is required to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that the defendant had the specific intent to sell a controlled substance for some form of remuneration. See People v. Harris, 99 Cal. Rptr.2d 618 (Cal.App. 2d Dist. 2000); People v. Parra, 82 Cal. Rptr. 2d 541, 543-44 (Cal. Ct. App. 1999). The terms “sale” and “sell” are not statutorily defined in California, but state courts have interpreted them in a manner consistent with their common meaning, i.e.,”DD’ to transfer to another for a price.”DD’ People v. Daniels, 537 P.2d 1232, 1235 (Cal. 1975). Therefore, in California a “sale” of drugs denotes an exchange of drugs for cash, goods, services, favors, or some other non-cash benefit. People v. Daniels, supra, at 1235; People v. Peck, 61 Cal. Rptr. 2d 1, 3 (Cal. Ct. App. 1996); People v. Lazenby, 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 541, 542-43 (Cal. Ct. App. 1992). A non-remunerative transfer of drugs, such as casual sharing, does not qualify as a “sale” under California law.
 
By its terms, California Health and Safety Code section 11378 prohibits the possession for sale of various narcotic controlled substances. Because a conviction under section 11378 requires proof beyond a reasonable doubt that the defendant possessed a controlled substance with the specific intent to sell it (or have it sold) for a price, we conclude that section 11378 has a “““trafficking element.” See Rendon v. Mukasey, 520 F.3d at 975-76.
 
Consequently, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s findings that the respondent has been convicted of an aggravated felony involving illicit trafficking in a controlled substance and that removability has been proved by clear and convincing evidence. See section 240(c)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(a); Woodby v. INS, 385 U.S. 276, 286 (1966). Cf. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.15, 1240.10.
 
*4 On appeal, the respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion in denying a continuance (I.J. at 3-4). The respondent had asked the Immigration Judge for a continuance to pursue post-conviction relief. The respondent argues on appeal that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of this conviction.3 An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).
 
We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown. We note that, in the plea form, the respondent was warned: “If I am not a citizen of the United States, I understand that this conviction may have the consequences of deportation, exclusion from admission to the United States, or denial of naturalization pursuant to the laws of the United States” (Exh. 2 at 3). In addition, we note that the respondent does not say that a post-conviction motion has been filed and granted.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The term “aggravated felony” includes illicit trafficking in a controlled substance (as defined in section 102 of the Controlled Substances Act), including a drug trafficking crime (as defined in section 924(c) of title 18, United States Code). See section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B). The term “drug trafficking crime” means any state felony punishable under the Controlled Substances Act. See 18 U.S.C. § 924(c)(2). See Lopez v. Gonzales, 549 U.S. 47 (2006).

2

The record of conviction includes the charging document (indictment, complaint, or information), jury instructions, written plea agreement, transcript of plea colloquy, judgment of conviction, jury verdict, a comparable judicial record, and any explicit factual finding by the trial judge to which the defendant assented (e.g., in police report or in pre-sentence investigation report). See Shepard v. U.S., 544 U.S. at 16, 26.

3

In Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

2015 WL 7074118 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FERNANDO SANCHEZ-MALDONADO
File: AXXX XX3 279 - Lumpkin, GA
October 30, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Johanna Cochran, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Diane Dodd
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals a May 21, 2015, decision by the Immigration Judge denying reopening of an April 2, 2015, decision, which ordered the respondent removed to Mexico. The Department of Homeland Security has filed a brief in opposition to the appeal. The record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge, under a clearly erroneous standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen was a 21-page document alleging ineffective assistance of counsel against the attorney who represented him in his removal proceedings. He made specific arguments against said attorney, i.e., that his attorney erroneously told him that he did not have any other options other to accept his order of removal (Motion at 13-14). He also contends that his attorney did not inform him or his father that he had filed a stay of removal (Motion at 9). Furthermore, he contends that he is eligible for relief from removal (Motion at 15-20). He complied with the procedural requirements delineated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (Motion at 9-10).
 
The Immigration Judge did not discuss the respondent’s specific arguments in his motion, only issuing a one-page handwritten order, stating that the respondent twice confirmed that he agreed to the order of removal, waived appeal, and was satisfied with the representation he received from his counsel. The Immigration Judge’s conclusion does not sufficiently address the issues raised by the respondent’s motion. See Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994) (when a motion to reopen deportation proceedings is denied, the Immigration Judge must identify and fully explain the reasons for the decision; otherwise, the parties are deprived of a fair opportunity to contest the decision on appeal, and the Board is unable to exercise meaningfully its responsibility of reviewing the decision in light of the arguments advanced on appeal); see also Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468 (BIA 1999) (holding that because the Board’s fact-finding ability is limited, it is essential for Immigration Judges to include in their decisions clear and complete findings of fact that are supported by the record and are in compliance with controlling law). The respondent has argued that his agreement to removal and waiver of appeal were based on erroneous advice rather than a tactical decision.
 
*2 Accordingly, we find it necessary to remand the record for the entry of a new decision that more fully addresses, in light of the evidence in the record, the respondent’s arguments and the reasons for rejecting them.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the aforementioned decision.
 
Joan B. Geller
FOR THE BOARD

2015 WL 7074137 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE JESUS MORA-CARDONA A.K.A. JOSE MORA
File: AXXX XX6 459 - Memphis, TN
October 28, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Divyesh Gopal, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has filed a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 10, 2014, denying his request for a continuance and granting voluntary departure with an alternative order of removal from the United States. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a brief or other response. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent failed to establish good cause for a continuance in his case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 355 (BIA 1983). In particular, the Immigration Judge correctly noted that the respondent had been granted two prior continuances, one providing nearly a year to obtain counsel and a second providing nearly another year to file all applications for relief (I.J. at 1-6; Tr. at 9, 16). The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent filed a request for the favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion (PD) with DHS approximately 2 days before the final hearing, the request was denied by DHS, and the respondent did not have any applications for relief pending on the date of the final hearing (I.J. at 3, 6; Tr. at 20). Compare Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009) (setting forth factors to consider when adjudicating a motion for a continuance based on a pending visa petition). The Immigration Judge correctly noted that DHS opposed the continuance request (I.J. at 5-6; Tr. at 21). Id.
 
On appeal, the respondent concedes that he had sufficient time to prepare his case (Resp. Br. at 7). He also notes that the decision of whether or not to exercise prosecutorial discretion lies solely with DHS, not with the Immigration Judge or the Board of Immigration Appeals (Resp. Br. at 7).
 
The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge failed to consider whether DHS was being reasonable in opposing the continuance request, where it was based on the respondent filing his PD request just 48 hours before the final hearing and the court’s filing deadline for all applications for relief (Resp. Br. at 7-8; Tr. at 21). See Matter of Hashmi, supra, at 791. It is clear from the Immigration Judge’s decision, however, that she concluded that the DHS’ opposition to the continuance was reasonable and supported by the record, and we agree with these conclusions (I.J. at 4-6). Id. The Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondent did not exercise due diligence in pursuing relief from removal (I.J. at 5). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate good cause for a continuance under these circumstances, and conclude that the discretionary denial of the motion for a continuance was appropriate.
 
*2 The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge failed to consider the likelihood of success of his request for prosecutorial discretion filed with DHS (Resp. Br. at 7-8). However, the correct measure is the ultimate likelihood of success on any application for adjustment to lawful status in the United States, not whether or not a respondent’s removal proceedings might be administratively closed in the exercise of prosecutorial discretion. See Matter of Hashmi, supra, at 790. Moreover, the respondent has not provided any specific arguments or evidence showing that he warranted a grant of prosecutorial discretion. To date, the respondent has not shown that he had any prima facie approvable application for relief from removal pending on the date of the last hearing.
 
The denial of a continuance will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing, and here the respondent has not shown such. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987). Furthermore, contrary to his claim on appeal, the respondent has failed to show prejudice (Resp. Br. at 8). See Matter of Sibrun, supra. In particular, to date the respondent has not established that he had any applications for relief pending that were prima facie approvable, including a request for the favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion by DHS, as discussed above. Id.
 
On the Notice of Appeal, the respondent alleges ineffective assistance by prior counsel. This allegation is not accompanied by any specific details, and is not raised as an issue in the respondent’s brief on appeal. Furthermore, this allegation is not supported by any documentary evidence in compliance with any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (establishing ineffective assistance of counsel), nor does the record support a finding of per se ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Appeal Brief; Respondent’s Notice of Appeal; I.J. at 1-6; Tr. at 1-30).
 
Finally, we are unable to reinstate voluntary departure in this case. The Immigration Judge provided the respondent with the required advisals and granted the respondent a 60-day voluntary departure period, conditioned upon the posting of a $500 bond. The record before the Board, however, does not reflect that the respondent submitted timely proof of having paid that bond. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3). See Matter of Gamero, 25 I&N Dec. 164 (BIA 2010). Therefore, the voluntary departure period will not be reinstated, and the respondent is subject to removal from the United States pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s alternate order.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent is ordered removed from the United States to Mexico, pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s alternate order.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA DE LOS ANGELES LOWE A.K.A. MARIA LOWE-ALARCON A.K.A. MARIA ALARCON-CERVANTES
File: AXXX XX7 912 - El Paso, TX
October 27, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Charles Spector, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. She has filed a timely motion to reconsider our August 7, 2015, decision denying reopening. The motion is denied.
 
The respondent contends that the Board erred in denying reopening because former counsel provided ineffective assistance. In her motion to reopen, she argued that former counsel erroneously conceded she was removable under section 237(a)(1)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(E)(i). As we previously found, former counsel did not concede removability. Further, on the facts of this case, we agreed with the Immigration Judge that the respondent was removable as charged, and therefore did not demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel or prejudice.
 
On reconsideration, the respondent repeats the arguments previously raised, but has not identified any material error of fact or law in our prior decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The respondent continues to argue that she is not removable as charged because her adjustment of status was not an admission under Martinez v. Mukasey, 519 F.3d 532 (5th Cir. 2008). The issue here was whether the respondent was removable as an alien who “prior to the date of entry, at the time of any entry, or within five years of the date of any entry” smuggled any other alien into the United States. Section 237(a)(1)(E)(i) of the Act.
 
On this record, the respondent did not smuggle other aliens prior to her entry or at the time of any entry, i.e., in 1996 or on October 26, 2007, when she adjusted her status. However, she was convicted of smuggling aliens on October 21, 2010, and this conviction was within 5 years of “any entry”, i.e., her adjustment of status. See Matter of Bouchra Agour, 26 I&N Dec. 566, 579 (BIA 2015)(adjustment of status is merely a procedural mechanism by which an alien is assimilated to the position of one seeking to enter the United States.) The words “any entry” are broad and contemplate a variety of ways to enter the United States, including adjustment of status. Since Martinez only involved the definition of admission or admitted for purposes of section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), Martinez does not apply.
 
*2 To the extent that the word “entry” in section 237(a)(1)(E)(i) may be construed as synonymous with admission,1 we nevertheless continue to find that Martinez is not binding here. The question here is removability, not eligibility for a waiver as in Martinez. Indeed, the Fifth Circuit was careful to limit the holding of Martinez to the question of a section 212(h) waiver. See e.g. Martinez v. Mukasey, supra, 519 F.3d at 542-45 (declining to apply Matter of Rosas-Ramirez, 22 I&N Dec. 616 (BIA 1999), which involved a question of removability, to section 212(h)). Since Martinez did not hold that adjustment of status was not an admission for purposes of removability, we decline to extend the Fifth Circuit’s holding to removability.2 See Matter of Chavez-Alvarez, 26 I&N Dec. 274, 275-78 (2014)(adjustment of status is an admission for purposes of determining inadmissibility or removability), rev’d on other grounds, 783 F.3d 478 (3d Cir. 2015). The respondent has not identified any material error of fact or law in our August 7, 2015, decision. Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration and Nationality Act no longer defines the word “““entry.” Matter of Bouchra Agour, 26 I&N Dec. at 576-77.

2

In our August 7, 2015, decision, we stated that “Martinez did not hold that adjustment of status was not an admission.” For purposes of clarity, that statement should state: “Martinez did not hold that adjustment of status was not an admission for purposes of removability.”

IN RE: DRIN SYLEJMANI

October 23, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Orlando Mondragon, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margot Johnson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law (conceded)
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status (conceded)
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; voluntary departure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Kosovo, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated November 12, 2014, granting the respondent pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The appeal will be dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that the Immigration Judge should have continued removal proceedings to allow the respondent’s wife, a United States citizen, to pursue a Petition for Alien Relative (Form I-130) on his behalf and for attorney preparation (Resp. Brief at 2-4). However, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) argues that because the respondent accepted pre-conclusion voluntary departure, he waived his right to pursue an appeal (DHS Brief at 2-3). A condition of a proper grant of pre-conclusion voluntary departure requires the respondent to waive his right to appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D). Therefore, we must evaluate whether the respondent waived this right knowingly and voluntarily.
 
Here, the respondent does not argue that his waiver of appeal was not made knowingly and voluntarily. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320 (BIA 2000). Instead, the respondent expressly states he “is not appealing the grant of voluntary departure . . .” (Resp. Brief at 2). Notably, the respondent accepted this benefit with the assistance of counsel. The respondent, who has new counsel on appeal, has not challenged the effectiveness of his counsel below. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (outlining the requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel); see also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647 (5th Cir. 2014) (requiring compliance with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada). Absent egregious circumstances, which are not alleged in this case, the respondent is bound by the tactical decisions and actions made by his attorney when such attorney is acting in his professional capacity. Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938, 942 (BIA 1995) (citation omitted).
 
*2 Given this procedural posture, we lack jurisdiction to consider the merits of the respondent’s appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39 (“[T]he decision of the Immigration Judge becomes final upon waiver of appeal . . . .”); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(a)(1) (“A Notice of Appeal may not be filed by any party who has waived appeal . . . .”); Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, supra, at 699 (“[I]nasmuch as the immigration judge’s decision is final, this Board lacks jurisdiction to adjudicate the case before us.”). Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. The record is returned to the Immigration Judge without further action.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: DARSONI DE OLIVEIRA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mayra Joli, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Chelsea Braden
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative decision on October 8, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision ordering the respondent removed to Brazil. The respondent seeks reopening 10 months later and requests equitably tolling of the time limit based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 713 F.3d 1357 (11th Cir. 2013) (en banc). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied as untimely, as the respondent has not shown that an exception to the time limitation on motions applies. See Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
We are not persuaded of any error with prior counsel’s representation, much less prejudice. See Ali v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 643 F.3d 1324, 1329 (11th Cir. 2011) (alien must establish prejudice in order to prevail on ineffective assistance claim); Dakane v. U.S. Att’y. Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273-74 (11th Cir. 2005); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (establishing ineffective assistance of counsel requires an alien to demonstrate prejudice from counsel’s conduct, such that it compromised the fundamental fairness of the hearing). Notwithstanding the respondent’s allegations, the Immigration Judge’s marriage fraud finding was well-supported considering, inter alia, the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services officer’s credible testimony, the respondent’s sworn statement admitting fraud, and her unpersuasive testimony. Nor will we second-guess counsel’s strategic decision to not challenge the Immigration Judge’s negative inference drawn from the respondent’s lack of eye contact, particularly given the specific and cogent reasons underlying the adverse credibility determination. See Matter of A-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 1106 (BIA 1998) (observing that an adverse inference based on an alien’s demeanor is generally accorded high deference, especially where such inference is supported by specific and cogent reasons undermining the alien’s veracity); Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (absent a showing of egregious circumstances, an attorney’s tactical decisions are binding on the alien). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: PATRICIA DE LOS ANGELES CALLES DE MELGAR FERNANDA GUADALUPE MELGAR CALLES
Files: AXXX XX9 516 - Atlanta, GA
AXXX XX8 083
October 15, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Benjamin Peppercorn Bratter, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondents,1 natives and citizens of El Salvador, appeal the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated July 1, 2015,2 denying their motion to reopen. We will dismiss the appeal.
 
On December 4, 2014, the respondents, through counsel, submitted written pleadings admitting all of the factual allegations contained in this their Notices to Appear and conceded that they were subject to removal from the United States pursuant to section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1; Exh. 2). These proceedings were completed when, on January 6, 2015, the respondents appeared before the Immigration Judge with counsel, and indicated that they wished to take an order of removal (I.J. at 1). The respondent filed the instant motion to reopen on June 11, 2015.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (“A motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal, deportation, or exclusion, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later.”). We acknowledge the respondent’s claim that her former attorney provided with ineffective assistance of counsel (Motion to Reopen). However, she has not established that she was prejudiced by her former attorney’s handling of this matter. In particular, we note that while the respondent indicates that she wishes to file an Application for Asylum and for Withholding of Removal (Form I-589), she has not demonstrated her prima facie eligibility for such relief. The respondent, who fears gangs in El Salvador, has not demonstrated a nexus between her fear of harm, and a protected ground enumerated in section 101(a)(42)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(42)(A). See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472 (BIA 1992) (explaining that the Board may deny a motion to remand or motion to reopen where a prima facie case for the relief sought has not been established); see Matter of M-E-V-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 227, 235 (BIA 2014) (“asylum and refugee laws do not protect people from general conditions of strife, such as crime and other societal afflictions”).
 
The respondent’s motion also did not establish that changed country conditions in El Salvador should permit her to file an otherwise untimely motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(4)(i). The respondent does not allege sufficient facts to establish a change in conditions in El Salvador which arose subsequent to her ultimate removal hearing on January 6, 2015. See Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247, 257 (BIA 2007).
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has not established that an exceptional situation exists which would warrant sua sponte reopening. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
For the reasons set forth above, we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2015 WL 7074194 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ASHABEN VISHAL PATEL
File: AXXX XX2 539 - Harlingen, TX
October 15, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcia Kasdan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Delia Irene Gonzalez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of July 28, 2014. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia on November 29, 2011. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
The Notice of Hearing (NOH) for the November 29, 2011, hearing was mailed to the respondent’s counsel, Norma Sepulveda, Esquire, on February 23, 2011 (I.J. at 1).1 The respondent failed to appear at her hearing and an in absentia order was entered on November 29, 2011. The respondent filed a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings on July 8, 2014, alleging that she did not receive notice of her hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that Ms. Sepulveda’s representation was limited to bond proceedings. However, the record does not support this assertion. As noted by the Immigration Judge, Ms. Sepulveda filed a motion to withdraw on October 17, 2011, with evidence showing that the respondent was notified of her November 29, 2011, hearing (I.J. at 2). The fact that Ms. Sepulveda had withdrawn representation because the respondent intended to seek new counsel elsewhere does not excuse the respondent’s appearance at her November 29, 2011, hearing.
 
The respondent has not alleged or shown ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006); Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605, 609 fn.15 (5th Cir. 2006) (alien’s right to due process is violated when the representation afforded him was so deficient as to impinge upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing, and, as a result, the alien suffered substantial prejudice); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). To show prejudice, an alien must make a prima facie showing that, but for counsel’s error, she would have been entitled to the relief she sought. See Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994). Here, while the respondent contends that she would seek asylum, she has not specified the basis for her claim, nor has she provided the requisite application (Form I-589). The respondent has not demonstrated that she is prima facie eligible for any relief, and thus she has failed to establish she suffered any prejudice, even had there been ineffective representation. See, e.g., Ogunfuye v. Holder, 610 F.3d 303, 306-07 (5th Cir. 2010) (explaining that to prevail on a due process claim, an individual must demonstrate with substantial evidence that the error could have changed the outcome of proceedings).
 
*2 The respondent’s appellate contention that venue should have been automatically changed once the respondent was released from detention is unavailing. There was no basis for the Immigration Judge to do so in the absence of any request for a change of venue filed by the respondent.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record contains a Form EOIR-28 from Ms. Sepulveda, filed on January 14, 2011.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSCAR LEONEL LOPEZ VASQUEZ A.K.A. OSCAR VASQUEZ
File: AXXX XX6 654 - Philadelphia, PA
October 15, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Petra D. Fist, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brian G. McDonnell
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 15, 2015, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings that were conducted in absentia on March 10, 2014. The respondent also requests a remand. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion denied.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent did not demonstrate that the in absentia order of removal qualifies for rescission. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). Nor do we find clear error in the factual findings supporting that determination. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an order issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed (a) within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that he failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, or (b) at any time if the alien demonstrates he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely as it was filed well after 180 days, more than 1 year, after the date of the order of removal and, in any event, establishes no exceptional circumstances that would excuse the respondent’s failure to appear. Contrary to the respondent’s apparent assertion, whether exceptional circumstances existed one year earlier does not assist the respondent in demonstrating that his untimely motion qualifies for rescission. However, to the extent the respondent claims lack of proper notice, his motion is timely.
 
Yet, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the respondent received proper notice of his March 10, 2014, scheduled hearing. As the Immigration Judge noted, the record reflects that the respondent was present with former counsel in court when the hearing date was furnished both orally and in writing (I.J. at 3; Exh. 3). The record does not reflect that either the respondent or former counsel ever indicated that they did not understand that the respondent’s next hearing was scheduled for March 10, 2014.
 
Likewise, the “new” affidavits proffered along with the respondent’s motion to remand neither specifically assert nor sufficiently reflect either that the respondent or former counsel did not receive proper notice or understand that the respondent’s next hearing was scheduled for March 10, 2014. Nor does such evidence sufficiently reflect that respondent’s former counsel failed to notify the respondent of his scheduled hearing.
 
*2 Moreover, while a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel can in some circumstances toll the filing requirements applicable to motions to reopen, an alien seeking such tolling must show that he or she exercised due diligence in discovering and seeking to remedy the ineffective assistance. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 248, 252 (3d Cir. 2005). The respondent does not contend that former counsel is responsible for his delay in filing his motion before the Immigration Judge. Indeed, the respondent does not dispute that he was, at the very least, aware that he had missed his scheduled hearing due to the alleged deficient performance of former counsel. Under the circumstances, the alleged “fixing the problem” response to the respondent’s continued inquiries about the status of his case alone is not a reasonable explanation for waiting more than a year to contact current counsel and move for reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel. On this record, we concur with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent has sufficiently shown due diligence in pursing reopening of his case. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, supra, at 252-53 (finding that a 13-month delay in moving to reopen was conclusive so as to deny equitable tolling, despite an alien’s difficulty in “navigating the shoals of American law”).
 
On the whole, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s resolution of the respondent’s claim. The respondent on appeal raises no other claims to relief. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed and his motion to remand is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 7074191 (BIA)
End of Document

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JENNIFER PARKER
File: AXXX XX6 784 - Eloy, AZ
October 8, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Benjamin T. Wiesinger, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the United Kingdom, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 30, 2015. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and denied the respondent’s request for another continuance. On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of the continuance.1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent alleges that she was not advised of the immigration consequences of the plea that resulted in the conviction that renders her removable and ineligible for relief from removal, so she filed a motion to vacate it.2 The motion before the criminal court was denied for untimeliness, and she filed a motion to reconsider the denial of the motion on November 7, 2014. That motion remained pending on June 30, 2015. The Immigration Judge granted the respondent continuances to pursue post-conviction relief from September 11, 2014, to June 22, 2015 (I.J. at 2-3).
 
On appeal, the respondent challenges the Immigration Judge’s denial of an additional continuance. She also says the motion to reconsider was denied on August 15, 2015, and says she has appealed the denial of the motion to the Arizona Court of Appeals.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown. While the respondent is free to pursue the appeal of the denial of the post-conviction motion, such an appeal does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

In the Notice of Appeal, the respondent generically asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that she was convicted of an aggravated felony, but she does not address that issue in her brief, so we find that issue abandoned.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. _____, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
