Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VISHAL KUMAR KANIYALAL PATEL A.K.A. VISHAL KANIYALAL PATEL
File: AXXX XX2 528 - Harlingen, TX
October 7, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcia Kasdan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Iris Guerra Bravo
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of August 5, 2014. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on November 28, 2011. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
The Notice of Hearing (NOH) for the November 28, 2011, hearing was mailed to the respondent’s counsel, Burhan Nomani, on April 8, 2011 (I.J. at 1).1 The respondent failed to appear at his hearing and an in absentia order was entered on November 28, 2011. The respondent filed a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings on July 8, 2014, alleging that he did not receive notice of his hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that Burhan Nomani’s representation was limited to bond proceedings. However, the record does not support this assertion. As noted by the Immigration Judge, Burhan Nomani filed a motion to withdraw on October 19, 2011, stating that the respondent is aware of his November 28, 2011, hearing, but had not maintained contact with counsel (I.J. at 2). The respondent has not alleged or shown ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006); Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605, 609 fn.15 (5th Cir. 2006) (alien’s right to due process is violated when the representation afforded him was so deficient as to impinge upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing, and, as a result, the alien suffered substantial prejudice); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). To show prejudice, an alien must make a prima facie showing that, but for counsel’s error, he would have been entitled to the relief he sought. See Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994). Here, while the respondent contends that he would seek asylum, he has not specified the basis for his claim, nor has he provided the requisite application (Form I-589). The respondent has not demonstrated he is prima facie eligible for any relief, and thus he has failed to establish he suffered any prejudice, even had there been ineffective representation. See, e.g., Ogunfuye v. Holder, 610 F.3d 303, 306-07 (5th Cir. 2010) (explaining that to prevail on a due process claim, an individual must demonstrate with substantial evidence that the error could have changed the outcome of proceedings).
 
*2 The respondent’s appellate contention that venue should have been automatically changed once the respondent was released from detention is unavailing. There was no basis for the Immigration Judge to do so in the absence of any request for a change of venue filed by the respondent.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

The record contains a Form EOIR-28 from Burhan Nomani, filed on February 7, 2011.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS EDUARDO MALDONADO-GOMEZ A.K.A. LUIS M. GOMEZ
File: AXXX XX3 121 - San Diego, CA
October 1, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murray D. Hilts, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 30, 2015, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent became a lawful permanent resident on April 22, 1996. On November 15, 2004, the respondent was convicted of: 1) transportation of a controlled substance in violation of section 11379(a) of the California Health and Safety Code, and 2) disobeying a court order to prevent domestic violence in violation of section 273.6(a) of the California Penal Code. Due to these convictions, on August 18, 2005, the respondent was issued a Notice to Appear charging him with removability for an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B), and for violating an order of protection under section 237(a)(2)(E)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(E)(ii). The respondent, through prior counsel, conceded removability and was ordered removed by an Immigration Judge on September 21, 2005. The respondent waived appeal.
 
On February 12, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, arguing that ineffective assistance of prior counsel prevented him from pursuing an application for cancellation of removal for lawful permanent residents during removal proceedings in 2005. Specifically, the respondent asserts that at the time of his removal proceedings, his conviction for transportation of a controlled substance was not an aggravated felony under controlling precedent; therefore, he would have been eligible to apply for cancellation of removal. On April 30, 2015, the Immigration Judge denied reopening, and the respondent now appeals.
 
In denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent was subject to the post-departure limitations on motions to reopen in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (I.J. at 1). Subsequent to the decision of the Immigration Judge, a precedent decision regarding the applicability of the departure bar to an alien’s statutory right to file a motion to reopen was issued by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. In Toor v. Lynch, 789 F.3d 1055 (9th Cir. 2015) the court held that the text of Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act (“IIRIRA”) makes clear that the an alien has a statutory right to file a motion to reopen and a motion to reconsider removal proceedings and this right is not limited by whether an alien has departed the United States. Therefore, the respondent’s removal from the United States did not preclude his ability to seek reopening within the jurisdiction of the Ninth Circuit.1
 
*2 The respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the final administrative decision in his case, which was the Immigration Judge’s September 21, 2005, decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1). The motion, in which the respondent seeks that his proceedings be reopened to allow him to apply for cancellation of removal for lawful permanent residents, does not fall within any statutory or regulatory exception to motion time or number limitations. Section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3), 1003.23(b)(4).
 
In his motion to reopen and on appeal, the respondent claims that prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance. While we recognize that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen for the reasons that follow.
 
The respondent was not entitled to equitable tolling to toll the approximately 10 year delay in filing his motion to reopen because the respondent failed to show that he acted with “due” or “reasonable” diligence in discovering any “deception, fraud, or error” that prevented him from timely filing his motion. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). On appeal, the respondent asserts solely that after he was removed to Mexico, he was unaware of the deficient performance of prior counsel, and that he only became aware of it after he unlawfully re-entered without inspection sometime after September 21, 2005, and contacted an attorney in August 2014. That the respondent waited until August 2014 to seek advice is not a showing of due or reasonable diligence, particularly given that he has not shown or otherwise alleged that he was prevented from asking a relative or friend to contact an attorney, or doing so himself, sooner than 2014. Nor has he shown that any ignorance of the law was caused by circumstances beyond his control. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001) (in order for equitable tolling to apply, the respondent needs to show that his ignorance was caused by circumstances beyond his control, and which go beyond a claim of excusable neglect). Accordingly, as the respondent has not shown that he warrants equitable tolling, the Immigration Judge appropriately denied his motion to reopen.
 
*3 Moreover, the respondent has not shown that his prior counsel’s allegedly deficient performance caused him prejudice, as the record does not demonstrate that prior counsel misapplied the law at the time of the respondent’s removal hearing. See Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893, 898 (9th Cir. 2008) (prejudice is established when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings). The respondent argues that he would have been eligible for cancellation of removal as his conviction was not an aggravated felony under controlling precedent. However, at the time of the respondent’s removal proceedings, there was no precedent from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit or the Board that indicated that the respondent would have been eligible for relief. Thus, the respondent has not demonstrated the requisite prejudice to support his claim, as he has not demonstrated that any alleged deficiencies of his former counsel affected the outcome of proceedings (I.J. at 2). See Iturribarria v. INS, supra, at 901-02.
 
Finally, we agree with the Immigration Judge that sua sponte reopening is not warranted (I.J. at 2). While we acknowledge that a fundamental change in law may be considered an exceptional situation warranting sua sponte reopening, we conclude that the respondent did not act diligently even after this change in law. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1134-36 (BIA 1999); Matter of G-C-L-, 23 I&N Dec. 359, 361 (BIA 2002) (explaining that because more than 5 years had passed since the enactment of new law, the interest of finality in immigration proceedings took precedence over sua sponte reopening an untimely motion to reopen to seek relief based upon that change in law). Further, considering the totality of the circumstances presented and alleged, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances sufficient to justify exercising his discretionary authority to reopen the respondent’s removal proceedings sua sponte (I.J. at 2). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge also held that the respondent was precluded from filing a motion to reopen under section 241(a)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(a)(5) (I.J. at 1-2). We agree that were the respondent subject to the reinstatement of his prior removal order under section 241(a)(5) of the Act, the Immigration Judge would be without authority to reopen the respondent’s prior removal proceedings. See section 241(a)(5) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 241.8 (procedures for reinstating prior removal order). However, there is no evidence in the record that the Department of Homeland Security has issued the respondent a notice that his prior removal order will be reinstated or has otherwise sought to reinstate his removal order. See 8 C.F.R. § 241.8.

IN RE: KAMALDEEP KAUR SANDHU

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anish Vashistha, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael D. Steinberg
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The present motion to reopen, which is the respondent’s second request for reopening, is both untimely filed by many years and number barred and is denied on that basis. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The motion has not been shown to qualify for any exception to the time and numerical limitations imposed by law on motions to reopen removal proceedings.
 
Moreover, we decline to equitably toll the applicable filing requirements based on the respondent’s alleged ineffective assistance of former counsels claim. The respondent does not assert, nor does the submitted evidence reflect, that she was prevented from timely filing the present motion by “deception, fraud, or error.” See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
Moreover, we do not find that there has not been any showing of due diligence in pursuing the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra. The doctrine of equitable tolling applies when “““despite all due diligence, [the party invoking equitable tolling] is unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of the claim.” Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001). The respondent does not dispute that she was, at the very least, dissatisfied with the performance of her former attorneys at the time services were rendered by each. Nor does she dispute that she was aware of both the Board’s June 22, 2007, decision dismissing her appeal, and October 16, 2007, decision denying her first motion to reopen. Other than hiring current counsel, for which the respondent provides no dates or details, the respondent has offered no reasonable explanation for waiting almost 8 years from the Board’s last decision in this case to file the present motion. Under the circumstances, we do not find that the respondent has sufficiently demonstrated that she exercised due diligence in pursuing her ineffective assistance claim against former counsels.
 
Further, the respondent has not demonstrated sufficient compliance with the procedural requirements for advancing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 53 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process). The submitted bar complaint packages noting that a copy of each package was sent to the corresponding counsel alone is insufficient to satisfy the requirement that former counsels be notified of the allegations against them and given an opportunity to respond. See Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the fact that the present motion is both untimely and number barred. Nor has she identified an exceptional situation that would warrant reopening of these proceedings under the Board’s discretionary sua sponte authority. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: MELVIN AVILA-MEJIA

APPEAL

Lee Knox, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:

Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; withdraw pleadings
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s November 6, 2014, decision denying both his request for a continuance and his motion to withdraw pleadings. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent’s contention that he should have been able to withdraw pleadings is without merit. See generally Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (finding that a respondent is bound by the tactical decisions made by his counsel). As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent did not specifically assert or describe any egregious circumstances surrounding his pleadings. While the respondent proffered a 1-page personal affidavit in support of his Motion to Withdraw Pleadings, he does not raise any argument on appeal that persuades this Board that the affidavit established a prima facie case that egregious violations of the Fourth Amendment or other liberties that might transgress notions of fundamental fairness and undermine the probative value of the evidence obtained occurred. See INS v. Lopez-Mendoza, 468 U.S. 1032, 1050-51 (1984). Moreover, the respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance of counsel; nor has he complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
We also find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision declining to grant the respondent’s request for a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). As the Immigration Judge noted, since initiated in September 2012, the respondent’s removal proceedings have been continued on multiple occasions. Further, as the Immigration Judge noted, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) indicated that it declines to exercise prosecutorial discretion in this case.
 
Additionally, as an alien who has not been admitted to the United States, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). In addition, the respondent did not submit an application for adjustment to the Immigration Judge and has submitted no evidence on appeal reflecting that he has an application for adjustment pending before the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS), or that he is eligible for any other form of relief from removal. Thus, a continuance to afford the respondent an opportunity to pursue adjustment of status or any other form of relief is and was not warranted.
 
*2 Finally, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent a 60-day voluntary departure period, conditioned upon the posting of a voluntary departure bond in the amount of $500.00 to the Department of Homeland Security within five business days from the date of the order. The record reflects that the respondent submitted timely proof of having paid the voluntary departure bond. Accordingly, the period of voluntary departure will be reinstated.
 
In sum, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s resolution of the respondent’s claim. The respondent on appeal raises no other claims to relief. Accordingly, we will enter the following orders.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(f).
 
*3 Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2015 WL 5996725 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JORGE LUIS SALCEDO
File: AXXX XX3 198 - Hartford, CT
September 21, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas S. Rome, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leigh Mapplebeck
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Peru, who is subject to an order of removal entered on February 19, 2015, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated June 11, 2015, denying his late-filed motion to reopen. The respondent has also filed a motion for stay of removal. The respondent’s appeal fee waiver request is granted. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed, and the motion will be denied as moot.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. We have considered the totality of the respondent’s claims on appeal, including his claim that he wishes to challenge his removability and to be granted time to pursue collateral relief for a criminal conviction, and conclude that no basis was established for granting the motion.
 
As pointed out by the Immigration Judge, the respondent’s motion is untimely (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Nonetheless, the motion may be considered a motion for sua sponte reopening. Sua sponte authority is an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
A main basis on which reopening was sought is alleged ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of the respondent’s previous attorney. However, the general procedural requirements for presenting an ineffective assistance of counsel claim have not been complied with, and no argument has been made that the procedural requirements should be waived. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec.637 (BIA 1988) aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Furthermore, the respondent does not challenge on appeal the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen insofar as that denial rejected an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. I.J. at 1 (June 11, 2015). Therefore, we need not address the issue further.
 
The Immigration Judge issued an oral decision on February 19, 2015. No appeal was taken from that decision, and the decision became final on March 23, 2015.1 Significantly, the respondent does not argue that he attempted to file an appeal, so no procedural issues involved with misfiled or lost appeal forms are before us.
 
While the respondent’s appeal brief mentions legal arguments relating to the question of removability, that question has been decided in a final order, and, except in rare cases, we do not grant a sua sponte motion based on an argument that could have been made on direct appeal. See generally Matter of J-J-, supra. The respondent’s expressed disagreement with the Immigration Judge’s legal conclusions, and his expressed dissatisfaction with the length of the hearing, are in the nature of an attempt to revive an appeal. The respondent has made no persuasive claim that there was, in this case, a miscarriage of justice that might support a finding that extraordinary circumstances exist for a sua sponte grant of reopening. See, e.g. Matter of Farina, 12 I&N Dec. 467, 471-72 (BIA 1967); Matter of Malone, 11 I&N Dec. 730, 731-323 (BIA 1966). We acknowledge, specifically, that the respondent disagrees with the Immigration Judge’s treatment of the statute under which he was convicted as being a categorical match for an aggravated felony.2 Without expressing an opinion as to the merits of this specific issue, we note that the respondent has not cited to any authority that compels the result he seeks, and his disagreement with the Immigration Judge on this matter of law does not constitute a basis for sua sponte reopening. We also acknowledge the respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge did not provide a full and fair hearing, but the respondent has not supported that assertion with specific and persuasive arguments, and upon review of the record, we find no basis for concluding that a full and fair hearing was denied.
 
*2 The respondent also mentioned the potential of collateral relief from his criminal conviction. However, as the respondent has presented no evidence that he has sought such relief, we need not address that issue further.
 
Upon consideration of the totality of the record, we agree ultimately with the Immigration Judge that reopening is not warranted. Accordingly, as we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record contains a memorandum of the court’s Decision and Orders, which indicates that appeal would be due by March 23, 2015. While the decision was recorded, no transcript was made of the decision, as no direct appeal from the decision was filed. A recording, which the Board has reviewed, exists of the hearing at which the oral decision was read; we take administrative notice that the recording is consistent with the Immigration Judge’s description of the decision. I.J. at 1 (June 11, 2015). We note that the respondent has not indicated that he or his counsel have been prevented from reviewing that recording.

2

The Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion indicates that the Immigration Judge had found that statutory provision to be a categorical match for an aggravated felony crime of violence.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE GRANADOS-ESPINOZA
File: AXXX XX6 146 - Las Vegas, NV
September 21, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Adriana Pereyra, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Arthur A. Ayo-Aghimien II
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1157(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated July 1, 2015, ordering his removal from the United States to Jamaica. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a motion for summary affirmance. We will dismiss the appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. As the Immigration Judge pointed out, the respondent did not identify any form of relief for which he is eligible in these proceedings. The respondent raised no challenge to the finding of removability under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii).
 
The record reflects that the respondent was convicted of one or more criminal offenses in the United States, and he asserts that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in his criminal proceedings, but he has not articulated a basis on which this assertion supports overturning the Immigration Judge’s decision at this time. We acknowledge that the respondent has filed a motion in a Nevada state court to withdraw his guilty plea and vacate judgment with respect to the conviction that forms the basis for the aggravated felony ground of removal in this case. While the respondent is free to pursue a collateral attack in the proper court, the mere fact that he is doing so does not justify further delay in these proceedings. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323 (BIA 1996). An alien may not collaterally attack the legitimacy of an otherwise valid federal or state criminal conviction in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Ponce de Leon-Ruiz, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (BIA 1996, 1997; A.G. 1997). Though the respondent asserts he has a persuasive basis for filing a post-conviction motion or motions, this does not render a conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra. Under the circumstances of this case, good cause for a continuance has not been shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6; Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that an alien should not be granted a continuance “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”).
 
*2 As the respondent’s arguments on appeal do not persuade this Board to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to order his removal from the United States to Mexico, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSEFA CARRILLO-PABLO MELINDA MARIBEL CALMO-CARRILLO
Files: AXXX XX7 908 - Seattle, WA
AXXX XX7 909
September 21, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Gautam Jagannath, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Eric Bakken
Senior Attorney
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document (both respondents) (sustained)
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondents, natives and citizens of Guatemala, were ordered removed in absentia on November 21, 2014.1 On February 20, 2015, the respondents filed a motion to reopen proceedings, which the Immigration Judge denied on April 8, 2015. The respondents filed a timely appeal of that decision. The record will be remanded.
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge, including the determination of credibility, for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including questions of judgment, discretion, and law, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear (Form I-862) was personally served on the respondents on October 7, 2014. On November 5, 2014, a notice of hearing was mailed to the respondent at the address contained on the Notice to Appear, informing her that she was scheduled for a hearing on November 21, 2014. The record does not contain any evidence that the notice of hearing was returned to the Immigration Court. The Immigration Judge ordered the respondents’ removal on November 21, 2014, when they failed to appear for their scheduled hearing. The respondents filed a motion to reopen on February 20, 2015. The Immigration Judge denied the respondents’ motion and the respondents filed a timely appeal.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondents did not establish that rescission of the in absentia order of removal and reopening of these proceedings is warranted based on exceptional circumstances (I.J. at 3-5). See sections 240(b)(5)(A), (C), (e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(A), (C), (e)(1); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondents’ association with a notario does not constitute exceptional circumstances as contemplated under section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
The adult respondent’s declaration is ambiguous as to the degree to which, if any, the respondent relied upon any inaccurate representations made to her by the notario with respect to attending the November 21, 2014, master calendar hearing. The respondent does not detail any particular false or misleading statement made to her by the notario with respect to her attendance at the hearing. She notes only that “Manuel did not tell us that I had to go to court. He did not say anything about that” (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Exh. B at 1). The respondent does not dispute that she received a Notice to Appear that includes a warning indicating that she may be ordered removed if she does not attend a hearing. Thus, the respondent was aware of the consequences of failing to appear, even assuming that the notario did not affirmatively advise her in this regard. Moreover, the respondent’s declaration acknowledges that the notario told her that he was not an attorney. See Singh-Bhathal v. INS, 170 F.3d 943, 946-47 (9th Cir. 1999) (holding that reliance on mistaken advice of non-attorney immigration consultant was insufficient to demonstrate exceptional circumstances warranting reopening of in absentia deportation order). Thus, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not establish that exceptional circumstances warrant reopening these proceedings. We also affirm the Immigration Judge’s ruling that because the respondent knowingly retained a non-attorney, she could not establish a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Hernandez v. Mukasey, 524 F.3d 1014 (9th Cir. 2008) (aliens’ reliance on deficient advice of immigration consultant who they knew was not an attorney could not support ineffective assistance claim).
 
*2 However, we conclude that remand is warranted for additional consideration of whether the respondent established that she did not receive notice of the November 21, 2014, hearing. The Immigration Judge correctly applied the presumption that the properly addressed notice sent via regular mail was received by the respondent. Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 671-72 (BIA 2008). However, in assessing whether the respondent overcame this presumption, the Immigration Judge did not consider all the factors we set forth in Matter of M-R-A-, supra. See also Sembiring v. Gonzales, 499 F.3d 981, 986 (9th Cir. 2007). Instead, the Immigration Judge only considered the respondent’s declaration, which the Immigration Judge concluded was insufficient to meet the respondent’s burden of proof.2
 
In light of our conclusion that the Immigration Judge did not appropriately consider the full range of the factors we set forth in Matter of M-R-A-, supra, we conclude that remand is warranted. On remand, the Immigration Judge should consider whether due diligence was exercised in seeking to redress the situation, any prior applications for relief that would indicate an incentive to appear, the respondent’s prior appearance at immigration proceedings, if applicable, and any other factors that may be relevant in determining whether the respondent met her burden of proof to overcome the presumption that she received notice of the hearing. Id. at 674-75. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the issuance of a new decision.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Board Member Roger A. Pauley respectfully dissents and would affirm the decision below that the respondent failed to establish a lack of notice or exceptional circumstances warranting reopening. See Joshi v. Ashcroft, 389 F.3d 732, 735 (7th Cir. 2004) (“a bare, uncorroborated, self-serving denial of receipt, even if sworn, is weak evidence”). Furthermore, the majority’s finding that the Immigration Judge failed to consider other relevant factors, despite acknowledging that he enumerated them in his decision, is unfounded.

2

We acknowledge the Immigration Judge articulated the factors. However, the decision does not reflect that the factors were actually considered.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HECTOR ALFONSO ORDONEZ-MARROQUIN
File: AXXX XX7 716 - Harlingen, TX
September 17, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case is before the Board pursuant to a July 28, 2015, order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, which granted the motion to remand this matter to the Board to consider what effect, if any, the Supreme Court’s intervening decision in Mata v. Lynch, 135 S. Ct. 2150 (2015), has on its treatment of the respondent’s motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel as a request for sua sponte reopening.
 
It is not clear that the decision in Mata v. Lynch has any direct applicability to our treatment of the respondent’s motion to reopen, since its holding dealt solely with whether the Fifth Circuit properly found it lacked jurisdiction to review the Board’s denial of a motion to reopen. Nevertheless, to the extent that the Supreme Court indicated in Mata that it might be improper to treat untimely or number-barred motions solely as requests to reopen sua sponte, we will reexamine the respondent’s claims on remand, as argued both in the Motion to Remand filed while his appeal was pending before the Board and as argued in a Motion to Reopen he filed on January 14, 2015.
 
In both motions, the respondent urged that the attorney who filed his first motion to reopen filed a patently deficient motion to reopen based on a claim of lack of notice, which led to its denial by the Immigration Judge and prejudiced him. In particular, he contends that counsel did not properly explain the basis of the motion and did not submit evidence in support of the motion that was adequate to establish the respondent’s claim that he did not receive notice of the scheduled hearing below that resulted in an in absentia order of removal being entered by the Immigration Judge on February 1, 2002. Although there is no time limit on motions to reopen claiming a lack of notice, such motions are not specifically exempted from the number limit on motions to reopen. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(iii).
 
To the extent that equitable tolling of the time and/or number limits may be found to apply to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, we have found that prejudice must be shown. See, e.g., Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent urges that he was prejudiced by the deficiencies in the first motion, and that his motion would have been granted if the proper arguments were set forth in the motion and if additional evidence had been submitted to support his claim that he did not receive notice of his hearing. Upon review of the all of the evidence presented, including the respondent’s updated affidavit of non-receipt, the cousin’s affidavit of non-receipt, and evidence showing the respondent’s potential eligibility for relief at the time he was placed in proceedings in 2001 (Motion to Reopen filed Jan. 14, 2015, tabs B, C, H, K), it appears likely that the outcome would have been different if this evidence had been presented at the time the initial motion was filed. In other words, it appears likely that if this evidence had been initially presented, the motion would have been granted based upon a consideration of the factors set forth in Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674 (BIA 2008), for determining whether the movant has overcome the lesser presumption of delivery that applies when a notice is sent by regular mail. Under the circumstances, we find that the respondent has shown he was prejudiced such that equitable tolling would be warranted. Moreover, he has presented sufficient evidence that he did not receive notice of the scheduled hearing such that reopening and rescission of the in absentia order is warranted. Matter of M-R-A-, supra.1
 
*2 Accordingly, upon remand from the Fifth Circuit, we will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen these removal proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order entered on February 1, 2002, is rescinded.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing and for the entry of a new decision.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Even if the Fifth Circuit would not apply equitable tolling, we would find, considering the totality of the evidence presented by the respondent in this case, that an exceptional situation exists warranting sua sponte reopening in our discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHAHIN EMAMIAN RASEKH
File: AXXX XX6 382 - Oklahoma City, OK
September 16, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paola M. Alvarez de Bennett, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Iran, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 7, 2014, denying his motion to reopen. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Court as discussed below.
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge, including the questions of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, submitted on May 1, 2014, finding that the motion alleges the same grounds of ineffective assistance of counsel as asserted in the respondent’s first motion to reopen filed on or about February 27, 2014. The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent’s motion was number-barred because it was a second motion to reopen filed in this case. Finally, the Immigration Judge declined to sua sponte reopen the respondent’s proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
In the motion to reopen, the respondent argued that the motion to reopen time and number limitations should be equitably tolled in his case (May 7, 2014, I.J. Motion, at 12-14). However, the Immigration Judge’s decision did not address these arguments. The respondent again argues on appeal that the motion to reopen time and number limitations should be equitably tolled and that he has diligently pursued his claims (Resp. App. Brief, at 7-12). Furthermore, while the respondent’s second motion to reopen repeats essentially the same ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his first counsel, it also included evidence of the respondent’s compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), as well as an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his second counsel in failing to make an adequate ineffective assistance of counsel claim in the respondent’s first motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge’s decision did not sufficiently address these arguments. Therefore, before we can perform a meaningful appellate review, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to address the respondent’s arguments presented in the motion, including his arguments regarding equitable tolling of the motion to reopen time and number limitations in light of applicable legal principles. See Mahamat v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 1281, 1283 (10th Cir. 2005); Galvez Piñeda v. Gonzales, 127 F.3d 833, 838-39 (10th Cir. 2005); Infanzon v. Ashcroft, 386 F.3d 1359, 1362-63 (10th Cir. 2004); Riley v. INS, 310 F.3d 1253, 1257-58 (10th Cir. 2002). Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with this order, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 5996707 (BIA)

2015 WL 5935616 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOZAMMAL HOQUE
File: AXXX XX0 593 - Harlingen, TX
September 8, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Salim Sheikh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark Whitworth
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s August 26, 2014, decision denying his motion to reopen. The respondent was previously ordered removed in absentia on May 31, 2011. The Department of Homeland Security has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
An in absentia removal order may be rescinded only if the alien files a motion to reopen: (1) within 180 days after the date of the removal order in which he establishes that his failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances; or (2) at any time in which he establishes that he did not have proper notice of the hearing in which the removal order was entered. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); see also section 240(e)(1) of the Act (defining the term “““exceptional circumstances”).
 
The respondent asserts that reopening is warranted as he did not receive notice of his May 31, 2011, hearing that resulted in his removal being ordered in absentia. However, as the Immigration Judge properly found, the notice for the hearing, which was mailed on October 20, 2010, was sent to and received by the respondent’s attorney of record at the time (I.J. at 1-2). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent received proper notice inasmuch as written notice of the hearing was provided to his attorney (I.J. at 1-2). See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; see also Men Keng Chang v. Jiugni, 669 F.2d 275, 277-78 (5th Cir. 1982) (finding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (same); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (providing that whenever a person is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney or representative of record, or to the person himself if unrepresented). Thus, the Immigration Judge determined that reopening was not warranted. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act.
 
The respondent also asserts that his prior attorney did not notify him of the time and place of his hearing, and the attorney did not file a change of venue request. The Immigration Judge properly construed this as a request for reopening under section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act on the basis of exceptional circumstances, and we agree with the Immigration Judge that the request is untimely as the underlying motion was filed more than 3 years after the date of the entry of the in absentia order (I.J. at 2). See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 602-03 (BIA 1996); see also section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act (requiring that a respondent file a motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances within 180 days from the date of the in absentia removal order).
 
*2 Motions to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel claims must be timely filed or be subject to equitable tolling. Although the respondent asserts that there is no time limitation for a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance, the Fifth Circuit Court of Appeals, in whose jurisdiction this case arises, has not determined if equitable tolling of statutory deadlines for ineffective assistance of counsel applies in immigration cases.1 In addition to the untimeliness of the motion, the respondent has not established what efforts he took to determine the status of his removal proceedings for the 3 years following his order of removal. Cf. Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 954 (5th Cir. 2012) (finding that in cases filed before 1992, a motion to reopen could not be denied based on due diligence). We therefore agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established that reopening is warranted under section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
We are unpersuaded by the respondent’s assertion that he suffered a loss of his due process rights with not being afforded an opportunity to obtain counsel (Resp.’s Br. at 9-10). The respondent secured counsel and was represented at the time the hearing notice was mailed on October 10, 2010. Moreover, the respondent’s counsel appeared on his behalf at the May 31, 2011, hearing. While the record demonstrates that the attorney did not include the respondent’s entire address on the written communication, the respondent has not demonstrated that he followed up with his attorney regarding his removal proceedings after he procured representation or explained why he was unable to be reached at the telephone number given to his attorney. As the attorney appeared on the respondent’s behalf at the hearing on May 31, 2011, we are unpersuaded that the respondent’s due process rights were not protected.
 
The respondent further asserts on appeal that he is eligible for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture. The time and number limits for motions to reopen do not apply where an alien seeks to apply for asylum or withholding of removal on the basis of changed circumstances or country conditions arising in the country of nationality, if such evidence is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(ii), 1003.23(b)(4)(i); see also Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013) (holding that proceedings conducted in absentia may be reopened where the alien seeks asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions). The respondent states that he is afraid to return to Bangladesh. However, the respondent does not allege or discuss any changed country conditions to support his claim, and he has not presented any persuasive evidence of a change in country conditions which is material to his eligibility for asylum (I.J. at 3).
 
*3 Finally, we find no basis for exercising our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Fifth Circuit Court of Appeals has stated that an alien’s request for equitable tolling on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is construed as an invitation for the Board to exercise its discretion to reopen proceedings sua sponte. See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 220 (5th Cir. 2008), abrogated by Mata v. Lynch, 135 S. Ct. 2150, 2155-56 (2015).

2015 WL 5944127 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOFA SOIFUA LUAU A.K.A. TOPA SOIFUA LUAU
File: AXXX XX8 123 - Salt Lake City, UT
September 3, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Peter Duong, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Tonga, was ordered removed in absentia by an Immigration Judge on February 19, 2008. He filed a motion to reopen on March 18, 2014, which was denied by the Immigration Judge on April 3, 2014, and the respondent timely appeals. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not replied to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
It is undisputed that the respondent had notice of his hearing. The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that the respondent failed to establish “exceptional circumstances” prevented his appearance. The fact that the respondent was afraid that he might be ordered removed if he appeared for his hearing did not constitute exceptional circumstances beyond his control that would excuse his failure to appear. See section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1); Tang v. Ashcroft, 354 F.3d 1192, 1194 (10th Cir. 2003) (holding alien has “the burden of establishing exceptional circumstances warranting rescission” of the removal order).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that he was not advised of the consequences of failing to appear at his hearing. As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent was represented by counsel, who waived the reading of the advisement of the respondent’s requirement to appear (I.J. at 2). To the extent the respondent maintains that his counsel failed to properly advise him, he has not complied with the requirements to state a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Tang v. Ashcroft, 354 F.3d 1192, 1196-97 (10th Cir. 2003) (no abuse of discretion in the Board’s denial of a motion to reopen where the alien alleged ineffective assistance of counsel but failed to comply with any of the Lozada requirements).
 
*2 The record also reveals that the Notice to Appear (NTA) was personally served on the respondent and the NTA lists, inter alia, his address obligations and failure to appear warnings in English (Exh. 1). There is no statutory or regulatory requirement that the address advisals and failure to appear warnings in the NTA be provided orally or in the respondent’s native language. Current law does not require that the NTA be in any language other than English. See section 239 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229. See also generally Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354, 359-60 (5th Cir. 2009); Lopes v. Gonzales, 468 F.3d 81, 84-5 (2d Cir. 2006) (“We reject Silva’s claim that the notices to appear were defective because they did not advise him in his native Portuguese that an in absentia order could be entered against him if he failed to appeal.”); Flores-Chavez v. Ashcroft, 362 F.3d 1150, 1155 n. 4 (9th Cir. 2004); Nazarova v. INS, 171 F.3d 478, 483 (7th Cir. 1999).
 
Moreover, we note that a motion to reopen alleging exceptional circumstances for a failure to appear at a removal hearing must be filed within 180 days of the order of removal, and only one such motion is allowed. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i) & 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent’s motion, filed 6 years after entry of the in absentia removal order, claiming that he failed to appear at the hearing due to exceptional circumstances was untimely, as it was filed well beyond 180 days after the removal order was entered (I.J. at 3).
 
The respondent also proffers evidence that a visa petition has been filed on his behalf. In Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998), the Board held that an alien may reopen for purposes of pursuing previously unavailable relief in circumstances where he was not given oral warnings in his native language of the consequences of failing to appear at a deportation hearing and where the motion complies with the general time limits governing motions to reopen. The motion is untimely filed, however, and thus reopening on this basis is not warranted.
 
We deny the respondent’s request to have transcripts prepared. See Board of Immigration Appeals Practice Manual, 4.2(f)(ii), at 47, available at http://www.justice.gov/eoir/vll/qapracmanual/BIAPracticeManual.pdf#page=55 (providing that transcripts are not normally prepared for appeals of denials of motions to reopen, including motions to reopen in absentia proceedings). As noted above, the respondent, through service of the NTA at the very least, was properly advised of the consequences of failing to appear, and thus transcripts are not warranted here.
 
*3 For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 5944127 (BIA)
End of Document

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANGEL PARIS-MENDEZ
File: AXXX XX0 737 - Las Vegas, NV
September 3, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David E. Walters, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patrick W. Lindemann
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 16, 2013, finding him removable as charged and granting him voluntary departure. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States because he is an alien who is present in this country without being admitted or paroled or who arrived at any time or place other than as designated by the Attorney General (I.J. at 2). See section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i). As the respondent is subject to removal from the United States, it is his burden to establish eligibility for relief from removal. See section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d).
 
The respondent has not established that, in light of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, these proceedings should be remanded to the Immigration Judge. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Magallanes-Damian v. MS, 783 F.2d 931 (9th Cir. 1986). In particular, the respondent has not established that his former counsel’s performance was so deficient that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. While the respondent argues that his former counsel should have pursued a claim to immigration benefits under the Department of Homeland Security’s Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (“DACA”) program, neither the Immigration Judge, nor this Board can grant the respondent DACA benefits. See generally Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-350 (BIA 1982). Instead, if the respondent wishes to pursue DACA benefits, such a request must be filed with United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”). Moreover, the respondent has not established that he is prima facie eligible for DACA.1 Furthermore, we note that aliens who are subject to final orders of removal are eligible to request DACA benefits. Thus, the respondent has not established that his former counsel has divested him of the ability to pursue DACA benefits.
 
*2 Considering these circumstances, we conclude the respondent has not shown, in the absence of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, that a remand of these proceedings would be appropriate based upon his not having established prima facie eligibility for DACA.
 
Lastly, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent a 60-day voluntary departure period, conditioned upon the posting of a $500 bond. Effective January 20, 2009, pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3)(ii), an alien granted voluntary departure shall, within 30 days of filing an appeal with the Board, submit sufficient proof that the required voluntary departure bond was posted with the Department of Homeland Security. If the alien does not provide timely proof to the Board, the Board will not reinstate the period of voluntary departure in its final order. Id. The record before the Board does not indicate that the respondent submitted timely proof of having paid the voluntary departure bond. Therefore, the voluntary departure period will not be reinstated, and the respondent shall be removed from the United States on the charge contained in the Notice to Appear, pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s alternate order. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent is ordered removed from the United States pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s alternate order.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) will grant Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (DACA) to an alien if he: (1) was under age 31 as of June 15, 2012 (i.e., was born before June 16, 1981); (2) came to the United States when he was under age 16; (3) has continuously resided in the United States from June 15, 2007, up to the present time; (4) was physically present in the United States on June 15, 2012, and at the time of making his request for consideration of deferred action with USCIS; (5) entered without inspection before June 15, 2012, or his lawful immigration status expired as of June 15, 2012; (6) is currently in school, has graduated or obtained a certificate of completion from high school, has obtained a general education development (GED) certificate, or is an honorably discharged veteran of the Coast Guard or Armed Forces of the United States; and (7) has not been convicted of a felony, significant misdemeanor, three or more other misdemeanors, and does not otherwise pose a threat to national security or public safety.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALIF FOFANA A.K.A. SALIF K FOFANA A.K.A. DUKE MENSAH A.K.A. SALIE FOFANA A.K.A. MOHAMMED FOFANA
File: AXXX XX8 182 - New York, NY
August 27, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Barry Ross Goldberg, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Administrative closure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mali, has timely filed an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 23, 2015, finding the respondent removable as charged under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i), and, in the absence of any applications for relief from removal, ordering the respondent removed. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings and for the entry of a new decision.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that removal proceedings should have been administratively closed for the purpose of allowing the respondent to pursue his application for immigration benefits under the Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (“DACA”) program. The Immigration Judge correctly stated that the decision to confer DACA benefits upon an alien as a matter of prosecutorial discretion lies with the exclusive jurisdiction of the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) and is not subject to review by an Immigration Judge or this Board. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 110 (BIA 2009); Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-50 (BIA 1982) (stating that deferred action status is a function of DHS’s prosecutorial discretion). We further observe that DACA may be granted regardless of the outcome of the respondent’s removal proceedings. See USCIA, Frequently Asked Questions, Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals, Q4, www.dhs.gov/deferred-action-childhood-arrivals.
 
The respondent also asserts on appeal that his offenses do not constitute crimes involving moral turpitude, and that the Immigration Judge should have found that the “petty offense” exception applied. The Immigration Judge specifically did not reach the issue of the respondent’s removability under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), observing that is undisputed that the respondent is removable under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act. However, we will remand this matter to the Immigration Judge to consider whether the issue of whether the respondent has committed a crime involving moral turpitude is relevant to his DACA application or any other forms of relief the respondent may seek on remand, and, if so, should rule on this issue.
 
*2 In addition, we note the respondent’s appellate assertion that “the fact that counsel for [the respondent] did not file an I-589 application for withholding of removal in light of country conditions in Mali raises substantial questions regarding ineffective assistance of counsel.” We do not need to resolve the issue whether the respondent’s prior counsel was ineffective as, on remand, the respondent will be able to apply for any forms of relief for which he may be eligible.
 
While the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge’s denial of bond was in error, we note that bond and removal proceedings are separate and apart. The Board does not have the authority to review the respondent’s custody status in these proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.19(d); Matter of P-C-M-, 20 I&N Dec. 432 (BIA 1991); Matter of Balderas, 20 I&N Dec. 389 (BIA 1991).
 
On remand, the parties should be afforded an opportunity to submit further evidence and argument, and the respondent should be permitted to submit applications for any forms of relief for which he may be eligible.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Patricia A. Cole

IN RE: ELIZABETH SILVERO-OVIEDO

August 24, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ronald S. Salomon, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on October 26, 2009, when we upheld an Immigration Judge’s order denying a motion to reopen proceedings that were conducted in absentia on October 14, 1998. The respondent has now filed another motion to reopen these removal proceedings. The motion, to which the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded, will be denied.
 
In seeking rescission and reopening of the in absentia removal order, the respondent reiterates a claim, previously raised and rejected by both the Immigration Judge and the Board, that the government did not provide her with proper notice of her October 14, 1998, removal hearing. She also appears, for the first time, to fault Jesus Pena, the attorney who assisted her in pursuing a visa petition through her United States citizen spouse after her removal hearing, for her failure to appear at the hearing. The respondent also seeks reopening of these proceedings to apply for adjustment of status based on the now-approved visa petition filed by her United States citizen spouse.
 
We find no basis to rescind the in absentia order and reopen these removal proceedings. First, we have already considered, and rejected, the respondent’s claim that the government did not provide her with adequate notice of her hearing (Board Order dated October 26, 2009). We decline to revisit that issue now.
 
Second, to the extent that the instant motion is based on any alleged ineffective assistance by Pena, it is untimely filed years beyond the general 180-day deadline for seeking rescission of an in absentia removal order based on exceptional circumstances. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i), 1229a(e)(1); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). We further note that the respondent has failed to comply with the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 954 (5th Cir. 2012) (acknowledging the Court’s rejection of a flexible approach to the Lozada requirements).
 
Finally, to the extent that the respondent now seeks reopening of these removal proceedings to pursue new relief in the form of adjustment of status, independently of her in absentia claims, the instant motion is untimely and has not been shown to fall within any of the exceptions for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2) & 1003.23(b)(1) (a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order); Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349, 356-57 (BIA 1998). We also do not find exceptional circumstances that would warrant reopening these proceedings pursuant to our discretionary sua sponte authority under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).1
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes

2015 WL 5244404 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOAO BATISTA RIBEIRO
File: AXXX XX9 835 - Philadelphia, Pennsylvania
August 21, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Katelyn M. Hufe, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles Ireland
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The proceedings in this matter were last before the Board on February 20, 2015. By a decision of that date, the Board dismissed the appeal of the decision of the Immigration Judge of June 26, 2013, which found the respondent removable as charged and denied his motion for a continuance. By the February 20, 2015, decision, the Board also denied a motion to remand filed by the respondent. The motion sought a remand on the basis that evidence obtained as a result of his arrest by United States Immigration and Customs Enforcement (“ICE”) should be suppressed in light of Oliva-Ramos v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 694 F.3d 259 (3d Cir. 2012).
 
By the motion now before the Board, the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The basis for the ineffective assistance of counsel claim is that former counsel failed to discuss with the respondent the circumstances of his arrest and failed to prepare a motion to suppress the evidence stemming from that arrest which the respondent speculates would have led to the termination of proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security has opposed the motion.
 
To make out an ineffective-assistance-of-counsel claim, an alien must show that prior counsel’s deficient performance prevented him from reasonably presenting his case and caused him “substantial prejudice.” Fadiga v. Att’y Gen., 488 F.3d 142 at 155 (3d Cir. 2007); Khan v. Att’y Gen., 448 F.3d 226, 236 (3d Cir. 2006); Uspango v. Ashcroft, 289 F.3d 226, 231 (3d Cir. 2002); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003). To evaluate the merits of an ineffectiveness claim, we apply the familiar two-part-error-and-prejudice test. Fadiga, 488 F.3d at 157. First, we ask whether “competent counsel would have acted otherwise.” Id. (quoting Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 129 (2nd Cir. 2000)). If so, we ask whether counsel’s poor performance prejudiced the alien. Id. To prove prejudice, an alien must show that there is a “reasonable likelihood” that the result of the removal proceedings would have been different had the error(s) not occurred. Id. at 159. This standard “properly requires the alien to show not just that he received ineffective assistance in his removal proceedings, but that the challenged order of removal is fundamentally unfair because there is a significant likelihood that the IJ would not have entered an order of removal absent counsel’s errors.” Id.
 
*2 In addition to these substantive requirements, the Board has three procedural requirements for motions to reopen based on ineffectiveness claims as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988): (1) the motion must be supported by an affidavit detailing counsel’s failings; (2) counsel must be informed of the allegations; and (3) the motion must show that disciplinary charges have been filed with the appropriate authority, or if not, adequately explain why not. In this case, the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. At issue, then, is whether or not the respondent suffered the requisite prejudice.
 
It is the position of the respondent that prior counsel was ineffective insofar as he did not discuss the circumstances of his arrest with the respondent and that he failed to submit a motion to suppress to disallow reliance upon information that was gleaned from what the respondent has characterized as an unlawful and racially motivated arrest. The respondent contends that had a motion to suppress been submitted, his removal proceedings would have been terminated upon the granting of the motion. However, it is to be noted that the respondent was found removable based upon pleadings rather than the information contained in the product of his arrest, the Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien (Form I-213). (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 71). While we agree that counsel may have been ineffective in not submitting a motion to suppress, as the Immigration Judge afforded him the opportunity to do so, the respondent has failed to establish “substantial prejudice.” The respondent conceded removability, has not since claimed lawful presence in the United States, and has not submitted an application for relief. As the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for any relief, the alleged ineffective assistance has not been shown to have potentially affected the outcome of the respondent’s case. Thus, the respondent has not established a basis to remand his case on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 5244404 (BIA)
End of Document

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:

APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent has submitted a timely motion to reconsider the Board’s April 30, 2015, decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings in which the Board dismissed his appeal on December 18, 2014. The motion is denied.
 
In denying the respondent’s motion, the Board found that he had not demonstrated compliance with the procedural requirements for reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). With his motion the respondent has submitted various documents, including a supplemental submission indicating that he filed a “preliminary inquiry” with the Committee on Professional Standards on June 17, 2013. This submission is in the nature of a motion to reopen, which is both time and number barred. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Moreover, we observe that this document confirms that neither of the two other such forms that the respondent filed with his prior motion to reopen were submitted to the noted committee, as he had alleged. As such, while we find no error in that regard, we do acknowledge that he has provided evidence that he filed a complaint as required by Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
However, we find no error in our determination that the respondent has not provided evidence that he informed his former counsel both of the complaint filed against her and of the contents of the complaint, and provided her an opportunity to respond, as required. Id.; Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644 (5th Cir. 2014). Contrary to the respondent’s suggestions otherwise, we are not required to accept as true his unsupported factual allegations. The respondent’s reliance on case law from a circuit outside the one in which this case arises for this proposition is unavailing (Motion at 4-5). Rather, we apply the law of the circuit in cases arising in that jurisdiction, and are not bound by a decision of a court of appeals in a different circuit. Matter of Salazar, 23 I&N Dec. 223, 235 (BIA 2002). As we find no error in our determination that the respondent did not satisfy the requirements for reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, reconsideration is not warranted. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
*2 Neil P. Miller
